# CONSERVATION OF STORMS

*a novel*

**Josh Storm**

---

*In a world where weather can be legally bought and reassigned, a small-town rain broker is asked to arrange the one transfer that would save her dying valley — by dooming the valley next door, where her estranged sister lives.*

---

# Chapter 1 — Form 9-C: Interbasin Reassignment (Application)

> *No transfer shall be valid absent the broker's seal; and the seal, once affixed, shall be presumed to have been affixed with knowledge of the transfer's effect.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §2(a)

The dust came into Ainsley Hale the way it came every morning now, off the brown pivot-circles east of town, a fine gray flour that got into everything a person hadn't sealed against it — the butter dish, the wash, the teeth, the space between the pages of a shut book. By the time Della Okafor-Prine turned the key in the frosted-glass door that read COUNTY RAIN BROKER · HALE, it had already settled a film on the sill inside, though the window had been closed all night, because that was what the county did while you slept. It filed itself down. Above the grain elevator that held no grain the sky was the color it had worn for six years — a flat, spent, apologetic blue, the blue of a thing that had done something and could not take it back and had stopped, long ago, pretending otherwise.

She did not look at it long. You learned not to. You learned to look at the counter.

The office was cool and smelled of paper and the machine-oil she used on the safe hinge. Junie was already at the ledger, bent over the wide gray book with the good pen, copying yesterday's transfers into the column in a hand that was still too careful, too round, the hand of a person who believed the record mattered. She looked up when the bell went.

"Feed's already talking," Junie said. "Something small came in overnight. Sixty-one to twenty-two."

"Read it out."

"Point-four acre-inches. Section eleven to section twenty-two, both Hollis parcels. He's moving his own rain off the fallow ground onto the corn." Junie frowned at the screen. "Can he do that?"

"He owns both. It offsets inside the parcel." Della hung her coat. She could feel the sobriety chip through the fabric where it hung on the hook, a small round weight in the right pocket, and she did not touch it, which was its own kind of touching. "Pull the deed. Confirm the allotment carries. If it balances, we file it."

It balanced. It nearly always balanced; that was the whole beauty of the thing, the reason men who golfed had built it. Della watched the number offset itself against the number, the plus and the minus meeting at the third decimal, conservation holding, the sky's arithmetic clean and closed. Point-four inches would leave the Hollis fallow and go to the Hollis corn, and in June a strip of ground that would have greened up gray, and the corn would drink it, and the whole exchange would touch no one but Hollis, who had signed for both sides of himself. A rare mercy in this office. A transfer where the man robbing you was you.

She reached into the safe and took out the stamp.

It was brass, heavy in the hand as a small tool meant for a different, older kind of work, the county seal cut into its face and worn shallow at the rim from her father's forty years and her own twenty-two. She set the form on the counter, inked the pad, and brought the stamp down.

The *thunk* of it was the most honest sound the office made. She felt it go up her wrist the way she always had, a small satisfying jar, competence with a weight to it, and under the satisfaction the older thing she never named and never quite got rid of — the animal part of her that thought weather was God's and not a lot number, that flinched a little every time her hand said *legal* over a piece of the sky. Eleven hundred stamps and it still flinched. She had come to think of the flinch as proof she was still awake.

"Filed," she said. "Log it and mark the timestamp."

Junie logged it. Della put the stamp back in the safe — she put it back after every filing, a habit her father had drilled into her at this same counter when her hands were smaller than the brass, *you do not leave the seal where a want can reach it* — and turned the safe dial and heard the tumblers drop.

The bell over the door went before she'd straightened up.

It was the Webber girl, Dana, twenty-two if that, with a baby on her hip and a paper in her hand and the look of a person who had rehearsed a sentence in the truck and lost it at the door. Her people ran cattle south of the highway on ground that had gone from marginal to dead in the six years the sky had been apologizing. Della had watched three Webber generations thin out at this counter.

"My granddad's allotment," Dana said. "The March-through-May on the south section. He wants to sell it. Not lease. Sell." She put the paper down like it weighed something. "He needs the money for the — he's got a payment on the new truck he can't make and the bank's calling it."

Della read the deed reference. The allotment was old and clean and severable, an inch and a quarter of spring rain that had fallen on Webber ground since before the Act, since before it was a thing you could weigh and cart off. She could sell it in a morning. Meridian's standing order would take it inside the hour at ten over ask, and the Webber south section would go the color of everything else, and there would be a truck.

"You understand it's forever," Della said. "A sale's not a lease. It severs from the deed and it doesn't revert — not if the land dries, not if your granddad dies, not if you inherit it and want it back. The rain leaves that ground and it doesn't come home. Your kids won't have it to sell. There won't be anything on that section to sell it *for.*"

The baby fussed. Dana bounced it without looking at it. "He knows. He says the ground's finished anyway. He says at least this way somebody in the family gets one thing out of it before it goes."

And that was the market at county scale, in a sentence, on a Tuesday: a man selling his grandchildren's rain in perpetuity to make a payment on a truck, because the ground was finished anyway, and being right that the ground was finished, and being the one who finished it by saying so. Della felt the old wrongness go up her wrists and she did not let it reach her face.

"I'll draw the sale form," she said. "Your granddad signs it himself, in front of me — I can't take it from you, he holds the deed. Bring him in before the Thursday window and I'll get you the true price. Not Meridian's number. The true one, so at least he knows what he's giving up when he takes less than it's worth to save a truck." She wrote the appointment on a card. "And Dana. Tell him to lease it if there's any way at all to lease it instead. A lease you can take back. This you can't."

"There's no way," Dana said, not unkindly, the way you'd correct someone about the weather, and took the card, and jostled the baby, and went out into the blue.

The bell settled. Junie had stopped writing again.

"You could've just sold it," Junie said. "The order's standing. It'd have been clean."

"It'd have been legal," Della said. "That's a different word. Log the appointment." She heard the edge in it and softened it, because the girl was twenty-two and still believed the two words meant the same thing, and Della's whole job for the next while was going to be deciding how long to let her believe it. "Everything we do here is legal, Junie. That's what makes it hard to tell where the harm's coming from. Write it down."

The bell went a third time. That was the morning in Hale — a bell, a form, a section of the sky going somewhere it wouldn't come back from. She had begun to think of the door as the county's throat.

Marvin Ott came in turning his hat.

He was sixty-six and stooped and gray to the eyebrows with the same dust that was in the butter, and the way he turned the hat, slow, working the brim through his big cracked hands, told Della what the visit was before he'd got the door shut. It was the turn of a man about to ask for something inside the rules that the rules would not give him. He'd been making it at her counter, off and on, for three years.

"Della."

"Marvin. Coffee's fresh if Junie made it and burnt if I did."

"I'm all right." He came to the counter and set the hat down and put both hands flat on either side of it, and she saw the dirt worn permanent into the creases, ground that would not wash out because it had stopped being on him and started being in him. "Wanted to ask you about the Cutter place lease. The half-inch."

She already knew. But you let a man ask. Letting him ask was most of what the job was, some days — standing still and giving a person the dignity of the sentence.

"The July allotment on Cutter," he said. "Half-inch a year, comes off it. Bank's got the ground now but the rain's still severed, still lease-able, and I got the field next to it going. My last watered field, Della. The alfalfa. I lose that field, I lose the cows, I lose the cows I'm done." He said it the way a man reports weather. No heat in it. "I want to lease that half-inch back. Onto my ground. Just the one season."

"You can," she said. "It's a lawful lease. Cutter's estate holds it, it's not futured out, and it's yours to bid on."

Something moved in his face that was almost hope, and she hated that she was about to take it back, and she took it back in the flat exact voice, because a true number set down flat was the one thing she could hand a man that wouldn't rot in his pocket by Thursday.

"The clearing price on July rain in this basin ran four hundred an acre-inch at the last window," she said. "Half-inch, your acreage, you're looking at a number with a comma in it. And you'll be bidding against the Renfro dairy, who put in for the whole Cutter block, and against Meridian's standing order, which takes anything east of the elevator at ten percent over ask." She let it sit. "I can't make the price. I can only tell you the price true. That's the half-inch of me you get to lease."

He nodded slowly, still working the brim, and did not argue, because he was not a man who argued with weather or with the price of it. He looked past her shoulder at the barometer on the wall, an old brass-cased wall unit with a needle that had not moved off FAIR in eleven weeks, and she watched him look at it the way she looked at it, the way you check a dead thing for a pulse out of a habit older than hope.

"Ground's been asking a while now," Marvin said. "Reckon it'll quit asking."

He was quiet a moment, still turning the hat, and then he said the thing she'd been waiting three years for someone to say plainly, and he said it looking at the dead barometer and not at her, which was how the men here said the hard things.

"You hear anything," he said, "about somebody bringing rain back to Hale? Real rain. A season of it." He turned the brim. "Fella at the elevator says there's a company been asking after the whole east block. Says they could water this valley back green if a body let them." He finally looked at her. "That'd be your table, wouldn't it. If a thing like that came through. You'd be the one signs it."

"I'd be the one files it," Della said. "If it were real. And it never is, Marvin. Nobody gives a county its rain back. You can only move rain. Anybody offering to green up Hale is offering to brown out somewhere else, and that somewhere is real, and it's somebody, and it's got a fella at *their* elevator too." She heard the flatness of her own voice and it did not comfort her. "It's not a gift. It's a bill somebody hasn't been handed yet."

"Well." Marvin considered that with the patience of a man who had considered a great many bills. "Long as it ain't me handed it. I'm tired of holding this county's."

There was one thing she could do, and it cost her nothing, which was the shameful part. She turned to the terminal.

"The Cutter July window doesn't open till the fourteenth," she said. "But there's a fractional under the Renfro standing order — a leftover point-oh-eight that didn't offset clean, sitting unassigned in the Cutter block. Too small for the dairy to bother reclaiming. I can flag it as a distress fraction under Section 12 and release it at floor price to the adjacent parcel." She met his eyes. "That's you. Point-oh-eight of an inch. At floor, that's under thirty dollars."

Marvin did the arithmetic she'd already done. Point-oh-eight of an inch on his alfalfa was two good rains in a summer, maybe three if the timing was kind, and it was not enough to save the field and both of them knew it was not enough to save the field. It was enough to make the field last a little longer than his hope did. It was the size of the mercy the county had left in it: a rounding error, released to a dying man at cost, with a filing fee she would waive and log as waived so the record stayed clean.

"You'd do that," he said.

"It's a Form 12 fraction. Favors have a filing fee. This one just has paperwork." She got the form. "I need your section number and your bond, and I need you to understand it's eight-hundredths of an inch, Marvin. It's not the field."

"I understand what it is." He gave her the numbers. She wrote them down. When she stamped it — the second *thunk* of the morning, softer somehow, or she told herself it was — he picked his hat up off the counter and put it on and stood a moment with his hand on the brim.

"You're a good broker, Della," he said. "Doss'd be proud."

She did not answer that. There was no answer to that a person could give and keep her breakfast down. She initialed the box and slid his copy across and said, "Rain your alfalfa when the window opens. Don't wait for a sky. There isn't going to be a sky."

He went out into the blue and the bell rang him gone.

Junie was quiet at the ledger for a while. Then, not looking up: "Why'd Hale sell so much of it? If everybody knew what dry does to a place."

"Nobody knew," Della said. "Or the people who knew weren't the people who sold." She was watching the door where Marvin had been. "It was cheap in the seventies. Money now, sky later. Later showed up." She heard how close that came to a sermon and cut it there, the way she cut everything there. "Log Marvin's fraction and mark the fee waived. I don't want Reyes finding an unwaived comp when the Authority comes through."

"The auditor's coming?"

"The auditor's always coming. That's what an auditor is."

She had not, until she said it, thought about Reyes; she thought about her now, briefly, the way you register a front you can't yet see — and set it down, and went to unlock the drawer for the day's certified copies, and that was when she found the card.

It had been pushed under the door in the night, along the floor, so that she'd stepped over it coming in and not seen it in the dust-dim. It lay against the baseboard face-up. Heavy stock, cream, the kind of card that cost more than Marvin's fraction. She picked it up. The printing was small and clean and expensive.

**KELLAN VOSS**, it said. *Regional Acquisitions.* And under that, in a blue that was not the sky's blue, that was a rich confident wet blue, the kind of blue a company chose when it had never once had to live under a spent one: **MERIDIAN SKY.** On the back, in a fountain-pen hand as neat as a ledger, three lines:

*Della — In town Thursday. Would value a half hour. Nothing to sign. — K.V.*

She stood at the counter with the card in her hand and the dust settling on the sill and the barometer holding at FAIR, and she knew what Meridian Sky was the way you know what a shape is on the horizon before it has a name — not the details, only the size of it. Meridian bought basins. Meridian was the great-grandchild of the men who'd made the county's sky cheap in the seventies, wearing a better suit. When Meridian wanted a half hour of a county broker's Thursday, it was not to buy a half-inch off a fallow section. It was because it had done the study already, run the numbers already, decided already, and had come, patient and pressed and remembering your name, to be sorry about it to your face.

*Nothing to sign.* That was the tell. In her experience the people who told you there was nothing to sign were the ones who understood exactly how much a signature was worth, and had come for one.

"Della?" Junie had stopped writing. "Something wrong?"

"No," Della said, and it was true and it was a lie, and she put the card face-down on the counter under the corner of the ledger so the wet blue wouldn't show. "Man wants a meeting. Men want meetings."

She got through the certified copies. She got through a bond confirmation and a deed pull and a call from the Authority satellite about the Critical Watch paperwork, and she kept her hands doing what her hands knew, and the card sat under the ledger corner all day like a barometer of a different weather, and she did not touch it again until she locked up.

She drove home the long way, past the Kettle.

She always drove home past the Kettle, which was a thing she did not examine, the way she did not examine tapping the barometer. The road ran along the top of the dry channel for a quarter mile, and there was the rusted sign, BOAT LAUNCH, its arrow pointing down a concrete ramp that went into gray pan and cracked mud and a scatter of bleached driftwood from a water that had not been there in the memory of anyone under thirty. The ramp ended in dust. Somewhere under the dust was where the river had been, the same river that rose in the mountains west and forked east and ran, thirty miles from here, past a green county, past a farm with a dairy and a hundred and sixty acres of honest alfalfa and a line strung between two posts where a woman she had not spoken to in eleven years hung out washing that dried white and stiff and clean, because Ridley had kept its rain, because their father had bought Ridley for that woman with everything Hale had left, and kept Della here in the dust to run the office and file it all down.

She did not think the sister's name. She had a discipline about the name the way she had a discipline about the drink. You did not pick it up. You did not turn it over in your hand. You let it lie where it lay, in the dry channel with the other things the water had left when it went.

At the house on Depot Street the dead lilac stood by the porch where her mother had planted it living, and the clothesline hung slack between its poles, and she did not use the line because the dust grayed the wash before it dried, so she brought her washing in wet from the machine and hung it over the kitchen chairs like a woman camping in her own life. She went inside and did not turn on the light and stood in the hot dark that had come in through the day and now sat on her chest the way the county's heat did at night, a weight the body could not file its way out of.

She took the chip out of her right coat pocket and held it in her palm. Eleven years. Bronze, worn smooth on both faces from her thumb, the number and the word gone soft under years of *no.* She turned it once, the way she'd turned it ten thousand nights, the way a tongue works a sore tooth, around and around, and she thought about the card on the counter and the wet blue on it, and about Marvin's eight-hundredths of an inch, and about the price of July rain with a comma in it, and about how the whole trick of staying sober was that you could hold a thing in your hand and know exactly what it would do to you and simply not do it, one night at a time, for eleven years, and how she had built a life on being the woman who could refuse.

She poured a glass of water from the tap and drank it standing up in the dark and it tasted of the pipes and the county and the far-off idea of rain, and she thought: *He'll offer me something on Thursday. And I'll say no.* She believed it the way you believe a barometer that reads FAIR — a dead instrument, its needle stopped at a word, and no reason yet in the room to doubt it.


---

# Chapter 2 — Form 3-A: Allotment of Record (Certified Copy)

> *An allotment, once severed from the deed, shall not revert to the parcel; and no certified copy of the record shall be construed to reopen what the record has closed.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §4(b)

The morning after the card, the wind turned around and came out of the east, which meant the dust that had settled overnight got picked back up and moved a foot to the left, and this was what passed for weather in Hale. Della opened the office into it. The blue was already up over the elevator, flat and spent and apologizing to no one who was listening, and she looked at the counter instead, the way you learned to. There was a message light on the terminal and a title company's name in the queue.

Junie was at the ledger. She had come in early to catch up the timestamps from the day before, and she had the good pen going, and the wide gray book open to the current page, and she did not look up right away because she was in the middle of a number and Della had taught her not to lose a number for a bell.

"Osgood Title," Junie said, when she'd finished. "They want a certified 3-A on the Larkin bottoms. There's a dispute."

"What kind."

"Two heirs and a buyer, all claiming the March allotment carried with the deed. Osgood needs to know if it was ever severed." Junie turned the screen so Della could see it. "It's old. It predates the digital register. It's back in the paper."

"Then it's back in the safe," Della said, and hung her coat, and felt the chip through the fabric, a small round weight in the right pocket, and left it where it hung.

The Larkin bottoms were the low ground under what had been the reservoir, before the reservoir was a gray pan with a boat ramp pointing into it. Now there was a family arguing over the water rights to a place that had no water, which was the county in a sentence, and Della did not say so, because Junie was twenty-two and would find that out on her own soon enough.

The paper register lived in the safe with the stamp, in the third drawer down, in acid-stiff folders arranged by section and year in her father's hand and then her own. She spun the dial and heard the tumblers drop and pulled the drawer, and the smell came up at her, the specific smell of old rag paper and iron gall and the machine oil she used on the hinge, and under it something drier that she thought of as the smell of the seventies, though a smell cannot be a decade. She found the Larkin section. She walked her fingers back through the years.

The dispute was easy. It took her four minutes. The March allotment on the Larkin bottoms had been severed in a block, sold clean, and the certified copy would show the buyer that the heirs had nothing and the heirs that the buyer had bought a deed with a hole in it. She found the sale entry and read it to be sure, and it was sure, and she pulled the folder to make the certified copy, and that was when the folder opened to the block, and the block was in her father's hand.

She knew the hand before she read the words. You know a hand the way you know a walk in a hallway. Doss wrote a tall exact copperplate that leaned very slightly backward, as if the letters were bracing against something, and he crossed his sevens the county way, and he had taught her to do the same, and her sevens on the current page thirty feet away were crossed like his, and she had never once thought about it until now.

The block sale ran two full pages. She had known it ran two full pages the way you know a scar is on your arm without looking. It was dated in a spring forty years gone, and it listed the parcels one under the other in his tall backward hand — Larkin bottoms, and the sections around them, and then the ones that were not around them, the ones scattered across the east half of the county with no reason to be in the same sale except that a man had gone door to door and gathered them, and against each parcel a volume in acre-inches, and against the volume a buyer, and the buyer was the same on every line. **Kettle Basin Land & Rain.** A name that meant nothing to Junie and everything to the drawer.

And in the margin, in a smaller version of the same hand, at the foot of the second page, ruled off neat inside its own little box because a fee is a fee and you keep it clean, the broker's commission. His. Noted to the third decimal, the way she noted everything to the third decimal, the way he had taught her. Conservation balanced. The county's rain went out on the top line and his fee came back on the bottom one and the two of them met in the middle and closed, and it was clean, it was perfectly clean, it balanced to the third decimal, which was all the Act had ever required of anyone.

Forty years ago a girl of fourteen had stood at this counter — this counter, the same brass rail worn to gold where the hands go — and watched her father assemble that block one parcel at a time, an inch here and a half-inch there off ground that was already going thin, and she had thought it was the most competent thing she had ever seen, a man gathering a scattered thing into an order, and she had been proud of him, and she had wanted the sevens crossed like his, and she had not known what the block was for, and by the time she knew, her sister had already been thirty miles east for a season, breathing, and the two things had never afterward come apart in her — the pride and the block — so that she could not read a fee ruled off neat in a margin without feeling fourteen and proud and complicit, the whole knot of it going up her wrist the way the stamp went up her wrist, the animal part of her that thought a father's hand was God's and not a lot number.

She did not look at it long. You learned not to.

"Della?" Junie had stopped writing. "You find it?"

"Found it." Her voice came out level, filed, in order. "It severed back in the seventies. The heirs have nothing. I'll draw the copy."

She took the block sale to the copier at the back and certified only the one line Osgood needed — the Larkin bottoms, severed, sold, closed — and she did not certify the second page, and she did not certify the margin, because a certified copy shows exactly what is requested and not one line more, and this was the discipline of the office and also, this morning, a mercy she was extending to herself. She stamped the copy. The *thunk* went up her wrist. She logged it, marked the timestamp, put the folder back in the third drawer with the block sale face-down, spun the dial, and heard the tumblers drop.

"Can I ask you something," Junie said, "that's not about the copy."

"Ask."

Junie had the pen still in her hand, held the way you hold a thing you've forgotten you're holding. She was looking at the screen where the block sale had been. "Why'd Hale sell so much of it? I mean I know it was the seventies and it was cheap and money-now, sky-later, you told me. But that." She nodded at the screen. "Whole blocks. Neighbors all going at once, in the same sale, to the same buyer. That's not money-now. That's somebody *organizing* it." She looked up, and her face was open and careful and entirely without accusation, which was worse than accusation. "Who organized it? Who put the block together?"

The barometer on the wall held at FAIR. Della heard the little office sounds she was always hearing — the terminal fan, the flag chain ticking against the pole in the east wind. She had a way of answering this question that was true and that closed the drawer, and she used it now, because Junie still believed the record was a thing that told you what had happened rather than a thing that decided what had happened, and Della's whole job for the foreseeable was going to be deciding how long to let her believe it.

"A broker put it together," Della said. "That's what a block sale is. Somebody with standing goes parcel to parcel and assembles the volume, because a buyer that size won't come for an inch, he'll come for a river, so you make him a river out of everybody's inch." She heard the flatness of it and let it stand, because flat was the one register in the office that couldn't be argued down into a comfort. "It's lawful. The broker takes a fee on the assembly. It's on the second page."

"Which broker."

"The one who had the chair." Della slid the certified copy into its envelope and squared the corners. "Log it certified, single line, per request. And pull the Renfro bond confirmation, it's due back today and I don't want Reyes finding it stale when the Authority comes through."

Junie held the pen a moment more. She was not stupid; she was unblooded, which is a different thing, and the difference showed in the way she let the question go — not because she was satisfied, Della saw, but because she had understood, in the flat answer and the squared corners, that pressing it would cost more than she could pay this early, and she filed that away too, the way you file a thing you'll take out later when your hands are older. She logged the Osgood copy. She pulled the Renfro bond. The morning went on the way mornings went on, a bell, a form, a section of the county's history certified single line per request, and the block sale lay face-down in the third drawer, and Della kept her hands doing what her hands knew.

At noon Junie went to the Trestle for a sandwich and Della was alone in the office with the barometer and the safe.

She did not open the safe; the drawer was closed. But she went to the cabinet under the terminal, the low one where the office kept the things that were not the county's — the spare stamp pad, the machine oil, a tin of pens — and behind those, flat against the back, a manila envelope with nothing written on it, and inside it a single photograph she had not looked at in longer than she could account for and could not have thrown away if a form had required it.

It was the two of them. Cass and Della, on the Larkin bottoms, before the reservoir went, before the block. Cass was maybe ten, which made Della seven, and there was water behind them, a whole reservoir of it, flat and bright and stupid with abundance, and Cass had Della by the wrist and was pulling her toward it, looking back at the camera with the bossy joy of an older sister with a body of water to give. They had the same hands even then. You could see it. The water in it was thirty years gone and the girl pulling was fifty-seven now and had not spoken to the girl being pulled in eleven years.

Della put the photograph back behind the pens and closed the cabinet.

The name was a thing she kept in the drawer with the drink and did not open. You did not pick it up. But the block sale had picked it up for her — it was the whole reason the water in the photograph was gone and the girl pulling was in a green county thirty miles east instead of dead in this dry one — and Della sat at the counter with the noon light coming gray through the dust on the window and did a thing she had not done in a long time, which was to take out a sheet of the office's plain paper and write, at the top, in the tall backward hand her father had taught her, a name.

*Cass.*

She looked at it. She had not written it in years. It sat on the page and did nothing.

She wrote: *There's a company been asking after the east block. I don't know yet what they want but I know what they are and I want you to*

She stopped. She read what she had. *I want you to.* Want you to what. Say *be careful,* to a woman who had spent eleven years being careful of exactly one person and it was Della. Say *Meridian,* and have the first word out of eleven years of silence be the name of the thing coming for her, so that Cass would know, correctly, that Della had known, and had written, and had not been able to say the one thing under it, which was not about the company at all. There was no version of the note that did not read, to Cass, as the broker warning the parcel. No version where the warning was not also, somehow, the assembly — a woman going door to door, gathering.

Della folded the sheet in half and in half again and did not tear it up, because tearing it up was a decision and she was not ready to have made one, and she put it in the right coat pocket next to the chip, against the bronze, two things she was holding and not doing, and she believed folding it was different from finishing it the way she believed a barometer that read FAIR.

Junie came back with the smell of the grill on her and half a sandwich for Della that Della hadn't asked for and ate anyway, standing up, and they did the afternoon. Osgood called to say the buyer was disappointed and the heirs were furious and both of them were going to have to accept that the record was the record, and Della said yes, that was generally how it went, and hung up. A man came in to future out a quarter-inch of next year's rain to make a note payment, and Della drew the form and told him true what it would cost him, in perpetuity, to have the money now, and he took the money now, the way they did, the way the ground was finished anyway. She stamped it. The *thunk* went up her wrist. It was the third stamp of a two-day stretch and she noticed, the way she had begun to notice things she had spent twenty-two years not noticing, that the wrist kept a small dull ache after now, and that she was gripping the brass harder than the ink required, and she made herself loosen her hand, and could not have said what she was gripping against.

Near closing the terminal chimed the overnight feed preview, the small courtesy the Authority pushed out before the market's real day, and Junie read it because reading it was hers now.

"Nothing much," Junie said, scanning. "Couple of intracounty leases. And a — huh." She leaned in. "There's a study filed. Feasibility, third-party. Interbasin." She looked up. "9-C feasibility. Kettle sub-basin to — " she frowned at the origin field the way you frown at a word you've read wrong " — from Ridley Fork."

The east wind ticked the flag chain against the pole.

"Let me see," Della said, and her voice was level, and she came around the counter and read the line over Junie's shoulder, and it was worse than Junie could know. A feasibility study. Not an application, not a transfer, nothing yet with a seal on it or a form to refuse — only a company running the numbers in the open where the courtesy feed would show them, which was itself a kind of message, the way *nothing to sign* was a kind of message, from people who understood precisely what a number was worth. Origin basin: Ridley Fork. The volume in the summary line matched, to the acre-inch, an old intact allotment on a hundred and sixty acres of bottomland alfalfa and a small dairy, and Della did not need to pull the parcel to know whose it was, because there was a photograph behind the pens of a girl pulling her toward a body of water, and the girl lived on that alfalfa now, and hung washing on a line that dried white and stiff and clean because their father had bought Ridley for her with everything Hale had left, and the fee ruled off neat in the margin of a folder that had been closed forty years and was, this morning, exactly as closed as the past ever is.

"Can they do that?" Junie said. "Just — study it? Somebody's whole basin?"

"They can study anything." Della straightened up. In her right coat pocket the folded note sat against the chip, and she was aware of it the way you are aware of a stone in your shoe, and she thought about the name in the tall backward hand at the top of it, and the door-to-door gathering that a warning and an assembly could not, from thirty miles east, be told apart. "A study doesn't move a cloud, Junie. It's paper. It's just paper."

She heard herself say it — *it's just paper* — in the office where her father's paper had moved her sister thirty miles and dried a reservoir to a boat ramp pointing at dust, and she did not correct it, because the correction was the whole book of her life and she was not going to read it out at a counter at closing time.

"Log the feasibility to the watch file," Della said. "Origin, volume, applicant, and the timestamp. Get the timestamp exact." She got her coat. The note and the chip shifted together in the pocket, bronze and paper, the two things she was holding. "And Junie."

"Yeah."

"Don't put my name on it."

Junie looked up, not understanding, because there was no reason for Della's name to be on a Meridian feasibility study, no line on the form that called for it, and she would have said so if the phone had not rung right then — the after-hours line, which only the Authority and one other kind of caller used — and Della, reaching for it, saw the number on the little screen, and it was not the Authority.

It was a Meridian exchange. And under the number, because Voss did his homework, because Voss remembered everything, the caller had set the name to display, and the name that came up on the after-hours line at closing, in a wet confident blue Della could not see but could feel, was not KELLAN VOSS.

It was **PRINE, C.**


---

# Chapter 3 — Form 12: Notice of Basin Condition (Hale, Critical Watch)

> *A basin found below floor volume shall be posted as under Critical Watch; the posting is a statement of condition and not a promise of remedy, and shall not be construed to create in any parcel-holder a right to relief.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §9(c)

The Authority posted the notice on a Wednesday, and by the time Della got to the grange on Thursday evening it had already been read aloud in every kitchen in the county by somebody who did not understand it, which was the worst way for a thing like that to travel. The notice was one page. She had a copy folded to a hard square against the chip in her coat, and she had read it enough times to know it said almost nothing, and that the almost-nothing was the cruelest thing it could have said.

The Hale grange sat where the last paved street gave out into gravel and the gravel gave out into the pivot circles, and above it the sky held the color it always held, going down toward dark — the apologetic blue draining westward, not into red, never into an honest sunset, just paling out toward the ground until the ground took it. Della had stopped, this last week, thinking of that blue as a color. She had begun to think of it as a finding. A thing the sky had been made to admit and could not stop admitting.

She parked the truck and went in the broker's door, the one that let her up the little wooden steps to the platform without walking through the crowd, and she was glad of it, because the hall was full.

It was fuller than she'd seen it since the co-op vote. Folding chairs in rows, men along the back wall with their hats in their hands, women in the good chairs down front, children on the floor at the edges where the heat pooled, two ceiling fans turning the hot air over itself without cooling anything, and the smell of it — hay dust and machine grease and sweat and the dry mineral smell of a room full of people who had come to be told about water. The Authority man from the satellite office, a soft young administrator named Prewitt who had driven up from the district seat and clearly wished he had not, stood at the lectern with the notice enlarged on a posterboard behind him, HALE COUNTY · CRITICAL WATCH in the Authority's flat sans-serif, and he was already losing them. She could hear it. The sound a room made when it had stopped believing the man at the front.

"—and I want to stress," Prewitt was saying, "that a Critical Watch is a *precautionary* posting. It does not mean the basin is—"

"Then what's it mean," a man said from the middle, not standing, and the room made the small sound of agreeing with the tone if not the words.

Prewitt looked down at his page. He was going to read them the definition. Della watched him decide to, and thought: *that will end you,* and it did.

"'A basin is posted to Critical Watch,'" Prewitt read, "'when its measured volume is found to be within one standard season's variance of the statutory floor, below which the Authority cannot guarantee the maintenance of the water table, the surface flow, or'"—he faltered, because he had reached the part—"'or the habitability of the affected—'"

"The what," a woman said.

"—of the affected townships," Prewitt finished, quiet, and the room went quiet with him, and then it did not go quiet, it went the other way, all at once, a dozen voices, and Prewitt put both hands flat on the lectern the way a man braces on a fence a bull is coming through.

Della came the rest of the way up onto the platform.

She had not planned to. Her job at a meeting like this was to sit to the side with the ledger and answer questions of fact when they were put to her — verify a number, confirm a window, be the desk the storm broke against. But Prewitt was drowning and the room was turning, and she was the only one on the platform whose name was on a door on Main Street, so she came forward and Prewitt stepped back with the relief of a man handing off a live wire.

"Della Prine's girl," somebody said from the back, not unkindly. She was fifty-four and a licensed civil servant and twenty-two years the broker, and still, when a room needed to place her fast, Doss Prine's girl. She did not correct it. It was, tonight, useful.

"You all know me," she said. She did not raise her voice; she had learned long ago at this same lectern, watching her father, that you brought a loud room down by getting quieter, so they had to still themselves to catch you. "So I'll give it to you straight, because Mr. Prewitt's giving it to you correct, and those aren't the same thing tonight."

A small laugh. It bought her the room for a minute, and she spent the minute carefully.

"The Watch doesn't take anybody's rain," she said. "It's not a transfer. Nobody's allotment moves because of this posting. It's the Authority saying on the record, in writing, that Hale's stretch of the Kettle sub-basin is down to about one bad season off the floor. That's the whole of it. It's the county's barometer, and the needle's low."

"How low," Marvin Ott said.

He stood along the side wall with his hat in his hands; she had not seen him come in, and she was sorry it was him who asked, because answering him true in front of everyone was a different thing than answering him true across her counter.

"Low enough that the Authority wrote it down," she said. "They don't post a Watch to be dramatic, Marvin. They post it because posting it moves a liability off their desk and onto the record. When the Authority puts a thing in writing, it's not warning you. It's covering itself for when the thing it wrote down comes true."

That landed the way a true thing lands, heavy and unwelcome, and she felt the room change — not toward her, but toward the shape of it, the machine of it, the sense settling over the hall that the men who'd built this had built it to be blameless.

"So what do we do," a younger man called, Renfro's boy from the dairy, angry the way the young ones got angry, at a target. "What's the remedy? The paper says the Authority *may compel remedy.* Compel who? Do what?"

And here it was, the thing she'd known she'd be asked, with no honest answer that would not either lie to them or gut them.

"The word in the statute is *may,*" Della said. "Not *shall.* The Authority may compel a remedy. It doesn't say it will, and it doesn't say what, and there's no clause anywhere in the Act that gives your parcel a right to demand it." She let that sit. "A remedy in this system means one thing. Rain moved *into* Hale from somewhere it's already falling. You can't make weather; you can only carry it. So a remedy for Hale is a shortage for somewhere else, and the somewhere else has got parcels too, and a broker too, and a grange hall too, with people in it asking the same question you're asking me." She looked at Renfro's boy and did not spare him and did not enjoy it. "There's no remedy that isn't a bill. The Watch only answers whether Hale's finally poor enough for somebody to think it's worth handing us one."

The hall was silent now in the other way, the settled way, the way a room goes when it has been told the truth and hates it and can't fault it. She stood in it. She had given them accurate information in a flat exact voice and it had done to the room what it always did, which was leave everyone worse and no one wronged, and she understood — the way you understand a thing with your body before your mind will hold it — that this was the whole of what she was for. Not to steal their rain. To stand at the front of the room where their rain was discussed and give them nothing to grab onto but the truth, which held no water at all.

Marvin turned his hat once and looked at the notice on the posterboard and looked at her, and said, mild, to the room and not to her: "She's the only one up there told us anything we can set a watch by."

It was a kindness. It went up her wrist like the stamp.

Prewitt took the questions after that — the how-do-I-file, the will-my-bond-hold, the ones with answers — and Della stepped back and let him, and the hall's heat sat on her chest, and the notice in her coat was pressed flat against the chip, the two hard flat things, the county's *no rain* and her own eleven years of *no,* riding against each other over her heart. She had spent a whole life believing those were different kinds of *no.*

It was while Prewitt explained bond continuance to a widow in the third row that Della saw the man at the back.

He stood along the rear wall with the farmers, but he was not a farmer. She knew it the way you know a delivered rain from a real one — by the wrongness of the fit. He wore no hat to turn. He had a pressed shirt the color of good cream and shoes that had never once been right for gravel, and a small leather notebook open on his palm, and he was not writing angry, the way a farmer would write. He was writing the way she wrote at the counter. Taking down what the room was, in a neat hand, for a purpose. He caught her looking and did not look away and did not smile; he inclined his head a half-inch, the way you'd acknowledge a colleague across a room where you were both working, and went back to his notebook.

She knew the wet blue on his card before she knew his face. She had put the card face-down under the ledger corner two days ago and not turned it over since, and here he was, turned over himself, standing in the back of her county's grief with a pen, taking its measure.

She did not go to him. She stayed at the side of the platform through the last of the questions and watched the room empty into the dark, the men putting their hats back on at the threshold, resettling them against a sky that had nothing in it, and when the hall was down to Prewitt packing his posterboard and two grange women stacking chairs, Della went out the broker's door into the gravel lot.

The heat had not broken with the dark. It sat over the lot the way it sat in the house on Depot Street, come in through the day and staying, and the sky overhead had gone to the deep spent color it went to at night, a blue so dark it was nearly the absence of blue, the last of the day's apology draining off the edge of the world. She was almost to the truck when he said her name.

"Della."

He said it the way you'd say the name of someone you'd known a while, warm, no title, and she stopped with her hand on the truck door and turned, and there he was, come around the corner of the hall unhurried, the notebook put away, both hands empty and open, a man with nothing to sign.

"Mr. Voss," she said.

"Kellan." He smiled, an unforced one, tired around the eyes in a way that read as human and might even have been. "I'm sorry — I know that was your county's bad night and I stood in the back of it like a crow. I should've waited for Thursday proper. But I watched you take that room off that boy from the district, and I wanted to say so to your face before you decided what I was."

"I know what you are," Della said. "You're the fella been asking after the east block."

Something moved across his face that was almost pleasure — not at being caught; at being *understood,* she thought, at not having to do the first ten minutes of a thing. "Word travels," he said.

"There's not much else moving in this county to carry it."

He laughed, once, low, and it was real, and that was the part she would think about later, driving home: that the laugh had been real. "No," he said. "There isn't. That's the whole of it, isn't it. The thing that room doesn't have a word for yet." He looked past her at the dark hall, the last light going off as the grange women found the switch. "You told them the truth in there. Every word of it. There's no remedy that isn't a bill. I've said the same sentence in forty grange halls and never once heard a broker say it out loud to her own people. Most of them let the district man drown and keep their hands clean."

"I don't have clean hands," Della said. "I have accurate ones. It's a different thing."

He took that in the way a man takes a card he's been dealt that's better than he expected, no flicker, just filing it. "Doss used to say something like that," he said. "Not those words. But that."

The name went through the dark between them and she held very still around it. "You didn't know my father."

"No," Voss said, easy. "I read him. Forty years of his hand in the county register before I ever drove up here, because that's the job. He was the best broker in the sub-basin for a generation, and he knew exactly what the system was and served it anyway, and wrote it all down in a fair straight column so the next person would know what they'd inherited." He let a beat go by. "He signed the note that carried Cass over to Ridley. I know that too. I won't pretend I don't — you'd smell it on me by Thursday and think worse of me for the hiding."

He said the name — *Cass* — the way he'd said *Della,* warm, familiar, a name he'd earned. He had not earned it. He had bought it, in a register, for the price of a train ticket and an afternoon of reading, and set it down in the gravel between them like a thing he was returning to her, and Della felt the ground move under her the way a delivered storm moved the barometer — the needle finally coming off FAIR because something was arriving on schedule she had not asked for and could not stop.

He had known before he pushed the card under her door. He knew the woman in Ridley whose sky he'd come to move was Doss Prine's other daughter, and he had come anyway, and he was going to be sorry about it to her face — which was worse than hiding it, because the sorrow would be real too.

She did not let it reach her face. She had eleven years of practice at not letting a thing reach her face while it took her apart under the counter.

"You've done your reading," she said.

"I have."

"Then you know I'll say no."

"I do," Voss said, and here it was, the thing that would ride home with her in the dark, the thing she'd turn over like the chip: he said it *gently,* not as a threat and not as a challenge, but the way you'd agree with a woman about her own weather. "I know you're going to say no, Della. I'd think less of you if you didn't. I'm not here Thursday to change that." He put his hand up, not near her, a small open gesture at the empty hall, the drained sky. "I just wanted you to know that when I ask, I'll be asking somebody who understands the machine better than I do. That's a rare thing in this work. I mostly ask people who don't know what they're standing in." He smiled again, and this one was almost sad. "Get home safe. It's a long dark road. I'll see you Thursday."

He turned and walked toward a car she could not see, unhurried, a man who had never had to hurry because the Act hurried for him, and Della stood in the gravel with the heat on her chest and the notice folded against the chip, and watched the last light die in the grange behind him.

She had come to tell her county the exact size of its ruin, and done it well, and the only person in the hall who had truly *seen* her do it — seen the competence, named it, valued it — was the man who had driven up from another world to use that competence to move a storm off her sister.

She got in the truck and did not start it. She had already, without deciding to, taken the chip out of her right coat pocket, and she turned it against the wheel, around and around, eleven years worn smooth, the muscle of *no* doing what it did on its own now, out of an old animal certainty that if she just held the thing and did not do the thing, she was safe.

For half of one minute at the back of that terrible room it had felt good to be *seen.* She had been so long the desk the storm broke against that she'd forgotten she was a person who could be known, and a man had looked at her and known her, and the warmth of it had gone through her before she caught what the warmth was *for.* She would say no to the offer, clean, on Thursday, and mean it. It was the other thing he'd found she had no form to refuse.

She put the chip back in her pocket and started the truck and drove home the long way, past the Kettle, past the BOAT LAUNCH sign pointing down its ramp into the dark pan where the water wasn't, and above the dry channel the sky held its finding, admitting and admitting, and thirty miles east under the same drained dark a line hung between two posts with washing on it, white and clean and drying, trusting the sky the way you trust a thing you have not yet learned is for sale, and Della drove between the two skies with her hands steady on the wheel and did not think the name, and the not-thinking was the loudest it had been in eleven years.


---

# Chapter 4 — Form 9-C: Interbasin Reassignment (Feasibility Study)

> *Feasibility does not imply authorization; a study is a description of what may be done, and confers upon no party the right to do it.* — Reg. 9-C.4

Voss came on the Thursday he'd said he would, at the hour a man comes when he wants you to know he keeps his word about small things so you'll trust him on the large. The bell went at ten past nine and Della knew who it was before she looked up, because the dust that came in with him did not come in the way the county's dust came, in a gray flour off the pivot-circles; it came in a single soft puff off good shoes that had been clean when he parked, shoes that were wrong for the gravel out front and right for everywhere he usually stood.

"Della," he said, as if they had known each other a long time and it had gone well. "I hope I'm not early."

"You're on time. That's rarer." She did not come around the counter. The counter was the whole architecture of the job — a broker stood behind it and the county stood in front of it, and everything that happened, happened across that line of scarred oak. "Junie, the certified copies for the Renfro title can wait an hour. Go get the mail."

Junie looked at the man's shoes and then at Della, and understood she was being sent out, and went, and the bell rang her gone. Voss watched her go with something that might have been kindness. "Your clerk," he said. "Alcott. Her granddad ran cattle up on the Sump road, didn't he. Before it dried."

"You've been reading."

"I read the county before I come to it. It's just manners." He set a slim folder on the counter, unopened, and left his hand flat on it, not pushing it, the way you'd rest a hand on a dog you didn't want to startle. "I'm going to tell you the whole thing straight, because you've been at this longer than I have and you'll see through anything I dress up, and I'd rather you spend that on the study than on me." He smiled, and it was a good smile, a warm and slightly tired one, the smile of a man sorry about the weather he was bringing. "Meridian wants to give Hale its rain back."

She waited. Under the counter her thumb found the seam of her coat pocket where the chip hung on the hook, and did not go in.

"Not a lease," Voss said. "Not a season. We buy back the east block — the allotments this county sold off in the seventies, in perpetuity, cheap, the ones that are dying it — we buy them back at our own cost from the parties upstream who hold them, and we return them to the deeds they were severed from. Marvin Ott's neighbors. The Webber south section. The whole apologizing half of your sky." He said *apologizing* like a man who had heard her word for it and liked it and was giving it back to her as a gift. "We restore it. A real water-year. Enough to green Larkin. Enough that your barometer over there remembers what it's for."

"You can't make rain," Della said. "You can only move it."

"I know." He said it gently, the way you'd agree with a child who'd said the true thing. "That's the whole business, isn't it. That's why I brought the study and not a promise." He turned the folder a quarter-inch toward her, still not letting go. "Everything that greens Hale comes off somewhere. Conservation. You know the arithmetic better than my engineers. So the only honest way to offer a county its life is to show it, up front, the ledger of where the life comes from. No surprises downwind. I don't like surprises downwind. Surprises downwind are how you get a hearing." He took his hand off the folder. "It's already done, the study. The math balances to the third decimal. It just needs a broker's seal on the offset to be real, and Hale's broker is you."

Della looked at the folder and did not open it.

"You want me to broker a 9-C," she said.

"I want you to *look* at a 9-C. Feasibility isn't authorization — it says so right on the reg, you'll have read it. Nobody's signing anything today. I told you on the card. Nothing to sign." He put both hands in his coat pockets, unthreatening, a man with empty hands. "I just don't think a woman should be asked to decide the fate of her county on my word about the numbers. So here are the numbers. Look at them tonight, over a coffee. If they're a lie, you'll catch it, and you'll throw me out, and you'll be right." He tipped his head at the folder. "They're not a lie. That's what's going to make this hard."

She opened it.

It was clean work; she'd give it that. It was the cleanest work she'd seen cross her counter in years, the kind of study her father used to admire out loud in the last years, *look how they've filed it, Della, look how neat a thing you can make out of a robbery.* The east-block buyback was there, parcel by parcel, the perpetuity-severed allotments named and priced and routed home, an inch and a quarter here, three-quarters there, the sky reassembled on paper over Hale like a broken cup glued so well you had to hold it to the light to find the seams. Larkin filling. A water-year. The corn on the Hollis ground drinking honest rain instead of Hollis's own robbed-from-himself fraction. Marvin's alfalfa under a real July storm, a wet clean line crossing his last field on a date, the way delivered rain crossed a field, and Marvin standing in it with his hat off.

She turned the page to the offset. Because there was always an offset. Because you could not make rain, you could only move it, and every gift on the east-block pages had to be, on the facing pages, a theft with an address.

The offset basin was named at the top of the page in the study's neat sans-serif, and it was Ridley.

She had known it would be Ridley the way she'd known the card was Meridian the way you know the size of a shape on the horizon before it has a name. Ridley was the nearest full basin. Ridley had kept its rain. Ridley had margin to bleed, which was only another way of saying Ridley had something left to take, which was only the market's way of saying Ridley had been complacent, had trusted its sky, had hung its washing out white and stiff and clean in the belief that a sky over your own ground was yours. Distance decayed a transfer; you stole from your neighbor, not from Brazil; the victim was always local, always downwind, always someone you might know. Ridley was thirty miles east, downhill, a different fork of the same river. Ridley was the only place close enough and full enough to green Hale.

She kept turning pages until she found the parcel schedule, because the study was thorough, because Voss did not like surprises downwind, because a 9-C had to name the exact severed volume and its origin parcels down to the section and quarter-section and deed. She ran her finger down the Ridley Fork column. Bottomland. A hundred and sixty acres. Alfalfa and a small dairy. An allotment old and intact and never sold, the exact volume — she checked it against the east-block total on the facing page, and it matched, of course it matched, it had been built to match — the exact volume Hale needed.

The deed reference at the foot of the parcel line read PRINE, C. & PRINE, W.

The office was very quiet. The barometer held at FAIR. On the wall the needle did not move, and in her chest the other needle, the one she'd learned to read in her fillings and her wrists, dropped off a cliff, the way pressure drops before a delivery, before a storm arrives on schedule that you can hear coming and be standing in the wrong place for.

She did not let it reach her face. She had eleven hundred stamps of practice at not letting a thing reach her face. She turned the page back over, squared the folder's edge against the counter, and looked up at Voss, who was watching her with an attention so complete and so gentle it was almost tender, the attention of a man who had done his reading and was waiting to see which of the things he'd read about her would be true.

"You know whose that is," Della said.

She had meant it as a fact and it came out almost as a question, which she hated, and she watched him decide how to answer it, and she watched the decision take no time at all, because he'd decided it weeks ago in an office with better carpet.

"I know it's a Prine allotment," Voss said. "I know the county broker is a Prine. I did the reading." He let that sit exactly as long as it needed to, no longer. "I didn't pick Ridley because of you, Della. I want to say that plainly. The physics picked Ridley. It's the nearest full basin, it's got the margin, and the volume runs almost to the acre-inch — I couldn't have built a cleaner offset if I'd designed the county from scratch." He paused. "That the holder shares your name is a thing I noticed. I notice things. It's not why."

Which was true, and was a lie, and was the exact shape of every true lie the whole rest of this was going to be built from — a man telling you the physics chose, the market chose, the arithmetic chose, nobody chose, and being right that the physics didn't care whose washing hung on the offset line, and being wrong that this made anyone's hands clean, because the physics did not fill out the form. A hand filled out the form. Her hand.

"She's my sister," Della said.

She had not planned to say it. The name was a thing you did not pick up, did not turn over in your hand, and she kept it that way the way she kept the bronze coin in her pocket and not in her mouth — and she had just set the whole thing on the counter between them, in front of a man who collected exactly this. She saw him receive it. She saw him not be surprised. And she saw, for one beat that she would turn over later in the dark and never be able to read, something move behind the gentleness that might have been the weight of it arriving, or might have been the satisfaction of a study confirmed. He let the beat pass. He was very good at letting beats pass.

"Then I'm sorry," Voss said, and he sounded it, which was worse than if he hadn't. "I mean that. I'd rather it were a stranger's name on that page. It'd be easier for both of us." He didn't reach for the folder. "But I'll tell you the thing I came to tell you, and then I'll go, and you can hate me on your own time. Ridley can spare it. That's what the study says and it's true — pull this volume and Ridley stays well above floor, well above critical, a green county that's a little less green, a family that has one dry season and grumbles and goes on. Nobody evacuates. Nobody drowns. Your sister brings in her washing a day late one week in July and never knows why." He said it as a comfort, and it was almost one, and that was the trap of him, that his comforts were half true. "And Hale *lives.* Marvin Ott lives. That girl with the baby keeps her granddad's section. You green a dying county and the cost is your sister waits on a cloud for one summer." He spread his hands. "I'm not asking you to choose between them. I'm telling you there's a form where you don't have to. Where everybody's a little wet."

"There's no form where everybody's a little wet," Della said. "You can't make rain. Somebody's dry. You've just moved which somebody, and dressed it so I can't see her from the counter."

"You can see her fine," Voss said quietly. "That's your whole problem, and I respect it. Most brokers can't see anybody. You can see all of them at once, upwind and down, and it's why you're good and it's why you're tired." He was not unkind. It would have been so much easier if he'd been unkind. "The county behind you is dying today, Della. In real time. It's dying while we stand here being decent to each other. And thirty miles east there's a basin with more rain than it can drink, and a form that would move the difference, and a broker who could sign it, and the only thing between Hale and a green year is that the broker's tired and the holder's her sister." He picked no phone up, made no threat, remembered her clerk's grandfather and her father's neatness and her sister's initials and threatened none of it. "I think that's a terrible thing to hang a county on. But I understand you didn't build it. I'm just the man who has to say it out loud."

Della closed the folder.

She squared it once more against the oak, slid it three inches back across the counter toward him, and put her hand flat on top of it the way he had, resting, not pushing, a hand on a dog you didn't want to startle.

"No," she said.

She said it flat and exact, the way she told a man the true price of July rain, one word set down clean with nothing under it for him to pry at. She did not raise it. She did not explain it. She had learned, over eleven years of holding a bronze coin in her palm and knowing exactly what it would do to her and simply not doing it, that *no* was a whole sentence, that the second you began to justify it you had begun to negotiate it, that the muscle only worked if you refused to reach for the thing at all.

"No," she said again, because she could feel that he was going to make her say it twice and she would rather spend it than have it taken. "I won't broker it. Not this volume, not this offset, not this basin. Not any 9-C that greens Hale off Ridley. Take the study back, or leave it and I'll shred it, but I won't put my seal to it, and without a seal it isn't a transfer, it's a wish. You said it yourself. Nothing's real till a broker signs it. I'm the broker. I'm not signing it."

She waited for the argument. She had braced for the argument — the county dying in real time, Marvin in the flood of his own hope, the girl and the baby and the truck, all of it aimed at the soft place where a decent person keeps the yes she's ashamed of.

The argument didn't come.

Voss looked at her for a moment with what she would later understand was not disappointment. It was recognition. It was a man checking a reading off a study and finding it accurate to the third decimal. Then he nodded, once, the nod you give a barometer that reads FAIR when you'd hoped, a little, that it might have moved.

"All right," he said. And — this was the part she would take home and turn over in the hot dark until it wore smooth — he did not pick the folder up.

"I'll leave the study," Voss said. He took his hands out of his pockets, empty, done. "You'll want it later. Not to change your mind — I don't think you're a woman who changes her mind, and I wouldn't insult you by waiting for it. You'll want it because when this goes the way it's going to go, you'll want to be able to point to the day somebody offered you the clean version, across a counter, with a coffee, and gave you the chance to say no while no still stopped something." He buttoned his coat. "There's going to be a day it doesn't. I'd like you to remember I told you that kindly."

"Get out of my office," Della said, without heat, which was how the people here said the hard things.

"It was good to finally meet you." He meant it. He went to the door, and the bell rang, and he stopped with his hand on it, and looked back at the folder sitting on her counter under her flat hand, cream-colored and clean and balanced to the third decimal, a broken cup glued so well you had to hold it to the light. He did not say the rest of it. He let the folder say it, and the barometer, and the door.

The bell rang him gone.

Della stood behind the counter with her hand on the study and the dust settling on the sill and the barometer holding at FAIR, and she did not move for a while. Then she took her hand off the folder and opened the safe and took out the stamp — not to use it, there was nothing to stamp, there would be nothing to stamp — just to have the weight of it in her hand, the brass worn shallow at the rim from her father's forty years and her own twenty-two, the small honest tool meant for older work. She held it the way she held the chip. She thought about the parcel schedule and the deed reference PRINE, C. & PRINE, W., and the exact volume built to match, and the washing on a line thirty miles east drying white and stiff and clean under a sky that had a volume now, and an origin field, and a buyer.

She had said no. She believed the no the way she believed a barometer that reads FAIR — as a fact about a dead instrument, as a reading she had no reason yet to doubt.

She put the stamp back in the safe, and turned the dial, and heard the tumblers drop, and then she picked up the folder to shred it and found, clipped inside the back cover where she hadn't looked, a second document — thinner, three pages, a form she knew by its number the way she knew her own name. It was a Form 15. A third-party application, standing to transfer, already typed, already bonded, already signed at the foot by KELLAN VOSS for MERIDIAN SKY, wanting only a date and a broker's processing stamp — not her *seal,* not her yes, only the flat administrative *thunk* that said a lawful application had been received and could not, by statute, be refused a hearing.

He had left her the study so she'd remember he'd been kind.

He had left her the 15 so she'd understand it wouldn't matter.

*Nothing to sign*, she thought, and the barometer needle in her chest did not drop this time, because it had already dropped, because the storm she could hear coming had a timestamp now and a volume and a basin of origin, and it was arriving on schedule, and she was standing exactly where it was going to land.


---

# Chapter 5 — Form 7: Broker's Bond (Annual Renewal)

> *The broker serves the Act, and not the parties; and the annual renewal of the bond shall be deemed a fresh affirmation that the broker holds no interest, kinship, or sympathy that would color the discharge of the office.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §2(d)

The feasibility study had sat on the corner of Della's desk for four days, where Voss had left it, and she had not thrown it away and had not read past the first page, and both of those facts were their own kind of confession, so on the fifth morning she did the thing you did when a piece of paper had started to have a smell. She filed it. She put it in the pending drawer between a bond confirmation and a deed pull, in its own gray folder, labeled in her own hand, *9-C Feas. — Meridian — DECLINED,* because a thing that was filed could not follow you around the room, and she turned the drawer key and felt a little of the four days lift off her chest, and that was when she remembered the bond was due.

It was due every year on the anniversary of her licensing, which was late spring, which was now. Form 7. The broker's bond, renewed. You paid the premium to the surety, affixed your current seal to the affidavit, swore in a paragraph of dead language that you served the Act and not the parties, and mailed it to the Authority, and for another twelve months you were the neutral filer, bonded, a gate with the county's name on it. She had renewed it twenty-two times and never once read the paragraph she swore.

She read it now. She did not know why. The morning was flat and hot already and Junie was at the ledger and the barometer read FAIR the way it had read FAIR for eleven weeks, and Della stood at the counter with the affidavit and read the paragraph she had sworn twenty-two times, *the broker holds no interest, kinship, or sympathy that would color the discharge of the office,* and the word *kinship* sat in the middle of the sentence like a stone she'd swallowed and forgotten and had just now felt go down.

She inked the pad and brought the stamp down on the affidavit. The *thunk* went up her wrist, the small honest jar, and she signed under the seal, *Della Okafor-Prine, Broker, Hale County,* the pen making the old shape, and she did not let herself think about the folder in the drawer with a farm named in it, thirty miles east, held by a woman whose kinship she had just sworn, on a bond, would color nothing.

"Filed?" Junie said, not looking up.

"Filed. It goes in the outgoing." She set it in the tray. "Twenty-two years I've sworn I've got no sympathy that colors anything. You'd think a person would read what she's swearing."

"What's it say?"

"That I'm nobody's. That's the whole job." She capped the pen. "You'll swear it too, in a year or two. Try to mean it. It's easier when you mean it."

Junie almost smiled and then didn't, because she was learning, slowly, that Della's flat sentences were the ones with the most under them, and had started to listen for the weight. That was the thing about the girl. She was still copying the record in the too-round hand, still believed a filed thing was a settled thing, but she'd begun to watch Della the way you watch a river you've been told has a channel you can't see, and Della felt the watching and did not know yet whether it was a comfort or the beginning of a debt.

The bell went. It was a Renfro hand with a signed lease application, the dairy moving an inch and a half of July rain off a section onto its milking pasture — clean, offset, lawful, a big operation feeding itself. Della verified the allotment against the deed, confirmed the bond, checked the offset, and it balanced to the third decimal, and she stamped it, the second *thunk* of the morning, and it was legal, and it was fine. The inch and a half of July would now fall on Renfro grass. The section south of the Renfro line belonged to a widow named Coyle who ran forty head on thin margin and had leased her own July out three years ago for a lump she'd already spent, and would stand in June and watch the Renfro pasture green up over a fence she could see from her kitchen, and never know the sky she didn't get had gone eight hundred yards north because a form Della stamped said it could.

Della logged it. She did not narrate the Coyle widow to herself; the narrating was a want, and you did not leave a want where the seal could reach it. But she felt the small cold thing she always felt, the flinch, and took a strange comfort in it the way she took comfort in the chip, that it was proof she was still awake — and then thought, with the clarity that came to her sometimes at the counter like a draft under a door, that a person could stay awake for twenty-two years and flinch eleven hundred times and still, the whole time, be doing the exact thing the flinch was flinching at. She had confused the flinch for a brake. It was not a brake. It was just a nerve.

"Della," Junie said. "There's a Hollis follow-on. The point-four from before — he wants to do the rest of the fallow now, the September, onto the corn. Same shape as last time." She had it up on the terminal. "Can I do this one? You watched me on the Renfro entry. It's inside-parcel, it offsets, both his."

Della came around and stood at her shoulder and looked at the screen. It was clean. It was the cleanest kind of transfer there was, a man moving his own rain off his own dying ground onto his own living ground, robbing nobody but himself, the rare mercy. There was nothing in it to learn a person the wrong lesson.

"Pull the deed first," Della said. "Always the deed first. The feed'll tell you what a man wants moved. The deed tells you whether he can. You file off the feed, someday you'll file a man's rain he sold last year and doesn't own, and it'll be your seal on it, not his mistake." She watched Junie pull the deed. "Good. Now the allotment carries?"

"Carries. Severable. Not futured." Junie's finger moved down the column. "Bond's good. Offset balances — point-four out of section eleven, point-four into section twenty-two, both Hollis." She looked up. "It's zero. It cancels."

"It cancels on paper." Della took the stamp out of the safe and set it on the counter between them, not handing it over, just setting it there, brass and worn and heavier than it looked, the way a thing you are teaching someone to hold should be set down and not passed, so the person has to reach for it and decide. "Ink the pad. Not too much. You flood the seal, it smears, and a smeared seal is a challenged seal, and a challenged seal is a hearing, and a hearing is a month of somebody's life. Light."

Junie inked the pad, light, her too-round hand careful. She set the form square. She reached for the stamp and picked it up and Della saw the small surprise in her face at the weight of it, the same surprise Della had felt at eleven when her father set it in front of her at this counter and made her reach, and Junie brought it down.

The *thunk* was Junie's now. It was a little uncertain, a little soft, but it was legitimate, it was the county saying *legal* in a new hand, and Della watched the girl's wrist take the jar and watched her not-flinch, because there was nothing in a Hollis inside-parcel transfer to flinch at. That was exactly why Della had let her have it first. You did not learn a child to swim by throwing her in the Kettle; you started her where the water couldn't take anything from anyone. And someday she would stamp a Coyle widow's sky north over a fence, and it would feel in the wrist exactly like the Hollis had felt, exactly this legitimate, exactly this light, and she would have learned by then that the wrist could not tell the difference — that the *thunk* was the same *thunk* whether it fed a man his own corn or took an inch off a woman who ran forty head on margin. That was the whole education. Della was giving it to her without a word, the way her father had given it to her: the seal does not know whose sky it is.

"Filed," Junie said, pleased, and logged it, and marked the timestamp, and Della put the stamp back in the safe and turned the dial and heard the tumblers drop and thought, *you do not leave the seal where a want can reach it,* and thought, too late as always, *and you do not leave a girl where the seal can reach her either,* and had no drawer to file that in.

The bell went a little after eleven.
