Della knew before she looked up that it was not a farmer, because a farmer opened the door slow and stood in it a second letting the cool find him, and this person came in clean and quick and shut the door against the dust in one motion, a person who did not want the county on her, and when Della looked up the woman was already reading the office — the barometer, the shelves of ledgers, the safe, the window and the elevator past it and the spent blue past that — reading it the way you read a page, fast, once, for the whole thing.

She was compact and tired-looking and wearing a jacket in the heat, zipped, as if she were cold, which in this county in late spring meant she had come from a place with a real climate and had not forgiven this one for not having one. She set a laminated Authority credential on the counter face-up without being asked, and a small brass hand-crank barometer beside it, the field kind, the kind that trusted nothing on a wall.

"Marisol Reyes," she said. "Basin Authority, field division. Hale's on Critical Watch. I'm here to observe the county's transfers through the watch period." No smile. Not unfriendly; just a person who did not spend a smile she didn't need. "You're Okafor-Prine."

"I'm the broker." Della came to the counter. "You'll want the transfer log, the standing orders, and the offset ledger for the watch period. I keep them current."

"I want to watch you keep them current." Reyes said it without edge, the way you'd state a volume. "Observers don't audit the past. We watch the present get made. If the past needs auditing, that's a different form and a different visit and you'll get the letter." She picked up her field barometer, glanced at it, set it down, and Della understood that the glance was not a habit like her own barometer-tapping; it was a reading, a real one, the woman actually took the pressure of a room she walked into, trusted the instrument in her hand over the dead one on the wall, and Della felt, unexpectedly, the pull of it — of a person in this whole apparatus who checked the live number and not the dead one.

"You keep a clean log," Reyes said. She had turned the transfer register and was reading down the morning. "Waived-fee comp, Section 12, adjacent parcel at floor. Marvin Ott. Point-oh-eight." She looked up. "You released a rounding error to a dying farm at cost and logged the waiver so it'd read clean. Why?"

"Because it was there and he needed it and the rules let me."

"The rules let you a lot of things you don't do." Reyes closed the register. It was not a compliment. It was not not a compliment. It was a fact, set on the counter beside the credential, and Della found she could not tell whether the woman had just approved of her or catalogued her, and understood, in the same beat, that Reyes could not tell either, and did not care, and would go on watching until the difference resolved into something she could enter in a column. "I'll be in and out. When Meridian files, I want to be in the room."

Della's face did not move. She had trained it for twenty-two years not to move, and it held, but somewhere under the counter her thumb had found the ledger's edge and pressed it, and she made the thumb stop. "Who says Meridian's filing."

"Nobody says. Meridian's on Critical Watch counties the way weather's on a barometer." Reyes zipped her jacket a half-inch higher, cold in the heat, homesick in her shoulders for a coast Della could see she couldn't afford to go back to. "They came to see you already. Didn't they. Man in good shoes wrong for gravel." She did not wait for the answer, which was its own way of getting it. "When they file, I want to be in the room. That's all. That's the whole visit." She took her credential and her little brass barometer off the counter. At the door she stopped and looked back, not at Della, at the wall unit with its dead needle on FAIR.

"That thing's lying to you," Reyes said, mild. "They lie when they've sat too long. A needle that hasn't moved in eleven weeks isn't reading FAIR. It's just stuck." And she went out into the blue, and the bell rang her gone, and Della stood looking at the barometer that had told her FAIR every morning of the drought, and did not tap it, for the first time in a long time did not tap it, because a woman with a live instrument had just told her the dead one was not calm. It was only stopped.

The rest of the day was ordinary and was not. She did a deed pull and a bond confirmation and turned away a futures speculator from two counties over who wanted to know what Hale's September was clearing, and she processed a lease and refused, politely and lawfully, to process a thing that was not lawful, and the whole day she felt the folder in the drawer and the word *kinship* in the affidavit she'd mailed and the sentence Reyes had said about the needle, and by the time she locked up the heat had come all the way into the office and the light off the elevator had gone the long gold that made the spent blue look, for an hour, almost like a sky that meant something.

She drove home the long way, past the Kettle, past the BOAT LAUNCH sign where somebody had wired a dead man's rowboat to the post years ago as a joke and nobody had ever taken it down, its hull gone chalk-white and split, aimed at a ramp that ran down into nothing, and she did not examine why, and at the house on Depot Street the dead lilac stood by the porch and the slack line hung between its poles, and she went in and did not turn on the light and stood in the hot dark that sat on her chest, and she thought about the phone.

She thought about it the way she thought about the drink. She knew exactly where it was and exactly what it would do. She could call Ridley Junction information and get the number in under a minute, the farm on the Ridley Fork bottomland, and a woman would answer, older-voiced now, coughing that deep cough that never left, and Della could say the six sentences that would warn her: *Meridian is coming for your rain. They want your allotment for a transfer into Hale. They filed a feasibility study with your farm named on it. I'm the broker. I said no. It won't matter that I said no.* Six sentences. She could say them and hang up and it would be, for once in her life, a thing done and not a thing filed, an interest, a kinship, a sympathy that colored the office — the exact thing she had sworn that morning, on a bond, she did not have.

She stood in the dark and did not pick up the phone, for the same reason she did not pick up the drink and did not pick up the name: because you did not, because the whole architecture of her life was the muscle of *no,* eleven years of it worn smooth, and she had built everything she was on being the woman who could hold a thing in her hand and know exactly what it would cost and set it down. And she set the phone down. She set the six sentences down in the dry channel with the name and the drink and the other things the water had left when it went.

Doss had refused himself nothing and she had refused herself everything, and she stood in her dark kitchen and felt the two of them arrive, for once, at the same counter. The not-calling had the same shape as the seal going down. The same wrist. She had just filed a thing instead of doing a thing, in her own kitchen, with no form at all, and the wrist knew it.

She poured a glass of water from the tap and drank it standing up in the dark and it tasted of the pipes and the county and the far-off idea of rain. She took the chip out of her right coat pocket and held it and turned it once, eleven years, the number gone soft under her thumb, and she did not call. It was the strongest thing she did all day. It was also the worst, and in this kitchen, tonight, she could not get a blade between the two.

The phone did not ring. She had half-expected it to, the way you expect the thing you refused to come and refuse you back. It sat on the wall being a phone, and outside the window the spent blue went to dark, and somewhere thirty miles east under a sky that still meant something a woman was bringing in washing that had dried white and stiff and clean, off a line, in the last light, under a sky that was being read, this week, in a folder in a drawer, by a company that bought basins, and by her own sister, who had sworn that morning on a bond that no kinship would color anything, and had just proven it, alone, in the dark, by saying nothing at all.


---

# Chapter 6 — Form 3-A: Allotment of Record (Prine Family)

> *Kinship confers no priority; the register shall record the parcel and not the person, and the broker shall extend to a relation no accommodation not extended to a stranger.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, Reg. 3-A.9

A Form 3-A was the smallest thing the office did and the one Della trusted most, because it invented nothing. It only certified what was already true. A man came in and asked what the record said, and the record said it, and Della pressed the seal to a copy and swore, under her bond, that the copy was the record and the record was the world. She had pulled thousands. She had never once been afraid of one until the Tuesday she pulled two.

The morning had come in the usual color. The dust had filed itself onto the sill in the night, and above the elevator the sky wore the apologetic blue it had worn for six years, and Junie was at the ledger with the good pen, and none of it knew what Della had come in early to do. She had let herself believe, driving in, that she was only being thorough. A broker who obstructs a transfer had better know exactly what she is obstructing. That was the flat true sentence she had built to walk in under. It got her to the counter and no further.

"You're early," Junie said.

"Title work," Della said. "Two certified pulls. I'll run them myself. Do the Renfro bond confirmation and leave me the deed terminal."

She waited until the girl was bent to the other screen, and then she pulled the first one.

The deed registry did not care whose name you typed. It was the fairest thing in the county for exactly that reason — it would hand you your enemy's rain or your sister's with the same three-second hum, the same green columns, the same absolute indifference. She typed the Ridley Fork bottomland, section and range, and the record came up, and there it was in the letters of the machine: the parcel, the deed, the allotment attached and intact — *Prine, C. & Prine, W., water-year allotment of record* — the acre-inches of it laid out in a column that did not know it was describing a life.

She read the volume. She had known the number since the feasibility study slid across her counter three weeks ago with Meridian's letterhead on it, but she had let herself not *look,* the way she let herself not look at the sky. Now she looked. The volume on Cass's deed and the volume Meridian needed to complete the Hale transfer were the same number to the third decimal. Of course they were. Voss did his homework. Voss had chosen the parcel the way you choose a keystone, for how much comes down when you pull it.

She printed the certified copy. The printer was old and worked the sheet out one line at a time, and she stood over it while it built her sister's rain a row of characters at a time, and she felt the old wrongness go up her wrists, and this time it did not stop at the wrists.

Then she went to the metal shelves and pulled the second one by hand, because it was too old to be in the terminal.

---

The archived ledgers went back to before the Act, brown-boarded books shelved in the cold room at the back where the safe was, and the spines were labeled in three hands — a stranger's copperplate from the county's first broker, then Doss's square print, then her own. She knew which book without looking at the dates. She had known which book for forty years. She took it down and set it on the cold-room table and it fell open the way a book falls open at the page you have opened before, and she was fourteen.

She was fourteen and it was the second dust year and she was standing exactly here, in this cold room, except the office had been warm then because the county had still had money enough to heat the back, and she was holding a lamp for her father because the light in the cold room had been out for a week and nobody was coming to fix a light in a county that was selling its sky.

Doss sat at the counter that was now hers. He was writing in the book that was now open on the table in front of Della-at-fifty-four, and Della-at-fourteen held the lamp steady the way she'd been told, because you did not shake the light on a man doing figures, and she watched her father's square hand build a column of parcels — not just the Prine allotment but the Halvorsen, the two Voss sections that were nothing to do with the Voss in the good shoes, the Ott ground he had left alone, the whole east block of families he had gone to one at a time in their kitchens and talked into selling their rain cheap to Kettle Basin Land & Rain, because it was money now and dry later and later was somebody else's problem. Her father did not talk to her while he wrote. Her father talked to her by letting her hold the light.

Upstairs, above the office, in the two rooms they lived in then, Cass was coughing.

You could hear it through the floor. It came up through the boards in a wet tearing rhythm that had been going for weeks, and Della-at-fourteen had stopped hearing it the way you stop hearing a clock, except when it changed, and it changed when it got bad, and it had been changing all that week. The doctor from the county seat had come and said the word *valley fever* and then a longer set of words about the asthma under it, and then the sentence that Della would carry the rest of her life, said not to the girls but to Doss, in the kitchen, in a voice meant to be low: *She can't take another dust season here. She has to be somewhere green or you'll bury her.* And Doss had said nothing, because Doss's grammar for love was allotments, and that was the night he had started assembling the block.

He finished the column. He totaled it. In the margin he wrote a smaller figure, set off with a rule, neat and unashamed, and Della-at-fourteen did not understand yet what the margin figure was, and Della-at-fifty-four, standing over the same page with her reading glasses on the cord and her hands older than her face, understood it completely. It was his fee. He had taken a broker's percentage on every family in the block, including his own. He had been paid for selling his neighbors' sky, and he had used the pay, all of it, to move his older daughter to a county where she could breathe. There was no line in the ledger that said any of that. The ledger recorded the parcel and not the person. Reg. 3-A.9. Her father had helped write the world she stood in.

When he was done he had blotted the page and closed the book and looked at her — at fourteen-year-old Della holding the dead lamp — and he had said the only thing he ever said to her about it, and he had said it the way he said everything, as a fact of arrangement, without cruelty, which was worse than cruelty because you could not argue with it.

"Your sister's got the bad lungs," he said. "You've got the strong ones." He took the lamp from her because she was tired of holding it and her arm had begun to shake and he had noticed, because he noticed everything, that was the terrible thing about him, he noticed everything and used none of it for mercy. "That's a piece of luck, Della. In this county, strong lungs is the only kind of luck there is. It means you can stay."

*It means you can stay.* He had said it to her as a gift. She had understood, standing there at fourteen, in the exact instant he said it, that he was going to send Cass to the green place and keep her in the dust, and that he thought this was the kind thing, and that some part of him had already done the arithmetic of his two daughters and decided which one was durable enough to be spent slowly and which one had to be spent all at once or lost. He had not chosen her to keep because he loved her more. He had chosen her to keep because she could survive being kept. She had been the strong lungs. She had been, she would understand very much later and never say aloud, the offset. He balanced the family the way he balanced a transfer, one daughter debited against the other, conservation holding, the arithmetic clean and closed.

Cass coughed again through the floor, above them, in the warm two rooms, and their father carried the lamp out of the cold room to go and tell his older daughter she was going to live.

---

Della closed the archived ledger.

She stood in the cold room with both hands flat on the cover the way Marvin stood at the counter with his hands flat either side of his hat, and she made herself breathe against the thing that had come up into her chest and sat there, the weight the body could not file its way out of. Then she carried the book out to the counter and set it beside the certified copy she had printed, and she did the thing she had come in early to do and had told herself was thoroughness.

She laid the two allotments side by side.

On the left, in her father's square print, forty years old, brown at the edges: *Prine allotment, section block, sold to Kettle Basin Land & Rain, offset receiving basin Ridley Fork.* Rain taken off Hale to put a Prine girl where she could breathe. On the right, fresh out of the old printer, still faintly warm, in the neutral letters of the machine: *Prine, C. & Prine, W., Ridley Fork,* the volume Meridian wanted, the storm they meant to pull back off Ridley and drop on Hale. Rain taken off Ridley to put a Hale county where it could breathe.

There was a step in the pull that she had not needed to take. A feasibility certification did not require the receiving side to confirm the origin record; Meridian's own study had already asserted the volume, and she was under no obligation, obstructing the transfer, to help them verify it. But a broker who slows a thing has to know its measurements exactly, and there was a colder reason under that one, which was that she wanted to hear another county say the number out loud. She picked up the phone and called the Ridley registry.

A woman answered who did not know her, which was a small unearned grace. Della gave her broker's bond number and asked for a certified read on the Ridley Fork bottomland allotment, deed-of-record, current water-year, for a pending interbasin feasibility. She heard the woman type. She heard, thirty miles down a copper wire that ran east under the dry Kettle and came up green on the other side, a keyboard entering her sister's rain into a machine in a county that still had rain.

"That's a nice old allotment," the Ridley woman said, comfortable, chatty, the way you could afford to be about weather when you had some. "Intact since apportionment. Never leased off, never bonded. You don't see many like it anymore." She read the volume. It matched the fresh copy on Della's counter and the old brown book beside it, all three of them the same number now, said in three hands and one wire. "Somebody looking to buy it?"

"Somebody's looking," Della said.

"Well, they won't get it. People on that ground don't sell. Old dairy family, I think, out on the Fork." A little laugh, kind, incurious. "Some folks just keep their rain. Anything else I can pull for you, broker?"

"No," Della said. "That's the whole of it." And she hung up before the woman could hear that it was, because the woman had just described Cass's whole life — *people on that ground don't sell* — and did not know she was reading it to the person who was going to.

The same shape. The same shape exactly, run backwards. Her father had drained Hale to fill Ridley and save Cass. Meridian was asking Della to drain Ridley to fill Hale, and the parcel they had chosen to drain was the one Doss had bought with the draining, forty years back — the green farm, the intact allotment, the sister. The rain was going home. It was going to be taken off Cass and sent back to the county it had been stolen out from under to save her, and the person the market had chosen to send it was Cass's sister, standing at Doss's counter, holding Doss's stamp, reading the record in Doss's hand. She had spent her whole life at this counter believing she was nothing like her father, that she only filed what he had chosen, and here on the counter in two hands were the two halves of the same crime, and the second half had her name on the line where the broker signs.

She heard Junie stop typing.

"Della?" A pause. "You've been in the back a while. You want coffee?"

"No." Her own voice came out level, which surprised her, the way it always surprised her that the voice held when the rest of her didn't. Competence was the last thing to go. She had learned that at fourteen with a lamp in a shaking arm. "Finish the Renfro."

But Junie had come around to the counter, the way the girl did, curious and unafraid and twenty-two, and she looked down at the two forms laid side by side before Della could turn them over, and she was a good clerk, and a good clerk reads.

"That's an old one," Junie said, looking at the brown page. "That's — is that section-block sale? From the seventies?" She leaned in, and Della watched her find the receiving basin, and watched her find the two Prines on the fresh copy beside it, and watched the girl's careful mind do what careful minds do, which is put the true thing together out of the pieces you leave lying out. "Ridley Fork on both. The old sale went *to* Ridley Fork. And this new pull comes *off* Ridley Fork. Off a — " she read the name, slow, and Della let her, because there was no way now to take the girl's eyes back off the page — "off a Prine."

"Log the Renfro bond, Junie."

"That's the same name as yours." Junie was not accusing. That was the unbearable part. She was doing what Della had trained her to do, which was to look at the record and say what it said, and the record said a thing about Della that Della had never once said in this office in twenty-two years. "Prine. That's — the sale, forty years ago, went to your family's own — " she stopped, and the color came up in her face, and she pulled back half a step, and asked the question Della had spent a lifetime arranging her whole existence so that no one would ever be positioned to ask. "The rain they want to move. Off Ridley. Is that — do you know these people?"

There was a long moment in which the office made no sound at all. The barometer held at FAIR. The dust settled on the sill. Somewhere thirty miles east a woman was hanging washing on a line that dried white and clean, above a green farm, under the intact sky her father had bought her and Meridian meant to take back, and did not know her sister was standing over the deed of it with the certified copy going cold under her hand.

Della turned the two forms face-down on the counter, the old one and the new one, one on top of the other, so their two skies lay pressed together in the dark.

"Cassandra Prine," Della said, "is my sister."

And she picked up the stamp — not to use it, only because her hand had gone to the safe for it the way it went when a thing at the counter got too large, the way a tongue goes to the tooth — and she held the brass in her fist, and she felt the county seal press its cold shape into her palm. On the counter the two forms lay face-down, one on the other, her father's crime and hers, the same sky taken twice, the arithmetic clean and closed to the third decimal, which was all the Act had ever required of anyone. The record recorded the parcel and not the person. Reg. 3-A.9. There was no line anywhere in any of these books where a person could write down that she would rather have been the one who died.


---

# Chapter 7 — Form 15: Third-Party Application (Standing to Transfer)

> *Any bonded party may apply; the broker may not refuse to receive, docket, or process a lawful application, and refusal of the party is not refusal of the transfer.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §2(f)

The application came in on a Tuesday, and it came in on paper, which was how Della knew it was serious.

The feed carried the small ones — the fence-line inches, the parcel-internal offsets, a man moving his own rain off his own fallow onto his own corn — because the small ones were nobody's fight and the machine liked to move fast where nothing was watching. But a 9-C, an interbasin, the rarest and most-litigated transfer the Act allowed, that came the old way. It came couriered, in a stiff manila envelope with a bonded seal on the flap and a return address in a city three hundred miles east, and it landed on her counter at 9:40 in the morning with a weight the feed never had, because a thing that arrives in your hand has decided to be undeniable.

Junie signed for it. The courier was a kid in a company polo, MERIDIAN SKY in the wet confident blue over his heart, and he did not know what was in it and did not care, and Della watched him pull his van out past the elevator that held no grain, and thought about how the men who built this had understood that ruin travels best when the person carrying it has no idea what it weighs.

"It's addressed to the office," Junie said. "Not to you. To the office."

"They're the same thing," Della said. "That's the trouble." She slit the flap with the letter knife her father had used for forty years and drew the document out.

It was clean. That was the first thing, the thing she felt in her wrists before she'd read a line of it — the *cleanliness* of it, the good stock and the true margins and the section numbers running down the left in a serif face that had cost somebody money. A **Form 15: Third-Party Application (Standing to Transfer)**, and stapled behind it a **9-C** already filled, already bonded, already feasibility-studied, the offset basin named, the volume named to the hundredth of an inch, and at the bottom the applicant's block, and in the applicant's block it did not say Kellan Voss.

It said **Meridian Sky Holdings, LLC, a bonded party in good standing,** applicant of record, standing under §2(f).

She read it twice. The counter was cool under her forearms. Behind her the barometer held at FAIR the way it had held at FAIR for twelve weeks, a dead needle over a dead sky, and she did not turn to look at it, but she was aware of it the way you are aware of a person who has stopped talking in a room.

Voss had said *nothing to sign.* And here was the elegance of it: he had been telling the truth. He had never needed her to sign. He had needed her to *refuse,* cleanly, on the record, so that Meridian could stop asking her nicely and start standing on the statute — and now here it was, standing, a bonded party filing a lawful application, and the whole architecture of the Act came down on her like a roof she had lived under her whole life without once looking up at the beams.

*Any bonded party may apply. The broker may not refuse to receive, docket, or process a lawful application.*

Her *no* had never been a gate. It had been a formality Meridian was gracious enough to observe until it became inconvenient. She was not the wall. She was the door, and a door's whole nature is that it opens.

"Della?"

"Docket it," she said.

Junie didn't move. "That's the Ridley one. The interbasin. The one you said no to."

"I said no to Voss. This is Meridian filing over Voss's head, which is Voss filing under a different hat, which the Act doesn't care about, because the Act doesn't know Voss has a face." She was already reaching for the docket book, the wide gray docket book that was not the ledger but its uglier cousin, the log of every application received. "A bonded party applied. I receive it. If I don't receive it, I've refused a lawful application, and refusing a lawful application is a bond violation, and a bond violation is the fastest way in the world to lose the seal — and the second I lose the seal they appoint somebody who'll stamp it before lunch." She wrote the docket number in her father's numbering scheme, the scheme she'd inherited with the office, HALE-15 and the year and the running count. "So I receive it. And then I do the only thing a door can do to a thing coming through it."

"Which is what?"

"I make it take a long time."

---

She spent the morning learning the shape of the wall she was not.

She had understood, in an abstract way, for twenty-two years, that her power was small. She had said it to Marvin last week without feeling it: *I'd be the one files it.* She felt it now. She could not stop the transfer; she had never been able to stop the transfer. What she could do — the whole arsenal of a county rain broker facing the third-largest agribusiness in the rain-poor world — was slow it. Not with a wall. With a maze.

She read the 9-C the way a person reads a thing that is going to be used against someone she loves, which is to say slowly, and looking for the flaw. And there were flaws, because there are always flaws; a document is a made thing and a made thing has seams.

The feasibility study cited a basin baseline she could requisition an update on — Reg. 9-C.4 let the broker require current data where the cited data ran more than three water-years stale, and Meridian's ran three years and a month, one month, and that one month was a note she could send back requesting refreshed hydrology, and refreshing hydrology took the Authority six to ten weeks in a year when the Authority was understaffed and captured and slow, and it was always understaffed and captured and slow. That was one.

The applicant's bond was posted, good, current — but posted by Meridian Sky Holdings, LLC, while the feasibility study was authored by Meridian Sky Acquisitions, a different subsidiary, a different tax ID, and Reg. 9-C.7 required that the applicant of record and the party of technical responsibility be *the same bonded entity or affiliated by disclosed instrument,* and the disclosure binding Holdings to Acquisitions was not in the envelope. A missing exhibit. She could reject the filing as incomplete — not refuse it, *reject it as incomplete,* the good kind of different word, the kind that meant *not no, just not yet, do it again correctly.* That was two.

And there was a bond-year irregularity, small, real, the kind of thing that would have slid past a broker who wanted it to slide: Holdings' bond had been renewed forty days ago on a schedule that suggested the old bond had lapsed for some hours in the gap, and a lapse, even of hours, even cured, was a thing the broker was *entitled to inquire into* under Reg. 7.11 before treating the party as in continuous good standing. An inquiry. A letter. A thirty-day response window she was permitted to grant and Meridian was permitted to resent and no one could call obstruction, because it was in the book, it was all in the book, she was not inventing anything, she was doing her job so exactly that her job became a barricade. That was three.

She built it the way her father had taught her to file, which was the way you set fence posts, one and then the next and the tamped dirt around each, and by noon she had three clean lawful reasons to hand Meridian's application back across the counter and make them spend six weeks picking it up again, and every one of those weeks was a week the sky stayed where it was, a week Cass hung washing under an honest thunderhead thirty miles east and did not know that thirty miles west her sister was fighting the weather with a rubber stamp and a rulebook and losing slowly enough to call it winning.

"You can do all that?" Junie was watching her build it, the three letters, the requisition, the rejection-as-incomplete, the inquiry. "It's — that's allowed?"

"Every word of it's allowed. That's the point of it." Della set the letters in a row, squared their edges. "The Act gives them a way around me and it gives me a way to make the way around me long. It's fair. It's completely fair. It just isn't fast, and I'm betting my whole hand on slow." She heard the flatness leave her voice and did not entirely stop it. "A man drowning doesn't get to be fast either, Junie. He gets to be *thorough.* You'd be amazed how long thorough can keep a person's head above the water."

Junie wrote it down. She wrote everything down; it was the best and worst thing about her. And then she asked the question she was going to keep asking for the rest of the book, the one Della had trained her to ask by answering it wrong the first time.

"Is this the public interest?" Junie said. "Or is it yours?"

Della picked up the stamp.

She hadn't meant to. Her hand had gone to the safe on its own while Junie talked, dialed the tumblers, drawn the brass out, and now it was in her fist, heavy, warm from her grip — and she was holding it over no form, there was no form to stamp, she was *rejecting* the form, refusal wore no seal — just gripping it, the way you grip a tool when the work is hard, and she caught herself doing it and did not put it down.

"The Act doesn't have a box for that question," she said. "Which is the only honest answer I've got. Log the letters and get them in the afternoon courier." She turned the stamp once in her hand, felt the ache start in her wrist, an ache that had not used to be there, that had come on sometime this year like weather coming into a house. "And Junie. Don't ever ask me that where Reyes can hear you."

---

Reyes came at four.

She came the way she came to everything, without warning and without small talk, the frosted-glass door swinging in on the flat hot light and Marisol Reyes in it, compact and tired and already cold in the dry heat, the collar of a jacket turned up in June because she was a coastal woman thirty years from a coast and her body had never forgiven the county for not being the sea. She set her hand-crank field barometer on the counter like a card played.

"You docketed a 9-C this morning," Reyes said. "Interbasin. Meridian applicant. It came up in the Authority feed at 9:52." She did not sit. Reyes never sat; sitting was for people who intended to stay a while and she never intended anything but exactly as long as the letter required. "And by three you'd sent it back three ways. Stale hydrology, incomplete affiliation, a bond-lapse inquiry." A beat. "That's a lot of book for one morning, Della."

"It's a lot of application. I read all of it."

"You read all of it fast." Reyes' eyes went over the counter, the squared letters not yet gone to the courier, the docket book still open, the stamp — the stamp still in Della's hand, and Della made herself set it down, easy, unhurried, back on the counter where a stamp lived, and knew that setting it down that carefully was worse than leaving it up. "The hydrology's three years and a month. You could've let a month slide. Most brokers would've let a month slide."

"Most brokers don't have an interbasin on their counter once in a career." Della kept her voice in the register where it lived, the flat exact register, the one that could not be caught out in a kindness. "I've got the rarest transfer in the Act being filed over my head by a bonded party I can't refuse, into a basin that isn't mine, out of a basin that isn't mine, and the Act says I process it clean or I lose the seal. So I'm processing it clean. Every seam. That's not zeal, Marisol. That's a broker doing the one job the statute left her when it took away her no."

It was true. It was completely true and she heard, under it, the other thing that was also true, and she watched Reyes hear it too, because Reyes was the one instrument in two counties that read true, a hand-crank in a room of dead wall units.

"Ridley Fork," Reyes said. "That's the offset basin. I pulled the study on my drive over." She was not accusing. She was worse than accusing; she was *asking,* forensically, the way she'd ask a gauge. "Small basin. Kept its allotments. Complacent. Good margin to bleed. And the parcel named in the offset — the one carrying the volume Meridian needs — I ran the deed on it too." She let it sit, exactly as long as the letter required. "It's held by a Prine."

The barometer held at FAIR. The dust came down on the sill. Somewhere thirty miles east a woman she would not name hung out washing that dried white and stiff and clean under a sky she still thought was hers, and here, in the cool office that smelled of paper and machine-oil, the auditor stood with the deed of that trust in her exact humorless hands and waited for Della to lie or not lie.

"Prine's a common enough name in this river system," Della said. "Web Prine's people farmed the Ridley bottomland three generations. No relation to me. Surname coincidence." Which was true — Web *was* no relation, the ugly little county irony of it — and it was the truest lie she had ever told, built entirely out of facts, and she felt it leave her mouth clean as a stamped form, and hated it, and did not take it back.

Reyes looked at her a moment longer than the letter required. That was the tell. Reyes never gave you a moment longer than the letter required unless the letter was not the thing she was reading.

"The farm's operator of record isn't a Web," Reyes said. "It's a Cassandra. Cassandra Prine. Fifty-seven." She picked her field barometer up off the counter, cranked it once, a small dry ratchet in the quiet, and read the needle and did not tell Della what it said. "You've got a Cass in your emergency contact card, Della. In your bond file. From when you renewed. Same age. I file the bond renewals. I read them. It's the job."

She let that be the whole of it. She did not say *your sister.* She did not say *conflict.* She did not say *if you are obstructing a lawful transfer to protect a relative you are no longer a neutral broker, you are a party, and a party who signs is a criminal, and I will have to be the one who finds it.* She said none of it, because Reyes did not spend words she didn't have to spend, and because the not-saying laid the whole future on the counter between them like a document filed, incomplete, awaiting an exhibit both of them knew was coming.

"Send your letters," Reyes said. "They're clean. I checked." She went to the door, and stopped with her hand on it, the flat hot light coming in around her small cold shape. "That's what worries me. They're too clean. A broker fighting for the public interest makes one good objection and lets the rest go, because she's got a county to run and no time. A broker fighting for a *person* makes three perfect ones in a morning and holds the stamp in her hand while she talks about it." She didn't turn around. "You held the stamp the whole time I was here. You didn't notice. I did."

The bell rang her out into the blue.

---

Della stood at the counter after the door had settled and did not move for a while.

The letters were squared and true. The docket book was open to HALE-15 and the running count. The stamp lay where she'd set it, brass, the county seal worn shallow, her father's forty years and her own twenty-two in the shine of it, and she looked at it the way Marvin had looked at the dead barometer, checking a thing for a life that wasn't in it, and understood that Reyes was right, and that being right was the auditor's whole tragedy and now it was going to be Della's.

She had spent the morning proving her *no* still meant something. And it did. It meant six weeks. It meant a maze, a rejection-as-incomplete, a bond inquiry, an honest thunderhead over Ridley for one more turning of the calendar. It was the best day's work she'd done in a year and it had accomplished, in the end, exactly one thing: it had taught Marisol Reyes that Della Okafor-Prine would use every clean lawful power the Act had given her to protect a person she wasn't supposed to know she loved — and would look her in the eye and call it coincidence.

Junie was watching her from the ledger, careful, unblooded, holding the good pen. "The letters," Junie said. "You still want them in the courier?"

Della picked up the stamp again, because her hand wanted the weight of it and she was tired of pretending it didn't, and this time she did not set it down. She turned it once, and the ache went up her wrist, the old wrongness that had used to live only in the animal part of her when her hand said *legal* over a piece of the sky, and had moved, this year, somewhere closer to the bone.

"Send them," she said.

She reached over and, one at a time, brought the stamp down on each of the three letters — not because a rejection needed a seal, it didn't, refusal wore no seal, there was no box on any of them that called for the county mark — she stamped them anyway, three times, *thunk* and *thunk* and *thunk,* pressing the seal into paper that had no legal use for it, because a woman has to do something with a stamp in her hand when she has just learned that the only person in the world who reads her true has read her, and there is no form yet for what comes next, and the future is already standing at the counter in a company polo with the envelope open, patient, bonded, and in good standing, waiting for her to run out of book.


---

# Chapter 8 — Form 9-C: Interbasin Reassignment (Objection Filed)

> *An objection to a proposed interbasin reassignment shall be heard by the Authority; provided that the pendency of a hearing shall not stay the delivery window, and that notice to an affected basin, once served, shall be presumed sufficient whether or not read.* — Regulations Under the Atmospheric Allotment Act, Reg. 9-C.11

The county line was a bridge, and the bridge was the tell.

Della crossed it at ten in the morning, the notice on the seat beside her in its stamped county envelope. For a quarter mile before the bridge the road ran along the top of the Kettle the way it did in Hale, over the same dead channel, the bed below the guardrail cracked and gray and holding nothing but its own memory of water. Then the bridge. Then the far side. And under the far side the channel was not dry. There was a current in it — brown, unhurried, ordinary, the most ordinary thing in the world and the strangest thing she had seen in six years — running east under the same span that ran west over dust, the same river, one side robbed and one side not, and she did not slow the truck, because slowing the truck would have been a kind of prayer, and she had put prayer in the same locked drawer as the name and the drink and did not go into it.

She had been across this line twice in eleven years. Once for the funeral, bringing her father back to be put in Hale ground. Not since. She had built a life inside a thirty-mile radius that happened to end exactly where the water started, and had told herself for a long time that this was geography.

Ridley came up green.

It came up green the way a bruise comes up, all at once, insisting on itself: the pivot circles turning honest green instead of the brown wheels of Hale, alfalfa cut and windrowed and putting a smell into the truck's vents she had forgotten a smell could be, wet and cold and mineral, the green smell before rain, except here it was just the smell of Tuesday. A public pool went by, chain-link and a diving board and actual children in it, shrieking, wet, and Della kept her eyes on the road because the children were more than she could take with a stamped envelope on the seat. Over Ridley Junction the sky was blue — but it was the other blue, the deep wet confident blue of a place that had never once had to apologize for itself, the blue on Voss's business card, and she understood, driving under it, that she had come thirty miles to serve legal notice on the color of her sister's sky.

That was the errand. It had a form number and a filing deadline and it was entirely honest, which was the worst thing about it.

The regulation required that an affected basin be served notice so its holders could be *heard.* Not warned. Heard. The men who golfed had been very careful about the difference. The notice in the envelope said a proceeding existed which might affect the recipient's rain, and that the recipient had thirty days to object to the objection, and it did not say *your sister is the broker* and it did not say *the storm they want is yours* and it did not say *run.* It said the recipient's rights would be presumed sufficiently protected whether or not the recipient read the notice.

She could have mailed it. The Act allowed service by certified mail — a stamp of a different kind, a receipt, no face. She had told Junie that affected-basin service warranted the broker's presence for the record, and Junie had written it down as though it were true, and it was true, and it was also the flimsiest lie Della had told in eleven years, because she had signed a thousand true things that were lies underneath and knew the weight of one in her own hand.

The farm was where it had always been. She had never seen it and she knew the way.

---

The washing was on the line.

She saw it before she saw the house — a line strung between two posts along the south side of the yard, the sheets and shirts and pillowslips hung out white, drying in a wind that carried water in it, stiff and bright as flags, snapping a little, the kind of white that dust had never gotten into because the dust here was held down by rain that came when it was asked. She stopped the truck at the end of the drive and sat with the engine ticking and looked at the washing and did not, for a moment, get out.

In Hale the line behind the house on Depot Street hung slack because the wash grayed before it dried, and Della brought her laundry in wet and hung it over the kitchen chairs like a woman camping in her own life. Here, thirty miles east, was the line she had been living the negative of — the same two posts, hung with the thing dust took from you first, which was the belief that you could put something clean out in the open and trust the sky to give it back to you clean. She looked at the washing and thought of nothing she would ever say out loud. Then she took the envelope off the seat and got out.

Web found her first. He came around the corner of the milk barn wiping his hands on a rag, big and slow and going gray, a bad knee in his walk, and when he saw who it was his whole tired face opened up like a man finding a tool he thought he'd lost.

"Della Prine." He said the maiden name without thinking, the way the old ones did. "Well, I'll be damned. I will be damned." He held out a hand, looked at the barn dirt on it, wiped it again and held it out anyway, and she took it, and it was warm and enormous and honestly glad, and something in her wrist that had been cold since the bridge got colder. "You come a long way."

"Web."

"Cass know you're coming?"

"No."

He absorbed that the way a decent man absorbs a thing he knows is not his to understand, and did not let it change his face. "Well. She's in. Iris too. We're just at the milking, you caught us slow." He was already turning toward the house, calling ahead, hospitable to the bone, and it occurred to Della with a small precise horror that she had come to this good man's farm to serve a paper that would put his cows on an auction truck inside two years, and that he was about to offer her coffee. "Cass! Cass, you got company you won't believe—"

The screen door opened before he finished.

---

Cass stood on the porch with a dish towel in her hands and looked at her sister across the yard, and Della looked back, and neither of them moved, and Web's voice ran down and stopped like a clock winding out.

They had the same hands. Della had made herself forget that — the Prine hands, big-knuckled, older than the face, and Cass was drying hers on the towel with them, slow, and the resemblance came across the yard like a slap you saw arriving. Cass was broader and browner, cured by an honest sun instead of a guilty one, the gray gone through her hair the same iron as Della's, and she had a stillness Della recognized because it was her own: the family stillness, the not-fidgeting that meant a Prine was holding something down.

"Della," Cass said. Flat. Not a question. Not a welcome. A fact entered in a ledger.

"Cass."

"You're not dead, then."

"No."

"Huh." Cass finished with the towel and folded it once over her forearm. "Eleven years. I'd about decided you were." She came down one step off the porch and no farther, and the one step was a whole language. "What do you want."

Web said, "Cass, for God's sake, let the woman in, she's come thirty—"

"She hasn't come to visit, Web." Cass didn't look at him. She looked at the envelope in Della's hand, and Della watched her look at it, watched her older sister's eyes drop to the county envelope and read the stamp on it upside down across forty feet of yard, because Cass had spent forty years married to a farm and knew exactly what a government envelope looked like and exactly what it meant that her sister had driven it to her by hand instead of mailing it. "She's come with a paper." Cass's mouth did something that was not a smile. "You always did like a paper. Even when you were little. Doss'd give you the forms to sort while he worked and you'd sort them for hours, happy as anything. The rest of us are down here with the actual weather and Della's inside with the paperwork of it."

"It's a notice," Della said. She heard her own voice go flat and exact — the office voice, the one she used at the counter to say the true thing and give a person nothing softer to hold — and she hated that it was the voice that came, and it came. "There's a proceeding. It affects the Ridley Fork basin. The Act requires I serve the holders of record. You and Web hold the Fork bottomland. I'm serving you notice."

"Serving us." Cass tasted it. "That's the broker talking, is it. Not the sister. The broker."

"They're the same person, Cass. That's the—" Della stopped. She had been about to say *that's the problem,* and did not, because she had a discipline about that too. "That's how it works. When it touches somebody, I'm still the one who files it. There isn't a version where I get to only be one of the two."

"There was a version." Cass's voice didn't rise. It never had. It just found the bone and pressed. "Forty years ago there was a version where you weren't the broker. Then Doss made you the broker, and you let him, and here you are on my porch with a stamp on an envelope. It is the *same* envelope, Della. It has always been the same envelope. Your whole life is that one envelope getting handed down the family."

Behind Cass the screen door opened again and a young woman came out — wiry, quick, a phone in one hand, hair up off her neck, the Prine hands on her too, three generations of them now on one porch, and she took in the truck and the envelope and her mother's back and read the whole scene in a second the way the young read everything.

"That's Iris," Web said into the silence, helplessly, because somebody had to say a name. "Our daughter. Iris, this is your aunt Della."

Iris did not say hello. She looked at Della with an interest so bare and so unfriendly it was almost a courtesy, the look of a person who has been reading about you and has now been handed the animal itself.

---

Della held out the envelope.

Cass did not take it. She looked at it, held out across the last ten feet of yard, the county seal on the flap, Della's own bonded signature under the address window — and she folded her arms over the dish towel and did not take it, and the washing snapped on the line behind her, white, and the wet blue held overhead, and a long time seemed to pass in which the only motion in the world was the sheets and the brown current under the bridge two miles back that Della could not see and could not stop hearing.

"Set it on the rail," Cass said finally. "I'm not going to hand-take a thing from you like we're friendly. Set it on the rail and it's served, isn't that right? Isn't that the rule? Served whether or not I read it." She knew it in her body, forty years of forms. "You people wrote it real careful. I don't even have to read it and you're clean."

"You should read it," Della said.

It was the closest she came. It was the whole of it. *You should read it* — three words, and behind them the warning she had driven thirty miles under two skies to deliver and could not: the storm they wanted was this line, this white washing, the exact volume of the sky over their heads. She could not say it. She had signed a thousand things she could not say. She set the envelope on the porch rail where Cass told her to, and it was served, and the Act was satisfied, and the sheets snapped.

Cass looked at the envelope on the rail. Then she looked at her sister, and for one moment — one moment Della would take home and lay in the dry channel with the other things — something moved under the contempt that was not contempt, some old durable exhausted thing, sister-shaped, and Cass opened her mouth.

"He shouldn't have—" she started.

And stopped. And closed it. And the contempt came back down over her face like the flat blue coming down over a spent basin, on schedule, and whatever she had been about to say went back to wherever Cass kept it, which was the same place Della kept the name, and neither of them would ever know if it had been the same sentence.

"Drive safe," Cass said. "It's a long way back to the dry." She turned and went up the steps and did not take the envelope with her, and the screen door shut, and that was the reunion, and it was ash, and Della had known it would be ash and had come anyway, which was the part she would have to look at later and did not look at now.

Web stood in the yard with his big glad ruined face and understood none of it and said, "Della — you sure you won't take some coffee—" and she said no, Web, thank you, and took the truth of his kindness full in the chest like the heat that came into the house at night, and turned for the truck.

Iris walked her to it.

---

She fell in beside Della without being asked, down the drive, quick short steps to Della's long ones, the phone still in her hand, and she didn't speak until they were at the truck and out of earshot of the porch, and then she leaned one hip against the fender the way the young lean on things they don't yet know are load-bearing, and she said:

"I've been reading."

Della had her hand on the door handle. She left it there.

"The registers," Iris said. "The old ones. Anybody can pull them — file a records request and it's all just sitting there in the county archive being true, everything bought and sold and severed and futured, back to the seventies." She wasn't hostile now. That was worse. She had gone past hostile into something clinical, the clarity of a person who has done the reading and cannot un-know it. "Hale sold its sky. Back in the seventies, a block of allotments off the east sections, severed in perpetuity, sold cheap to an outfit called Kettle Basin Land and Rain. Whole county's future, gone in eighteen months, for money-now." She looked at the wet blue over the farm, then at her aunt. "You know who Kettle Basin Land and Rain is now? You do, don't you. You know exactly."

"Iris."

"They changed the name. That's all they did. Changed the name and kept the deeds." Iris pushed off the fender and stood square. "And here's the part I can't get past. Somebody signed all that. Every one of those severances, forever — a broker signed them, put a seal on them. You can't sell a county's rain without the broker's seal, it says so in the Act, section two-A, I read that too." She tilted her head. "So I went looking for who the Hale broker was back then. Whose seal is on the paper that dried out your whole county before my mother was thirty." She let it sit exactly as long as Della would have let it sit at the counter. "And I keep coming up with the same last name. Which is your last name. Which is my last name. Prine."

The washing snapped on the line behind the house.

"You're the broker," Iris said. Not cruel. Cruel would have been survivable. She said it the way you name a species. "Whatever this is you drove out here to put on our porch rail — you're the one who signs it. Same as him. Same seal, probably. Did you inherit the actual stamp?" She saw the answer in the thing Della's face did not do, and something in her went very still and very old for twenty-six. "You did. You've got his stamp in that office. You're the one who signs it."

Della opened the truck door.

"Read the notice," she said. "Make your mother read it. There's thirty days on it." It was all she had. She got in.

Iris stepped back from the fender. She didn't say goodbye either. She stood in the green yard with her phone in her hand and the whole shape of it already assembled behind her eyes, and Della understood, backing the truck around, that the young woman had done in a records request what the sisters had not done in forty years, which was to say the thing out loud — and that the thing was loose in the world now, could not be un-said, could not be sealed back into the drawer, could not revert.

She drove out under the wet blue and came to the bridge and crossed it, and under the span the brown current ran east on the Ridley side and the dead channel lay gray on the Hale side, the same river, and halfway across, at the exact seam where the water quit, the sky changed color over the windshield — deep wet blue behind her, flat apologetic blue ahead, the line between them as clean and as scheduled as a delivery. She drove into the apologizing half of it, toward home, the notice served and the words unsaid and her sister's daughter's voice going around in her chest the way the chip went around under her thumb, *you're the one who signs it,* and she left the chip in her pocket and drove with both hands on the wheel and did not reach for it once, which cost her the whole way home. Forty feet behind her, on a porch rail in the green county, a stamped envelope sat unopened in the wind — served, sufficient whether or not read — and the washing on the line went on drying white, for now, for a little while longer, clean.


---

# Chapter 9 — Form 22: Emergency Transfer (Expedited)

> *Upon a finding of emergency, the Authority may expedite any pending reassignment and abridge the objection period; provided that the broker's seal shall remain required, and the emergency shall be presumed to justify the transfer.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §11(a)

The heat came into Ainsley Hale on the third day and did not leave, and by the fourth day it had a shape.

You could see it from the Larkin overlook where the boat ramp went down into pan — a lid, low and gray-white and motionless, pressing the valley flat under it like a hand on a thing it means to keep still. The old men at the Trestle had a word the trade sheets used too: a dome. Della had read about domes in the national pages the way she read about monsoons in Bengal, distant weather that happened to other basins. Now it sat on hers. It did not move because nothing moved it; there was nothing left in the Hale sky to push it, only the flat spent apologetic blue underneath the gray, doubly apologizing now.

She did not look at it long. She looked at the terminal.

The feed had gone strange in the night. Not busy — strange. All week the market had been a slow thing, an inch here off a fallow section, the ordinary retail cruelty of a Tuesday. Now the screen was full of a single word repeated down the Hale column in the flat green type the machine used for everything, weddings and evictions alike: **EMERGENCY. EMERGENCY.**

"What is that," Junie said. She had come in and not taken her coat off, which meant she'd felt the lid too, the way you feel a low ceiling in a room you knew was tall.

"Crop-failure petitions," Della said. "Filed overnight. Someone's been busy."

She scrolled. The petitions were clean, each a small honest disaster properly documented: the Renfro dairy, a corn block east of the elevator, the Coyle widow's forty head, section after section of Hale ground filing under §11 for a finding of emergency — because a dome that didn't move meant a crop that didn't make, meant cattle off water in the heat, meant, the form had a box for it, *imminent basin distress affecting habitability.* Each was true. Each was signed by a real cracked hand. And each, filed alone, would have sat in the queue its thirty days like everything else.

They had not been filed alone. Someone had filed forty of them in a night, together, so that the Authority, opening at eight, would find not a farmer but a *county* declaring emergency — and a county in emergency woke §11, and §11 was a different animal. It let them cut the thirty days she had bought Ridley, the whole of the warning in the envelope on Cass's porch rail, down to a week. To nothing. The men who golfed had built a fire door into the Act, and someone had found it and set the county on fire to open it.

She knew whose hand had gathered forty farmers into a night's filing. It was not a farmer's hand. Farmers came one at a time, turning a hat.

The bell went.

"Della." Marvin Ott stood in the door with his hat already in his hands, the gray lid hanging over the elevator behind him, and she saw at once that today he had not come to ask for eight-hundredths of an inch. He had come with the emergency on him like the dust. He set the hat on the counter and put both hands flat and did not do the slow work of the brim, and that stillness was worse than the turning. "My alfalfa's laying down."

"Sit, Marvin."

"I won't sit." He said it gently. "If I sit I'll not get up and I got the cows to see to. Four days of this and it's laying flat like something walked on it. The point-oh-eight you gave me — I put it on. Came up green a week and now it's down with the rest, and there's not another drop to lease at any price, because there's no July in this basin. It's this." He tipped his gray head at the window without looking. "That thing sitting on us."

"I know."

"You know." Something came into his voice that had never been in it at her counter, not in three years of hat-turning, and it took her a moment to name it because it was so far from the weather-flat register the men here used, and then she named it and it went through her like the *thunk* went up her wrist. It was pleading. "Della. There's a company. The whole county knows it now — Meridian, the outfit that's been asking after the east block. Prewitt told the Renfro boy they filed something big, that they can bring rain back, real rain, a season of it, enough to stand this valley back up — and all it wants is your seal. Your seal, Della. You."

There it was.

She had known it was coming the way she'd known the card was Meridian before she'd turned it over — not the words, only the size. But knowing the size of a thing does not prepare you for its face, and its face was Marvin Ott, dust to the eyebrows, the last honest field in Hale laying down behind him, begging her — the word was begging — to take the rain off a green county thirty miles east where a woman with the same hands as Della's hung washing on a line.

"You'd be the one signs it," Marvin said. "You told me that yourself, back in the spring. Well, it's come, hasn't it. It's real, and I'm asking you to file it. I'm asking you to save my field."

"Marvin—"

"I know what you told me." His big hands had begun to move on the brim, slow, the old work coming back to him because his mouth was doing a thing his hands didn't know how to help with. "You said it's a bill somebody hasn't been handed yet. I heard you. I laid there thinking about the fella at the other elevator." He looked up and his eyes were wet and it was not weather. "And I'm sorry, Della. God help me. I looked at my field laying down and I thought, *let it be him.* I've held this county's bill my whole life and I'm tired, and I wanted it to be somebody else's turn. That's the kind of man four days of this makes. I'm asking you to make it somebody else's turn."

The office was very quiet. Junie had stopped at the ledger. Outside the lid pressed down and the sky under it apologized.

Della felt the sobriety chip through the coat on the hook, the small round weight of eleven years, and she did not touch it, which was its own kind of touching, and she understood with a cold exactness what they had built the emergency for. Not to make her sign. To make *Marvin* ask her to. Voss did not need her yes; he had a form for going around it. What he needed was a face on the refusal — Marvin's alfalfa laying down flatter each day she held, until the holding was the cruelty and the signing the mercy, robbing Ridley turned inside out like a coat and worn as a rescue, and the proof standing at her counter turning his hat.

"I can't make the price," she said, and heard the flat exact voice come, the office voice, filed down past feeling, and let it come because it was all she had. "I've told you that a hundred times. But this one's different. This one I *can* make — that's the trap in it. I could reach out and green your field back up before Sunday, and I'm going to tell you no, and be the one thing standing between you and your rain, and you're going to walk out and hate me. And you'll be right to."

"Then why." It was not a shout. Marvin Ott had never shouted in his life. It was worse than a shout. "Why, if you can. My field's real, Della. It's laying down *right now.* You put the fella at the other elevator up against my field and I can't see him. I can't see his face and I can see mine."

"Because I can see his," Della said.

She had not meant to say it. It came up out of her the way the drink used to come up, unbidden and exact, and once it was in the air she could not put it back, and Junie's pen had stopped and the emergency scrolled EMERGENCY down the screen at her elbow, and she stood very still, the family stillness, holding it down, and did not say the rest. Did not say *it's my sister's face.* She had a discipline about the name the way she had a discipline about the drink. You did not pick it up in front of a dying man and use it to explain why he had to keep dying.

"You know somebody over there," Marvin said slowly. He had been reading weather off her face for three years. "In Ridley. The place they'd pull it from. You *know* somebody."

"Everybody knows somebody, Marvin. There's no basin far enough away that you don't." She reached for the true thing, the general thing, the thing that was not the name. "Meridian's gift to Hale is a bill made out to a county that's done nothing to us but keep its own sky. If I sign it, your field greens up and by August theirs is laying down, and there's a Marvin over there turning his hat at *their* broker, asking her to make it somebody else's turn. And she'll have somewhere to send it. And it'll be us again. That's not a rescue. That's a wheel."

Marvin took that in; taking hard true things in was most of what his life had made him. But his field was laying down, and the true thing did not water it, and she watched him weigh the wheel against the alfalfa and watched the alfalfa win, because it was his and it was real and it was dying. She could not even hold it against him. That was the worst of it.

"Prewitt says they can shorten the whole thing with the emergency finding," Marvin said quietly. "Says it could come through in days if the broker signs. Days, Della. My field's got days." He picked up his hat. "You're telling me my field dies so a field I can't see stays green. I'm too tired to argue it, and I know Doss'd — " He stopped himself on the name, because he'd seen, three years running, that Doss's name shut Della's face, and he was a courteous man even ruined. "I'll go see to the cows."

"Marvin."

He waited, hat in hand.

"When it comes through," Della said, "and it will — they've built it so my signing only decides who signs it — it won't be me that saved your field. Hear it from me straight now. If your field greens up this summer, it's because somebody thirty miles east lost theirs, and I said no, and the machine did it over my no. Don't thank me for it. I'll have spent every day between now and then trying to stop the exact thing that saved you." She held his eyes. "That's the only honest thing I've got, and it won't water a single row. Take it anyway."

Marvin looked at her a long moment under the gray lid, and something in his ruined courteous face went gentle and terrible at once, the look of a man who has understood more than he wanted to.

"You're carrying a bill too," he said. "Aren't you. Carried one this whole time and never handed it to a soul." He put the hat on. "Ground's been asking a while now. Reckon we're all of us going to quit asking here directly." And he went out into the heat, and the bell rang him gone, and through the frosted glass she watched his stooped shape cross under the apologizing sky toward his truck, a man going to see to cows he was going to lose.

Junie had not moved. When she spoke her voice was careful, the careful of a person laying a foot on ice she isn't sure of.

"Della. If it's going to come through anyway — if your seal or not doesn't stop it — then why not sign it? Marvin's field would live. If it happens either way, at least it'd — at least he'd get the season out of it."

There it was again, from the other side of the counter now, the same argument in a younger mouth, and Della heard her own thirty-two-year-old self in it so clearly that she gripped the edge of the counter with the Prine hands until it steadied.

"Because it doesn't happen either way," she said. "That's what they want you to believe — that it's already decided, that your no is just a delay and delay is just cruelty, so you might as well have the fee and the clean conscience of *at least somebody I know got helped.* That's the whole machine, in your mouth, at twenty-two." She made herself gentle it, because the girl had asked in good faith, because the girl was the price of every wrong thing Della was about to be tempted into, made visible. "Nothing happens anyway. Somebody's hand does it. As long as it has to be a hand, the hand matters. The day you decide it doesn't is the day you become the kind of broker they can build an emergency around." She turned back to the screen. "Log Marvin's petition with the rest. And Junie — don't ask me that again. Not because it's wrong to ask. Because you already know the answer, and asking it out loud is how you practice not knowing."

The bell went a fourth time, and she did not need to look up to know the shoes would be wrong for gravel.

Kellan Voss came in out of the heat unhurried, in a pale summer suit that had not one grain of dust on it, which was its own small miracle in Hale and its own small insult, and he wore no hat because men like Voss had never once needed to keep the sun off, and he smiled at Della the way you smile at a colleague you respect, warm, sorry, correct.

"Della. I won't stay. I saw Marvin Ott going to his truck just now — the Ott place off the south road, four generations." He said it without a note of threat, the way a man recites a thing to prove he cares, and it was worse than a threat, it was homework. "His last field's laying down. I genuinely hate that. There's no version of what I do where I enjoy watching a man's field die when I've got the water to stand it up." He set nothing on the counter; he had learned, since the study, not to. "The Authority made the emergency finding this morning. You'll have the paper by end of day. I'm not here to push. I just think you deserve to hear it from a person: the window's going to open, and open fast, and the only question left is whether it opens with your seal on it or without. And I'd so much rather it were with. Not for me. For you. So that when Marvin's field greens up, it's your hand that did it, and not some out-of-county broker the Authority sends when yours won't serve."

"It won't be with," Della said.

"I know." Voss looked at her, and for just a moment — one moment she would take home and lay in the dry channel with the other things — something moved under the warmth that was not warmth, some flat old accounting, the look of a man reading a number he already knew. "You keep saying no. I admire it. It's a real thing you're doing." He turned for the door, and paused, and delivered the last of it over his shoulder, gently, remembering everything. "But I've been doing this a long time, and I'll tell you what I've learned. A no is a door you're holding shut with your body. It works right up until the building's on fire and the people inside would rather you moved." He tipped his head toward the window, toward the gray lid, toward Marvin's dust settling on the road. "Doesn't it feel warm to you?"

He went out into the heat, and the bell settled, and did not ring for a long time.

Della stood at the counter. On the screen the word scrolled down the Hale column, EMERGENCY, EMERGENCY, and outside the lid did not move because nothing moved it, and she reached out and tapped the barometer on the wall the way she tapped it every day, out of the habit older than hope, checking a dead thing for a pulse.

The needle sat on FAIR.

She tapped it again, harder, the way you're not supposed to, and the brass case rang under her knuckle, and the needle held on FAIR, dead, stuck, saying *fair* over a county under a dome, the most honest liar in the office. She stood with her hand still raised to it and understood that the storm she could feel coming was not going to move that needle, because it was not a storm. It was a form. It would arrive on schedule, punctual as an eviction, and the instrument built to feel weather coming would sit on FAIR through the whole of it, because there was no needle on any wall in the county that fell for paper.

She took her hand down.

"Junie," she said. "When the emergency paper comes, don't process it. Set it on my desk. I want to read every word of what they've built before it touches this counter." She was already reaching for the safe, for the day's certified copies, because her hands knowing what to do was the last discipline she had. "And put the fan on. It's going to be a long summer."

Junie went to the window unit and pressed the switch and nothing happened, and pressed it again, and the old machine shuddered and blew a breath of hot valley air into the room that was not one degree cooler than the air already in it, only moving, and the two of them stood in it — the woman and the girl, the ledger open between them — while the fan pushed the heat around and around the office in a circle it could not break, and outside the sky went on being sorry, and somewhere in the district office a clerk under a golfer's instruction was stamping a finding that would abridge a sister's thirty days down to nothing, and the barometer held on FAIR, and held on FAIR, and held.


---

# Chapter 10 — Form 3-A: Allotment of Record (Kettle Basin Land & Rain)

> *A successor entity, however renamed, reorganized, or acquired, assumes all rights, allotments, and severances of its predecessor; and the register shall recite the chain without regard to the number of the names.* — Regulations Under the Atmospheric Allotment Act, Reg. 3-A.14

The girl had left a thing loose in the world and Della came in Monday to run it down.

She had not slept, which was ordinary, and had not drunk, which was the point. She had lain in the hot dark with Iris's voice going around under her thumb — *you're the one who signs it, did you inherit the actual stamp* — and somewhere past two in the morning the question had turned itself around in her chest the way a form turns around when you have been sorting it too long and suddenly read it upside down and see what it actually says. Iris had asked *who signed.* That was the daughter's question, the young one's, the one that wanted a name to hate. Della lay in the dark and found she was asking the other one, the broker's, the one nobody had thought to ask in forty years because the answer had always been sitting in the archive being true.

*Who bought.*

She was at the counter before Junie, which almost never happened, and had the archive key out and the metal shelf pulled and three feet of the old ledger's weight on the counter before the coffee had finished. Junie came in at eight to find her aunt-that-wasn't standing over the old block-sale years with her reading glasses down on her nose and a legal pad going, and had the sense the girl was getting to say nothing and pour two cups.

"What are we pulling," Junie said, setting one down. Not *what am I helping with.* She had learned that much.

"A chain of title." Della didn't look up. "The block sale. The east sections, the years it all went upstream. I want the buyer of record on every line, and I want every place the name changes after."

"That's a records request kind of a job." Junie meant it as an offer.

"It's a broker verifying a successor entity ahead of a pending nine-C. It's Reg. 3-A.14, and it's mine, and it's on the clock." She said it flat and it was true and it was also the flimsiest kind of cover, the kind she had spent a lifetime learning to file smooth, because a broker did not, in the ordinary run, pull forty years of a dead man's ledger to find out who her enemy's grandfather had been. "Log it as due diligence on the Meridian application. If Reyes asks, that's what it is."

"Is it not what it is?"

"Log it, Junie."

---

The ledger was her father's for the first stretch, and reading it was a thing she did at a slight remove from her own hands, the way you watch your hands do a task you have done ten thousand times and no longer have to be present for. Doss's column-hand was smaller than hers and squarer, a hand that had never once run a line crooked or blotted a decimal, a hand that believed, the way Junie's round careful one still believed, that the record was a moral object and not just an accurate one. She turned the wide stiff pages and the dust that had been sealed in the gutter for forty years came up gray and got in her teeth, the same dust, the county filing itself down even in the book of its own filing.

There it was. She had seen it before, in the spring, when the title dispute pulled her into these pages and she had not looked long. She looked long now.

*Block sale, east sections, Hale.* Line after line of it, deed-references she half knew, family names she wholly knew — Webber, Coyle, Renfro, Ott struck through and left un-sold, a dozen more — allotments severed in perpetuity, the plus and the minus meeting clean at the third decimal on every line, conservation holding, the sky's arithmetic closed and honest. And in the buyer column, on every line, in her father's square exact hand, the same four words:

**KETTLE BASIN LAND & RAIN.**

And ruled off neat in the margin of the block, the way you'd note a thing you were proud of having gotten right, Doss's broker's commission, to the third decimal, on the whole block, his own family's allotment included, because the Act did not have a box for a man declining to profit from his own ruin and Doss had not been a man to leave a lawful box unfilled.

She knew all that. That was the sin, and she had made a discipline of not looking at it, and Iris had said it out loud on a green porch and now it was loose. But she was not here for the sin this morning. She was here for the buyer. She put her finger under KETTLE BASIN LAND & RAIN and she started walking it forward, year by year, the way you walk a debtor forward, page by page, watching a name for the place it changes.

She was fourteen the year the block closed.
