She let herself have that, once, cold, at the counter, because it was true and load-bearing: she had been fourteen, and she had sat on the high stool at the end of this same counter sorting the copies into the pile that went to the buyer and the pile that stayed in the county, and the buyer's pile had been the fat one, and her father had let her lick the stamp pad brush and had said, without looking up from the column, *neat pile, Del, that's the whole trade, a neat pile and the seal square,* and she had been proud, God help her, she had been proud of the fat neat pile of her county's future going out the door to a company called Kettle Basin Land & Rain, and she had not known one single thing about what she was sorting except that she was good at it and her father had said so.

She set that down. She was good at setting things down. It was the same muscle as the chip.

---

The name changed in the eighties, the way Iris had said. It did not vanish; it *succeeded.* That was the horror of the register — nothing was ever undone, only carried forward under a better name.

Kettle Basin Land & Rain became, on a Reg. 3-A.14 filing dated to a decade she made herself not anchor, **Kettle Basin Holdings.** Same deeds, same severances, same allotments, a new name ruled in above the old one and the old one not struck out but *carried,* because the Act required the register to recite the chain without regard to the number of the names. Kettle Basin Holdings became, two pages and some years on, a thing called **Larkin Water Partners** — and she stopped, at that, with her finger on the line, because Larkin was the reservoir, Larkin was the cracked gray pan out past the BOAT LAUNCH sign, Larkin was the boat ramp that ended in dust, and some man in an office had taken the name of the dead water off her county's map and hung it on the company that had killed it, and had thought, presumably, that it sounded wet. Larkin Water Partners became **Meridian Kettle, LLC.** Meridian Kettle became, on the most recent filing in the book, in a hand that was neither her father's nor hers but a clerk's she didn't know, contract work from the district office, four cleaner words than her father's:

**MERIDIAN SKY HOLDINGS.**

The same deeds. The same severances. The same rain, off the same sections, running now under a name Della had first read three weeks ago on a cream card slid under her door in the night, in a wet confident blue.

She sat with it.

It was not a surprise. Iris had handed it to her on the porch — *they changed the name, that's all they did.* Reyes had all but named it, running the affiliation of Holdings to Acquisitions on the Form 15. Della had known it the way you know a shape on the horizon before it has a name. But knowing a thing from a green porch and finding it in your father's own hand, walked forward line by line under your own finger, forty years of a company changing its clothes and keeping its deeds, was a different weight, and it went up her wrist the way the stamp went up her wrist, an ache closer to the bone than it had been in the spring.

The company buying Hale's future was the company that had bought its past. The men who had come, patient and pressed and sorry, to green up her valley in exchange for her sister's sky — they were the great-grandchildren of the men who had made her sister's sky necessary in the first place, who had bought Cass's escape and Hale's ruin in the same eighteen months, off the same fat neat pile a fourteen-year-old had sorted proud. It was not a coincidence and it was not a haunting. It was a business. It had simply never sold. Once severed, it shall not revert. The rain had left Hale forty years ago and gone into a company, and the company had changed its name four times and never once let go of the deed, and now the company had circled back around to the same county, the same family, the same counter, to complete a thing it had started when Della was fourteen and licking a stamp brush.

"Della." Junie was at her shoulder. She had not heard the girl come around the counter. "You've been on that page twenty minutes."

"I'm reading the chain."

"I can see the chain from here." Junie's round hand came down on the page, one finger on MERIDIAN SKY HOLDINGS, one on KETTLE BASIN LAND & RAIN, four inches and forty years apart. "It's the same outfit. The one Iris said. The one that bought us out is the one that wants back in." She said it the way the young said the thing the old had made a career of not saying. "Why would they, though? That's what I don't— they already own the deeds. They already took the rain. Why come back and *give* it to us? Why buy back the east block and green up Larkin and hand Hale a whole season, when they could just sit on what they've got and let us dry?"

And there it was, out of a twenty-two-year-old's mouth, the question Della had been circling since two in the morning without letting herself land on it, because landing on it changed the shape of the choice she had been telling herself she'd already refused.

"That's the right question," Della said slowly. "That's the only question." She took her glasses off and pressed the bridge of her nose, and the dust was in her teeth and the barometer on the wall held at FAIR and the sky through the window did its apologizing, and she made herself walk the arithmetic out loud, once, because Junie was the only person in two counties she could walk it in front of and because saying it to the girl was the closest she would let herself come to praying. "Meridian doesn't do favors. There's no line in the ledger for a favor. So the deluge isn't a favor. If they're offering to give Hale a season, it's because the season *earns* them something worth more than the rain."

"Worth more than a whole valley's rain."

"Worth more than a whole valley's rain." Della looked at the four names walking down the page. "Follow the shape of it. To green up Hale they have to pull the storm off Ridley. Fine. That's the nine-C, that's the offset, that's what they've said out loud. But an offset that size doesn't just wet Hale and dim Ridley one shade. It pulls Ridley *down.*" She was seeing it now, the way she saw a delivery coming across a field, arriving on schedule, and she hated the punctuality of it. "It pulls Ridley toward the floor. And a basin at the floor—"

She stopped. Junie waited, and did not fill it, and Della understood the girl already had it, had had it before she'd asked, had asked the question to make Della say the answer, which was a thing Della herself did at this counter, and she felt the small cold pride and the sick under it of watching the apprentice learn the trade she was learning.

"A basin at the floor goes critical," Junie said. "And critical allotments sell cheap. Because the holders panic, and the Authority compels a remedy, and the only buyer with the money is—"

"Is the outfit that engineered the drought." Della closed the ledger. The sound of it was not the *thunk* of the stamp; it was softer and worse, a book of forty years shutting. "They don't want to save Hale, Junie. Saving Hale is the receipt. They want Ridley. They've always wanted Ridley — Ridley's the wet one, Ridley's the one still worth taking. The deluge for Hale is the honest-looking front. It's the thing they can say to a grange hall full of scared farmers so the county thanks them for the drought that comes after. Buy the sick county's gratitude, use it to gut the healthy one, buy the healthy one's collapse at floor price, and move on to the next basin wetter than the last." She heard her own voice go flat and exact, the office voice, the one that stated a thing without dressing it. "It's the same play they ran on us. Buy low on a dying basin. Only forty years ago the dying basin was Hale, and this time the dying basin is going to be Ridley, and they need my seal on the nine-C to start it drying."

The office was very quiet. The feed murmured to itself. Somewhere south of the highway a truck that hadn't been paid off yet went by on the county road, and the sound of it came in through the closed window with the dust.

"You can't prove any of that," Junie said. Not a challenge. A clerk stating the state of the file.

"No," Della said. "I can't prove a word of it. I can read a chain of title and I can do arithmetic and I can smell it, and none of those things is a defendant. It's the buy-low. It's the oldest thing in this book." She put her hand flat on the closed ledger, on the cover Doss's hand and hers had both worn shiny, and left it there. "But I know its handwriting. I've been reading it since I was fourteen."

---

The bell went at eleven and it was not a farmer.

It was a courier from the district office with a thin envelope for the broker's signature, and Della signed for it standing at the counter with the ledger still out and the four names still walking down the open page where Junie hadn't yet reshelved it, and she slit the envelope and read the single sheet inside twice, and set it down on the counter face-up next to the ledger, and the two documents lay there together in the flat light.

It was a routine thing, and that was the worst of it. A notice of amended application, filed by Meridian Sky Holdings, LLC — a change to the filed offset schedule, a bond figure revised, a delivery window moved, the whole of it as dry and forward-facing as a change of address. Nothing on it said *sink Ridley.* Nothing on it ever would. It advised the county broker, in the flat courtesy the men who golfed reserved for the people they were dismantling, that the applicant had refined the volume of the pending 9-C offset, and it was signed, in a fountain-pen hand as neat as a ledger, KELLAN VOSS. It was a notice that a lawful thing was proceeding lawfully. It proved nothing. It was, she thought, exactly the kind of paper you'd send if you had a second thing hidden inside the volume and wanted the record to show only the first.

She read the revised volume twice and could not, from the number alone, tell how far past Hale's need it ran — you could not read *intent* off an acre-inch, and Voss knew it, and had counted on it — but the arithmetic she had walked out loud for Junie sat up off the page anyway and would not lie back down. A company that only wanted Hale green did not refine its volume *up.* She had no proof of that. She had a suspicion with her father's handwriting under it.

She put the notice down on the open ledger, on the exact line where her father's square hand had written KETTLE BASIN LAND & RAIN forty years before, so that the wet-blue-inked letterhead of Meridian Sky lay across the faded four words of its own great-grandfather, one document on the other, the same deeds, the same sections, the same family on both ends of it — and she stood and looked at the two of them stacked, and did not touch the stamp, which was its own kind of touching.

"Junie," she said. Her voice was very level. "Get me the Ridley Fork holder-of-record card. The bottomland parcel. I need the current allotment volume and the floor threshold for the Ridley Fork basin, and I need to know the exact number of acre-inches between the volume they've just filed and the number that takes Ridley critical. I want to see how much daylight they left themselves."

Junie's hand had already gone to the terminal. Then it stopped. The girl looked at the notice on the ledger, and looked at Della, and asked the question the daughter on the porch had asked, and the apprentice had every right to ask, and Della had no version of herself left that got to only be one of the two people who could answer it.

"The bottomland parcel," Junie said carefully. "Ridley Fork. That's—" She had learned the name in the spring, at this counter, from Della's own mouth. "That's your sister's ground."

"Yes," Della said.

"The number between what they want to pull and critical." Junie's round careful hand hovered over the keys, not typing, understanding all the way down now, twenty-two and learning what the trade actually was. "That's the number that's how much rain my—" she stopped, corrected herself to the county register's cold grammar, because she was a clerk and it was in her now— "how much rain the holder of record has left before her ground's an evacuation."

"That's the number," Della said. "Pull the card."

And Junie, who believed in the Act the way you believe in a thing that hasn't burned you yet, put her fingers on the keys and began, in her round and careful and still-honest hand, to type out the acre-inches between Cass and the floor.


---

# Chapter 11 — Form 31: Report of Suspected Diversion (Storm Theft)

> *Any imbalance not accounted for shall be reported to the Authority within seventy-two hours of discovery; failure to report shall itself constitute a diversion.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §13(b)

The imbalance came into the office the way the dust did — while Della slept, along the floor, under the door she'd sealed against everything but this. It was on the feed when she opened the terminal Tuesday morning, a red flag in the reconciliation column that had not been there Monday, and Junie was standing over it not writing, the good pen capped in her hand, which was how Della knew before she crossed the room that the number was wrong in a way the ledger could not fix.

"It doesn't balance," Junie said. "The sub-basin. It's short."

"Short how much."

"Point-three." Junie's voice did the thing it did when the round hand it wrote in met a thing that would not sit in a column. "Point-three acre-inches came off the upper Kettle sometime in the night and there's no filing under it. No offset. I ran it three times. The origin's ours and the destination's nowhere." She looked up. "Rain that was ours to have, and it left, and there's no form."

Della came around the counter and read it herself, because you read it yourself, and the number was the number. A basin's water-year is a closed book; conservation is the whole beauty of it, the plus meeting the minus at the third decimal, the sky's arithmetic clean and shut. This did not shut. Point-three inches of rain that would have crossed the upper Kettle before dawn — real rain, honest rain, the first honest front Hale had been owed in weeks — was simply not in the ledger and not in the sky, and between those two absences there was no line, no seal, no timestamp, no name.

She knew what it was. She had refused to know it for a season, the way you refuse a name you have a discipline about, but she knew it now with the flat certainty of a woman reading a deed. A legal transfer left a record and no rain. This left rain gone and no record. There was only one thing that made a hole with no defendant.

"Somebody pulled it," Della said.

"Pulled it." Junie turned the word over. "You mean stole—"

"I mean somebody bent a front off the upper Kettle without filing, sold it to a buyer downwind, and debited nobody's allotment for it, which means there's no offset, which means the basin's out of balance by exactly what they took." She was already at the shelf, pulling the reconciliation ledger, the one in her father's hand for the first four hundred pages and hers for the rest. "And I've got seventy-two hours from the minute I saw it to report it, or the not-reporting is its own crime, and I saw it four minutes ago." She checked her watch and wrote the time in the margin because the time was now evidence. "Get me Reyes."

"The auditor?"

"You report a diversion to the Authority. Reyes is the Authority that answers a phone." Della found the blank Form 31 in the drawer, the Report of Suspected Diversion, a rectangle of small print she had filed exactly twice in twenty-two years, both times for droughts that turned out to be droughts. "And Junie. When you get her — don't say theft. Say imbalance. The word for it is *imbalance* until the Authority says otherwise. We report what the number does. We don't report what a person did. We don't know a person yet."

She did not say the other thing, which was that she already knew a person, or close enough — that a stolen point-three off a basin already going critical, in the exact season Meridian was working to route around her, was not a coincidence a broker got to have. But you filed the number. You did not file the fear. The fear had no box.

---

Reyes came the next morning in the same coat she wore in the dry heat because she was always cold in it, and she brought the hand-crank field barometer she trusted over the wall units, and she did not accept the coffee. She read the Form 31 standing at the counter, and then she read the ledger page Della had marked, and then she cranked the little barometer and watched its needle and wrote a number in her own book, and only then did she look up, and her face had the forensic stillness of a person who has stopped being anyone's friend for the duration of a thing.

"Your report's clean," Reyes said. "Point-three, upper Kettle, no filing, timestamped to your discovery. By the book. I'll note it was filed inside the window." She tapped the wall barometer with one knuckle, the way Della tapped it, and got what Della always got. "This isn't reading FAIR. It's just stuck. You know that."

"I know the needle doesn't work. I tap it anyway."

"So do I." Reyes set her book down. "You want to know what the pulled front does, downwind, past this county's edge, so you can pretend the harm walked out of your jurisdiction. I'll save you the drive. Point-three off the upper Kettle bends east and it doesn't reach the next filed basin — it decays before it gets there. So it didn't go to a buyer with an address. It went to somebody close. Somebody who can't file for it because they've got no allotment to receive it into." She let that sit. "A theft this small, this local, isn't a company. A company pulls acre-*feet* and papers it. This is one man, one truck, one field, moving retail. You've got a runner in your dry channel, Della."

"I've got an out-of-balance basin and a Form 31," Della said. "That's what I know. The rest is your job."

"The rest is both our jobs. That's what a diversion is." Reyes pulled her coat closed against the office cool that wasn't cool. "I can find the field the rain went onto. Green ground in a brown county tells on itself; a man can steal the storm but he can't hide the alfalfa. But you know these people and I don't, and the Act says a broker refers *and assists.*" She said the clause the way she said all of them, exactly, without weight, so the weight fell on Della. "So you're going to come identify the field with me. Today. And when we find the man in the channel, you're going to stand there while I ask him where the rain came from, because he'll lie to me and he might not lie to a face he knows."

Della thought of the dry Kettle, the BOAT LAUNCH sign, the ramp going down into pan, the place the water wasn't. She thought of how a runner would work out of exactly there — that the place the water isn't is where you go to take some, because nobody watches an empty channel, because there is no crime scene like a place a crime is already assumed to have happened to. And she thought, with the low sick clarity of a woman being handed a shape she'd been avoiding, that she was about to go looking for a thief in the company of the law, and that everything she was afraid of about Meridian and the offset and Cass was going to be waiting for her down there in the dust, wearing a stranger's face and telling her the truth.

"Let me lock the safe," Della said.

---

The green field told on itself, exactly the way Reyes said it would.

They found it from the county road, a mile east of the elevator — a quarter-section of somebody's failed dryland corn gone abandoned two years back, brown as the rest of the valley on every side, and then, in the low draw where the old Kettle channel ran, a wet dark seam of ground and a flush of volunteer green so wrong in that country that Della's eye slid off it twice before it would land, the way an eye slides off a thing it has decided cannot be there. It had rained on one field in Hale County in the night. Only one. A hard clean line of it, delivered, punctual, dropped where no form had sent it and no sky had promised it, onto the one draw where a truck could sit below the road and not be seen.

The truck was there now. A converted grain hauler, rust and gray primer, backed down into the dead channel with its bed hooded in canvas and a hum coming off it that Della felt in her back teeth before she heard it — a hum in the fillings, ozone and hot metal on the still air, and no birds, she noticed, no birds within a quarter mile of it though the wet ground should have been black with them. She had felt that hum before, only ever standing outside the fence of a cession station, and to feel it here, retail, in a dry draw, off a truck, was to understand in her body a thing the ledger had only ever let her understand on paper.

A man came out from under the canvas when their tires crunched the channel lip. He was thirty or so and looked fifty in the way this country made men look, sun-blistered, lean the way a runner is lean, quick-handed, wiping his palms on his jeans and grinning before he had any reason to grin, the grin of a man whose whole trade is being liked faster than he can be caught.

"Auditor," he said, reading Reyes's coat and her little cranked barometer at one glance. Then he looked at Della, and something crossed his face that was not fear — a kind of recognition, warm and terrible. "And the broker. Both of you. Well." He spread his hands over the impossible green. "You caught the rain. Congratulations. What are you going to do, put it back?"

"Tobias Renn," Reyes said, not a question, reading it off some other page she carried in her head. "You're going to tell me where the front over this draw came from at three this morning."

"It came from the sky, ma'am. Same place all of it comes from." Toby's eyes stayed on Della. "You're Prine's girl. Doss's. I ran a load past your daddy's ledger years back and he never caught it either, and he was better than you." He said it without malice, almost with affection, the way you'd tell someone their father had a good arm. "You filed a 31 on me. I felt it. You people think a form is a thing that happens *to* the rain. It's not. The rain already left. The form's just you writing down that you noticed."

"The rain was Hale's," Della said. "This basin's going critical. You took point-three off a county that's dying and you dropped it on a dead cornfield for a man who couldn't afford a lease, and you didn't debit anyone, so the basin swallows the loss, so the floor comes up faster, so a man three sections over loses his last field a season sooner." She heard the flatness come into her voice, the flat exact voice she kept for the counter, the one that laid a number down and let it be as bad as it was, and she gave it to him. "You didn't move rain around a fence. You mined it out of a basin that has none to spare. That's not a Robin Hood. That's a bank robbery in a town where the bank's the water table."

She expected him to flinch. He laughed instead — not cruel, delighted, the laugh of a man who has been waiting a long time for someone competent to say the wrong true thing to him so he could hand back the right one.

"And what do you do," Toby said. He came a step up the channel, easy, hands loose. "When you stamp a 9-C. When you offset a section off a fallow onto a corn. When some company slides a study across your counter and you file the transfer that greens up one county and browns out the next. The rain leaves somebody, Della. It always leaves somebody. You file it, I run it — the widow downwind is dry either way. Only difference is you get a bond and a pension and a seal that makes it *legal,* and I get a truck and a warrant, and the rain doesn't know the difference and neither does the ground." He was close now, and his voice dropped, and it was almost gentle. "You know the only reason your hands look cleaner than mine? Better stationery. You make the widow *sign* for it. I don't even make her do that."

The dry channel was silent except for the hum in her teeth. Reyes had gone still beside her, watching, not stopping it, because Reyes wanted to see what Della's face would do to a face it might know, and there was nothing on Della's face, she made sure of that, she stilled instead of fidgeting the way she always had. But under the counter of her ribs the old wrongness came up her wrists the exact way it came when she brought the stamp down, the animal part that thought weather was God's and not a lot number, and she understood, standing in the wet ground he'd stolen, that the flinch she'd carried for eleven hundred stamps and the crime she was standing on were the same flinch, and had always been, and that she had built a life on believing there was a wall between them that was made of nothing but a filing fee.

"Where did it come from," Reyes said again, into the silence, patient as a form. "The three a.m. front. Which basin did you pull it off, Renn. Not where it landed. Where you took it *from.*"

And Toby's grin changed. It didn't leave. It just stopped being for them. He looked from the auditor to the broker and back, and Della watched him do arithmetic she couldn't see, some ledger of his own about what he owed and to whom, and when he answered he answered Reyes but he was watching Della, and he pitched it soft, and it went into her like a needle finding a nerve.

"You want to know can I do it again," he said. "That's the real question under your question, isn't it, auditor. Not where it came from. Whether there's more where it came from." He wiped his hands again, slow. "There is. There's a basin thirty miles east of here so fat it doesn't even guard its own sky. Kept all its rain since the seventies. Hangs its washing out white on the line and never once looks up." And Della's whole body went cold in the heat, because a man in a dry channel had no business knowing the color a stranger's wash dried, and he had said it looking straight at her. "I could green this whole valley up out of that basin and it'd never miss the water. Save Hale, clean and quiet, no company, no 9-C, no widow anybody in this county has to see. Rain from a basin that can spare it." He tipped his head at Della, and the grin came back, all the way, for her alone. "You've been fighting a company all season to keep from robbing that basin the legal way. Wouldn't it be something. If the only clean way left to save your county was to let me steal it for you."

Reyes said, "That's a confession to conspiracy, Renn, keep talking," and reached for her book — but Della had stopped hearing the auditor. She was standing on stolen rain in her father's channel and a stranger with a truck had just offered her, out of a basin he did not know was her sister's, thirty miles east, white washing on a line, the one thing in the world she could not take and could not stop wanting: a way to save Hale without her seal on it, without her name in the ledger, without robbing Cass on paper — by robbing Cass without any paper at all.

Her wrist ached where the stamp would go. She did not answer. She did not answer him for a long, long moment in the humming dry channel, and both of them — the thief and the auditor — watched her not answer, and both of them wrote down, in whatever ledger each of them kept, exactly how long the broker took to say no.

She got the word out finally. "File your report, auditor," she said, to Reyes, not to him, and turned for the truck. But the not-answering had already happened. It was in the record now, the way the imbalance was in the record, the way everything in this office was — noticed, timestamped, un-take-back-able. She had learned to stay sober by holding a thing in her hand and knowing exactly what it would do to her and simply not doing it, one night at a time, for eleven years. She got in the truck and put her hand in her right coat pocket and turned the chip once, around, the way a tongue works a sore tooth, and behind her in the channel Toby Renn called out, cheerful, certain, to the back of her head:

"You know where to find me, broker. I'm where the water isn't."

The chip turned smooth under her thumb, eleven years of *no* worn soft, and for the first time in all of them she was not sure the muscle that refused a drink was the same muscle she'd need — was not sure, driving up out of the dry Kettle past the ramp that ended in dust, that a life built on the power of not-doing had ever been tested by a thing she wanted this much.


---

# Chapter 12 — Form 9-C: Interbasin Reassignment (Public Hearing)

> *The public interest shall be presumed served by the Act, and no showing to the contrary shall be entertained where the transfer conforms in all particulars to its requirements.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §1

The grange hall had been built in a wetter century to hold a whole county's worth of people, and for one evening in the seventh week it nearly did.

They came in off the section roads while it was still light, the trucks parking two deep along the ditch and out into the stubble, and the dust they raised coming in hung over the lot in a low gold wall that did not settle, because dust here no longer settled, it only moved from one man's air into another's. Inside, under the tin roof that ticked as it let go of the day's heat, the folding chairs went out in rows a hundred deep and filled, and then the walls filled, men standing along them with their hats in their hands the way Marvin turned his at the counter. Della came in the side door by the little stage, and she did not look at the room, because you learned not to. You learned to look at the table.

The table was where the Authority sat. Prewitt from the district office in the middle with the docket, a court reporter beside him with her machine, and at the far end, cold in the heat, a cardigan buttoned over her blouse in a room where men were sweating through their shirts, Marisol Reyes, with her hand-crank field barometer set on the table in front of her like a small dark clock she trusted more than the wall.

The wall barometer of the grange hung behind the stage, brass-cased, twin to Della's own, and its needle stood at FAIR, and no one in the room believed it.

"We'll come to order," Prewitt said, and tapped a pen on the table, and the room came down slow, like water finding its level. "This is a public hearing on Application 9-C dash oh-four-one, Interbasin Reassignment, Ridley Fork sub-basin to Kettle upper sub-basin, applicant of record Meridian Sky Holdings. The purpose of this hearing is to receive comment. The purpose of this hearing is not to decide." He read it off the sheet, the caution the men who golfed had built into the form so that hope could be gathered up in a room and then filed. "Any ruling will be reserved."

Somebody near the back said, low, not meant to carry and carrying, *then what's it for,* and Prewitt did not answer, because the Act had already answered on the notice, in language that presumed the public interest served no matter what the public came in off the section roads to say.

Della took her chair at the end of the table, on the Authority's side, because that was where the broker sat: not with the county and not with the company, at the seam between them, the neutral filer, the gate. From the chair she could see the whole room and the room could see her. She set her hands flat on the table and stilled them and did not look for the two faces she knew were coming from thirty miles east, and found them anyway, because the body looks where the discipline says not to.

Web and Iris had come in and taken chairs three rows back on the aisle. Web had put on a clean shirt for it and his hat was in his lap and his big face was turned up toward the stage with the open uncomprehending trust of a man who believed that if you went to the meeting and said the true thing plainly, the meeting would hear you. Iris sat beside him with her phone face-down on her knee and a folder on top of it and her eyes already on Della, and had been, Della understood, since before Della sat down.

Cass had not come. Of course Cass had not come. Cass stayed on the porch with the true cruel thing and let it be true from a distance, and had sent in her place the two people she loved most, to sit in the front of a county's grief and speak for the sky over her washing line. That was Cass entire: she would not come and she would not not-be-there.

---

Voss spoke first, because Meridian had asked to, and the Act let the applicant open.

He did not come up to the stage. He stood at the little microphone in the aisle, among the people, one of them, his jacket left unbuttoned so as not to be the only pressed man in the room, and he did not read from anything. He knew it by heart, the way you know a thing you believe.

"My name is Kellan Voss. I work for Meridian Sky." He let that sit and take its boos, and it took a few, low, and he nodded at them like a man agreeing. "I know what my company's name means in a room like this. I'm not going to stand here and tell you it doesn't." He turned, slow, taking the whole hundred rows in. "So I'll tell you what we're offering, and you can hate the man who's saying it, and the offer will still be true. We are offering to buy back the east block. All of it — the allotments this county sold off before most of you were farming — and return them to Hale. We are offering to green up Larkin. We are offering this valley the one thing nobody has offered it in forty years, which is its rain."

The room made a sound then that was worse than the boos. It was the sound of hope in a place that had trained itself out of hope, a low involuntary lean-forward, chairs creaking, and Della watched it move across the rows like wind across a field, and watched Marvin Ott, four rows back, lean with it, his cracked hands going still on the crown of his hat.

"You'll be told we can't make rain. That's true. You'll be told that to give Hale a storm, one has to come off somewhere else. That's true too. I won't insult you by pretending the sky is free." He spread his hands, empty. "It comes off Ridley Fork. Thirty miles east. A basin that has margin — more rain than it uses, more than it needs, more than it has ever once gone short of. We're not asking you to take from the poor to feed the poor. We're asking a rich basin to spare what it won't miss, so a county that is one season from evacuation can go on existing." He looked, briefly, at the middle distance where a decent man puts his eyes when he says the hard part. "Somebody was always going to broker Ridley's spare storm. It might as well buy back your children's sky."

He sat down to a silence that was half the room hating him and half the room, God help them, doing the arithmetic on their own ground.

It was the best thing he could have done, Della thought, hands flat, still. He had said the true parts and let them do the work. He had not lied once. *A basin that has margin.* Every word of it accurate, and every word of it a knife, because *margin* was a lie told by whoever wasn't downwind, and there was nothing she could stand up and correct, because the number was real and the number balanced and conservation held to the third decimal, and the third decimal was somebody's cows.

---

They took public comment then, and the county stood up one at a time and split itself down the middle at the microphone.

The Coyle widow, who'd leased her July away and ran forty head on the promise of it, stood up and said take the deal, what's Ridley to us, and got a scatter of hard *amens*. A man from the Lutheran church said you don't buy back a sin with another county's rain and got his own. Then Marvin Ott stood up, slow, the whole room quieting for him because he was the oldest holding-on there was, and he turned his hat once and looked not at the Authority but at Della, the way he'd looked at her over the counter.

"I don't know Ridley," Marvin said. "Never been. Got no quarrel with it and no love for it." He turned the brim. "I know my alfalfa's dying and I know these people are offering water and I know Della Prine there will tell me straight what it costs, because she always has. So I'll take her word. Whatever she says it is. That's my comment." He nodded at her once, a courtesy, and it went into her like the second thunk of a morning, and he sat down.

Della kept her hands flat on the table and did not let her face do anything, and under the table her right hand wanted the chip in her right coat pocket the way a tongue wants a sore tooth, and she did not reach for it, which was its own kind of reaching, because Marvin had just handed his life across a grange hall to her judgment in front of the whole county, and the whole county had heard him do it, and there was no version of the truth she could speak from this chair that would not either take his water or take Cass's, and the neutral filer at the seam of the table had, for the length of an old man's sentence, no seam left to sit in.

Then Prewitt called Iris Prine-Halloran, because she had asked, in writing, to be heard as a holder of record, and the county turned to look at the young woman from the green county. Iris stood up with her folder and did not go to the aisle mic. She came up to the stage mic, the one meant for the Authority, and no one stopped her, and she set her folder on the podium and opened it, and she was not nervous — the way you are not nervous about a thing you have already proven to yourself alone at a kitchen table with a stack of registers, so that all that is left is the saying.

---

"I hold, with my father, the Ridley Fork bottomland this transfer wants the rain off of," Iris said. "So I'm an affected party. I'm allowed to be heard. It says so in the Act." A small dry thing, aimed at Voss's own move, and Voss, in his chair, tipped his head half an inch, acknowledging it, one careful person to another.

"Mr. Voss says Ridley has margin." She looked at her folder. "I ran our own numbers before I drove out here, because I keep our books. We have margin the way a man has margin the year before the year he doesn't." She let that sit; it landed; the farmers knew that year. "But that's not what I came to say. Mr. Voss said something true, and I want to say a truer thing next to it, and then I'll sit down."

She turned a page. Della watched her turn it and knew, the way you know a front you can't yet see, exactly which page it was, because there was only one page it could be, and she had read it herself in a certified copy in her father's hand four weeks ago and folded it back into the drawer.

"This county sold its sky," Iris said. "Everybody in this room knows that. You sold it off in the seventies, the east block, cheap, forever, to an outfit called Kettle Basin Land and Rain." She looked up. "Kettle Basin Land and Rain is Meridian Sky. Same deeds. Same money. They changed the name." A stir went through the rows; some of them had not known, or had known and not let themselves. "So the company that dried this valley out is the company standing here tonight offering to sell it back its own rain. That's not a rescue. That's a landlord raising your rent and calling it a gift."

Voss did not move. He had known she had it. He had probably known before she did.

"But here's the part," Iris said, and now she did the thing, and she did it the way Della would have done it, letting it come down slow and flat and exact, no heat, the bone found and pressed. "You can't sell a county's rain without a broker's seal. It's in the Act. Section two-A. No transfer's valid absent the broker's seal. So somebody sealed it. Somebody in this county, forty years ago, put their name and their stamp on the severance of your children's sky, block by block, forever, and took a fee off every one." She turned the last page over. She did not need to read it. "I filed a records request. I have the name of the broker who signed the seventies block sale that dried out Hale County."

The room had gone very still. Prewitt had a hand half-raised, unsure whether there was a rule against this, finding none, because the Act had never imagined anyone would come to a hearing to indict the chair.

Iris looked up from the folder, and she did not look at the room.

She looked at Della.

"The broker's name was Prine," Iris said, into the microphone, to the whole county, with her eyes on her aunt. "Dellard Prine. Hale County rain broker for forty years — the man who sealed the block sale that dried this county out." A beat. "And the broker in that chair tonight — the one Mr. Ott just said he'd take the word of — is Della Prine. His daughter. Trained by him. In his office, with, I'd bet, his stamp." The lightest possible pause, a courtesy, the size of the mercy Iris had left in it. "So when you take the broker's word on whether this transfer's fair, you might want to know it's the same family's word it's been for forty years. This county's been dried out by a Prine with a seal once already. That's my comment."

And she closed the folder, and she did not sit down in the front rows; she walked down the center aisle in the whole county's turning silence and out the side door into the dust, and Web got up, bewildered and loyal, and followed his daughter, his hat in his hand, out of a room where his family's name had just become a verb.

---

The county did not turn to Della all at once. It turned the way dust settles, in ones and twos, a face going still, a whisper, until the whole hot hundred-deep room had come around to the chair where the neutral filer sat with her hands flat and her father's stamp thirty feet away in a locked office and forty years of severed sky under her family's name, and Prewitt, who had a docket and no idea what to do with it, cleared his throat and did the only procedural thing left.

"Ms. Prine," he said. "You're the broker of record. For the record — is there anything you wish to say to the comment."

There it was. The seam, handed back to her, and no floor under it.

She could deny it — *my father's acts are not before this hearing* — and it would be true, and a wall, and the county would hear the wall. She could confirm it — *yes, his stamp, yes, his daughter, and it changes nothing about whether the number balances* — and that would be true too, and it would be the broker admitting she was not the neutral filer, was a party, was kin on both sides of the same river, and a broker who was a party to what she filed was, by the Act, no broker at all. Either true sentence ended her. The Act had built her a chair with two true answers under it and no third, and had presumed, in §1, that the public interest was served no matter which one she gave.

She was aware of Reyes at the far end of the table, cold in her cardigan, not writing, watching her the way you watch an instrument you are deciding whether to trust — is this woman fighting for the public interest, or for a person, or for herself, and which of the three would show now, under the whole county's eyes, when the broker was asked the one question the office had no form for.

Della kept her hands flat.

"The comment," she said, and her voice came out in the office register, flat and exact, and she heard how thin it sounded even to her, "is accurate as to the record. Dellard Prine was the Hale County broker in the years named. The block sale is in the county register and any person may pull it, as Ms. Prine-Halloran did." She did not say *my father.* She did not say *my sister's daughter.* She let the record speak in the record's voice and let the room fill in the words she would not put her seal to. "As to the present application — the broker's office takes no position on the merits. The office verifies the allotment, confirms the bond, and files what conforms. It does not decide whether a transfer is fair. There is no box on the form for fair."

It was true. It was the truest thing in the building. And she watched it land in the county like water on the dead channel — running off, finding nothing to soak into — a room full of the people it was drying out, hearing the office say, in a dead calm voice, in the seventh week, that fairness was not a field on any form it kept.

Reyes wrote something down. One line. Della could not see it and did not need to. She knew the shape of a thing being entered against a name.

"So noted," Prewitt said, and looked at his docket for a rule that would let him leave, and found it. "The record will reflect the comment and the broker's response. Ruling on Application 9-C dash oh-four-one is reserved. This hearing is adjourned."

He tapped the pen. It was the smallest sound in the room and it ended the meeting the way the stamp ended a morning, one dry noise standing in for a thing that could not be taken back, and the county got up in the ticking heat and filed out into the dust it had raised coming in, past the broker at the table, and no man among them said *you're a good broker, Della* on the way out, and Marvin Ott went by last and slowest of the standing men and put his hat on at the door and did not look at her, and that, the not-looking, was the whole of what the hearing had cost her, entered without comment.

She sat at the table until the hall was empty and the reporter had packed her machine and Prewitt had gone, and only Reyes remained, folding the legs of her hand-crank barometer, cold, exact, in no hurry.

"Web Prine's people," Reyes said, not looking up. "No relation. Surname coincidence." She had it word for word from the audit file; she said it back the way you read a man his own statement. She set the little barometer in its case and clicked it shut and finally looked at Della across the empty grange, thirty-nine and tired and homesick for a coast she couldn't afford, the one incorruptible instrument left in a captured room. "Your offsets balance, Della. I've checked every one. They balance to the decimal." She stood, and picked up the case, and the wall barometer behind her held at FAIR over both their heads. "That was never the same as saying they're clean. I used to think you knew the difference better than anybody in two counties." She went to the side door, and stopped in it, and did not soften. "Tonight your niece knew it better than you did. And she said it out loud. You want to think about why the twenty-six-year-old was the only one in the room willing to."

She went out into the dust, and the door swung shut behind her on its slow hinge, and Della sat alone in the emptied hall under a needle that read FAIR, in the exact center of the seam that had just been sawn out from under her, and reached at last into her right coat pocket and turned the chip once, around, the way a tongue works a sore tooth — eleven years of *no* worn smooth under her thumb — and thought, with the flat clarity of a woman reading a barometer she has finally stopped believing: *the whole county knows now.* Knows she was Prine. Knows she was Doss's. Knows, from a girl's folder, whose sister she was.

And the transfer had not been stopped. Nothing had been stopped. A ruling was reserved, which meant the form was still walking, and now it was walking toward her over open ground, with no neutral left anywhere in Hale County for her to stand on.


---

# Chapter 13 — Form 3-A: Allotment of Record (Eleanor Prine, Deceased)

> *Death does not extinguish an allotment; the right passes to the estate, and the estate to the heirs, and the heirs shall be bound by the record as it stands.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, Reg. 3-A.2

The request came in on Authority letterhead the morning after the hearing, and Della was almost grateful for it, because it gave her hands something to do that was not remembering the sound of Iris's voice saying *the broker's own father* into a room full of people who had known her mother.

A title company in the district seat wanted a certified copy of the Eleanor Prine allotment of record. Someone downstate was clearing an old estate — a probate that had sat unclosed for forty-two years the way probates did in counties that emptied out faster than the paperwork could keep up — and the file had surfaced with Eleanor's name on it, and by law a certified copy could only issue from the county broker, over the county seal, out of the county ledger. Her mother's rain. Della was the only person alive licensed to hand it over, and she was going to hand it over to a stranger for a thirty-dollar fee, because that was the shape the job made of everything, even this.

"You want me to pull it?" Junie had the letter in her hand. She had been careful with Della all morning, careful the way you are careful around a person who has been hit in public and not yet said whether it hurt. "I can find it. What year?"

"I'll find it," Della said. "It's before your columns start."

The old ledgers lived on the bottom shelf of the safe room, the tall gray books going back through her own hand to her father's, the leather gone the color of the county. She knew where her mother's year would be without looking at the spines. She had walked past it a hundred times a month for forty-two years and never once taken it down, which was its own kind of address, the way a person keeps a photograph in a drawer they open every day and never turn face-up.

She took it down now. The dust came off the top edge in a gray line onto her wrist, the county filing itself down even in here, even into the record of the woman it had orphaned two girls to.

She carried the book to the counter and opened it to the water-year that had Eleanor in it, and the flat exact hand there was her father's — younger, the loops a little more generous than the hand she'd inherited, a man not yet forty writing his wife's name into a column, and she understood, seeing it, that Doss had certified his own wife's allotment, because he was the broker, because there was no one else in the county who could, because in this family even grief went over the seal.

---

*The dust came the year Della turned twelve, and it came wrong.*

*It came in a spring with no rain behind it — Hale's own rain, this was before the great sell-off finished, when the county still had a sky of its own to lose — and it came off ground that had gone bare because the snowpack failed in the mountains west and the Kettle ran thin and the pivot circles browned early, and then it came off itself, the way a dry country will start to eat its own dirt, one dust year feeding the next. By June the sky over Ainsley Hale was not blue at all. It was the color of a bruise going yellow, and the light came through it flat and sourceless, and the wash on Eleanor's line came in grayer than it went out, and Eleanor said, folding it, that she had never in her life brought washing in dirtier than she hung it, and that a county where you could not get a sheet clean was a county telling you something.*

*Eleanor was already sick by then. Della understood this only afterward, in the way children reassemble the year they were twelve out of the pieces adults let them hold. There had been a cough for a long time, low in the chest, that everyone called a cold and then a bronchitis and then stopped calling anything, because naming it would have meant taking her to the district seat, and the truck needed a clutch, and the allotment they might have sold for the clutch was the family's rain, and Doss had a rule about the family's rain that was the one he kept longest and broke last: you did not sell the thing that fed the ground that fed the girls. So Eleanor coughed through the dust year and hung the wash and brought it in gray, and the cough got into the dust and the dust got into the cough, and one of them finished the other in August, and Della was twelve and holding a lamp in a hot dark room because the power had gone out in a dust storm and her mother could not get her breath and her father had driven to the district seat for a doctor who arrived, when he arrived, to certify a death instead of prevent one.*

*Della held the lamp. She remembered that. Not the last breath — the mind files that one down, out of mercy, into a fact instead of a sound — but the lamp, the weight of it, the way her arm ached from holding it steady over the bed for what her father needed it held steady over, and the way the dust came through the shut window anyway and settled on the white sheet while her mother lay under it, so that by morning the sheet Eleanor had failed to get clean was gray again, filed down, the county writing its name on her too.*

---

The certified copy took Della four minutes to draw. The allotment was old and clean, an inch and three-quarters of Hale rain attached to the Depot Street parcel and to the ground her grandparents had held east of it, the good original apportionment of the mid-seventies, a rich enough right for a poor enough county, and it had passed to Eleanor's estate on her death and sat there forty-two years accruing nothing, because there was no rain left in Hale to accrue, because within four years of Eleanor's death the whole of it had been sold upstream and the sky had gone apologetic and stayed.

She wrote *DECEASED* in the estate line the way the form required and did not let the pen slow on it.

"Della." Junie was at the far end of the counter, not writing, the letter still in her hand. She had been reading it, Della realized, reading past the request line to the estate reference, doing the arithmetic a smart clerk could not help doing. "This is your mother."

"It's an allotment of record. Reg. 3-A.2. Death doesn't extinguish it. It passes to the estate." Della inked the pad and did not reach for the stamp yet. "Everything I told you the first week. The rain doesn't care whose name comes off the deed. It goes over the seal regardless."

"I'm not asking about the reg." Junie set the letter down. She was twenty-two and still, after the hearing, after the room, still asking the questions Della had trained herself out of asking two decades ago, and Della both wanted to stop her and could not have stood it if she had. "At the hearing yesterday. Iris said your father sold Hale's rain. To move his — to move somebody. And now there's your mother's allotment sitting here, and it's from the same years, and I'm the one keeping the ledger, and I'd rather hear it from you than keep finding it in the book."

Della looked at the girl. Then she did the thing she had not planned to do, which was to reach past the certified copy and pull the current file — the one that had come in on the courtesy feed weeks ago, the feasibility study, the offset schedule with a deed reference on it that read PRINE, C. & PRINE, W. — and she laid it on the counter next to her mother's page. She did not say anything about it. She let the two records lie there side by side the way she had let her father's block sale and Cass's living allotment lie side by side on this counter in another week, the way the whole trap kept arranging itself into pairs whenever she stopped guarding against it.

"My mother died in a dust year," Della said. Flat. Exact. The voice that filed a thing without softening it. "My sister nearly died in the next one. Same lungs. Cass had the bad ones, I had the strong ones — that's not a figure of speech, Junie, that's a doctor's line, that's the sentence the whole family got sorted by. And there was no money, because there's never money in a place like this, and there was no second parent anymore, because the dust had already taken the one who'd have sold everything before she let a daughter go the way she went." She touched the study, once, with two fingers, and drew them back. "My father had one allotment worth enough to move a dying girl somewhere green. His own family's. So he sold it. And organized the neighbors into selling theirs, and took his fee on all of it, and bought my sister a life in Ridley with the last rain Hale had, and kept me here to run the office he was going to leave me."

Junie was very still. "Because you had the strong lungs."

"Because I could be left in the dry place." Della squared the certified copy against the counter's edge, an old motion, a settling motion, the hands doing their work while the mouth said the thing. "You want to know why Hale sold so much so cheap. That's why. It wasn't cheap. It was a girl's breathing, marked down to what a company would pay for it. My father did a monstrous thing, and he did it for love, and he never once said sorry, because he wasn't, because he'd do it again." She heard the hearing in it, Iris's voice, *this county has done this before,* and she cut it there. "Log the certified copy. Fee's thirty dollars and it's not waived. My mother's estate can afford it. There's nobody left in it to mind."

She brought the stamp down on the certified copy of Eleanor Prine's allotment of record.

The *thunk* went up her wrist the way it always did, but it did not stop at the wrist this time. It carried up into the forearm, into the old ache that had been getting worse for weeks, the wrongness that had lived in her hands since the first stamp of her life and had lately started living higher, closer to the bone — and she stood a moment with her hand still on the brass and her mother's name under it, certified, sealed, real, and understood with a clarity she had spent forty-two years and eleven sober years arranging her whole competence to avoid: that she had just done to her mother, in four minutes, over a fee, exactly what her father had done to a county. Handed the rain along in good order. Filed it down. Kept the record clean.

She put the stamp back in the safe and turned the dial and heard the tumblers drop, and it did not sound like legitimacy this time. It sounded like a lock.

---

She drove home the long way, past the Kettle, past the BOAT LAUNCH sign pointing its rusted arrow down into the gray pan, and she did not examine why the dead channel could hold her eye the way it did, the same way she did not examine the barometer or the name she would not pick up. But at the house on Depot Street she did not go inside.

She stood in the yard in the last of the light, by the dead lilac her mother had planted living, and she looked at the clothesline.

It hung slack between its two poles the way it had hung her whole adult life, the wire gone dull, a single wooden pin still clipped to it near the middle, weathered gray. She did not use the line. Everyone in Hale knew you did not use the line; the dust grayed the wash before it dried, so you brought it in wet and hung it over the kitchen chairs and lived like a person camping in her own house. She had told herself for years that this was practical. That it was the dust, the physics of the county, a thing that had happened to the wash the way it happened to the butter and the sills and the sheets.

But her mother had used this line. Her mother had hung wash on this exact wire in a dust year worse than any Della had filed through, had brought it in gray and cursed the county and hung it out again the next morning, because that was what you did, because you did not stop washing just because the sky had stopped being on your side. And Della had inherited the wire and the poles and the pins and had quietly stopped using them, and had called it the dust, and had never once let herself see it plain: that it was not the dust. It was that she could not stand to bring in gray what her mother had failed to bring in clean. She had left the line dead on purpose, forty years, and called it weather.

Thirty miles east there was a line strung between two posts on the Ridley Fork bottomland, and on it a woman she had not spoken to in eleven years hung washing that dried white and stiff and clean, because their father had bought her a sky that would keep its bargain, and Della had grown up believing the difference between the two lines was justice — that Cass had been given the clean sky and Della the gray one, and that the gray one had made Della honest and the clean one had made Cass a coward who ran. She had built forty years of contempt on that, the way you build a wall on the one flat stone you can find in a field of them.

Standing in the yard now, with her mother's certified allotment logged behind her and the offset schedule with her sister's name on it lying next to it in her mind, she saw the thing she had spent her whole competent life not seeing, and it did not comfort her and it did not exempt her. It indicted her forward instead of back.

Her father had taken the rain off everyone he loved to save the one of them who was dying, and had kept the durable child in the dust to run the machine, and had done it for love, and would do it again. And here was the sky, wearing a better suit, remembering her name, sliding a study across her counter that pulled a storm off her sister to save the neighbor turning his hat, asking her — asking Della, the durable one, the one who could be left in the dry place — to do the exact thing her father did. For love. For a good reason. For Marvin. Take the rain off the family to save the family. Take a fee you can't avoid. Tell yourself you had no choice.

She understood, at last and too late for it to be a warning to anyone but herself, that she had not been raised as her father's victim. She had been raised as his rough draft.

She took the chip out of her right coat pocket and held it in her palm in the dying light. Eleven years. Worn soft under years of *no*, the number and the word gone smooth. She had built a life on the discipline of holding a thing in her hand and knowing exactly what it would do to her and not doing it, one night at a time, and she had believed — she saw now she had believed it the way you believe a barometer that reads FAIR — that the muscle of refusing the drink was the same muscle that would let her refuse the sky. That *no* was *no*. That if she could sit eleven years in a dry county and not go into the Sump, she could sit at that counter and not become her father, and the market would break itself against her the way the drink had.

But the drink had never needed her yes. The drink only needed her to stop saying no, and then it took her. The sky was different. It did not need her to stop refusing. It had an apparatus built to route around a refusal, a study already run, a fee already calculated, a schedule with her sister's rain measured out to the acre-inch — and it did not care whether Della said no, because it had learned, the way her father learned, that a broker who will not sell the family's rain can always, eventually, be shown a dying girl and a good reason and offered the chance to do it out of love instead of greed, which is the offer no durable child was ever built to refuse.

The light went out of the yard. The heat came up out of the ground the way it did, and moved toward the house, and would come in through the shut window and sit on her chest all night, a weight she could not file her way out of.

Della clipped the chip back into her pocket. She went to the clothesline and put her hand on the dead gray wire, and it was warm from the day and rough with rust under her palm, and she stood there holding it the way she held the barometer, the way she held the chip, checking a dead thing for a pulse out of a habit older than hope.

Then she took the one weathered pin off the wire, the last one her mother had left clipped there forty-two years ago, and she held it in her fist, and she did not put it back.

She went inside and did not turn on the light and stood in the hot dark, and for the first time in eleven years she wanted a drink not because of anything that had happened but because of something she had finally understood, which is the worst reason, the reason with no bottom — and she poured a glass of water instead and drank it standing up, and it tasted of the pipes and the county and the far-off idea of rain, and she held her mother's clothespin in her other hand and did not put it down.

Across the county line, over Ridley, the sky was still the honest blue of a place with rain of its own, and under it her sister was bringing in the wash white and stiff and clean, trusting the line. Della stood in the dark in Hale with the dead woman's clothespin closed in her fist and the empty water glass sweating in her other hand and the drink she had wanted still wanting, and she did not move for a long time, and the heat came in through the shut window and sat on her chest, and there was no form for what she held in her hand, and no fee, and no seal that could file it down.


---

# Chapter 14 — Form 18: Interim Custody of Transferred Water (Bonded Storage)

> *Water in transit belongs to the transferee; and the transferee's title shall not abate for the pendency of any objection, delivery being a right that vests upon bonding and not upon receipt.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §7(a)

The notice of witness came on a Monday, in the mail, on the Authority's gray stock, and it named a delivery window for the Tuesday and a place — the Coyle section, north of the highway, forty head and a July allotment the widow had leased away last spring — and it required, per the Act, the presence of the county broker to certify volume at receipt. Della read it twice at the counter and did not need to read it a third time. She had certified deliveries before. Most of them she had certified from the office, initialing the terminal record when the timestamp and the volume came through clean, because most deliveries in Hale these days were rain going *out*, and you did not need to stand in a field to watch a thing leave.

This one was rain coming *in.* A small legal one. Point-three of an inch, a fraction of a fraction, bonded into interim custody by Meridian Sky and released to the Coyle ground under a demonstration order — the word was on the notice, *demonstration,* dry as everything else the men who golfed wrote down — and it was the first rain scheduled to fall on Hale County ground in fourteen weeks, and it was somebody else's, routed through the widow Coyle by a form Della had not signed, and it would arrive at 2:40 in the afternoon whether Della stood in the field or not.

"They want you to watch," Junie said. She was reading the notice over Della's shoulder, which she had started doing, lately, the way a person starts standing closer to a fire. "Why do they want you to watch a point-three?"

"They don't want me to watch the point-three." Della folded the notice back along its crease. "They want me in the field when it comes. Get your hat. You're certifying it with me. You'll do the volume read and I'll co-sign it, and you'll learn what a delivery does to a barometer, which is the only part of this job the terminal can't teach you."

"Is it — I mean is it different? Delivered rain?"

"It's punctual," Della said. "That's the whole of what's wrong with it. Get your hat."

---

They took Della's truck out the highway at half past one, Junie with the field kit on her lap and the hand-crank barometer the Authority issued its certifiers, a little brass-and-leather instrument in a case, older than either of them, that you wound and read and trusted because the wall units in this county had all gone stuck at FAIR. Reyes was already there when they came up the Coyle drive — the auditor's plain sedan nosed against the fence, Reyes standing beside it in a canvas coat she did not need in the July heat, because Reyes was always cold, and she had her own field barometer out already, the hand-crank kind, holding it the way another woman might hold a rosary.

"Broker," Reyes said.

"Auditor."

"You brought the apprentice."

"She's certifying. She should see one land."

Reyes looked at Junie the way she looked at everything, without warmth and without malice, weighing her, filing her. "Then she should see it read right." She cranked her instrument a half turn and looked at the needle and said nothing about what it told her, which was Reyes's way of telling you it had told her something.

The widow Coyle came out of the house wiping her hands and did not come all the way to them. She stood at the edge of her own yard with her forty head bawling in the lot behind her, thin cattle on thin ground, and she watched the three women and the fenced dry section and the flat blue over it with the face of a person who has been told a gift is coming and has lived long enough to weigh a gift in her hand before she thanks anybody for it. Her July was gone; she'd leased it off last spring to make a note, the way they all did, and the ground in front of her would have stayed brown as a boot the whole summer on its own account. What was coming at 2:40 was not hers. It was a demonstration, delivered onto her section because her section was empty and cheap to demonstrate on, the way you test a pen on the corner of the pad.

Marvin Ott's truck came up the drive at two.

Della had not expected him and was not surprised. Word of a delivery moved through a dry county faster than the delivery did. He got out slow and came across the yard turning his hat and stood a little apart from the women, near the widow, two old holders of finished ground come to watch water fall on somebody's dirt, and he looked at the fenced section and then he looked at Della, and what was in his face was not hope exactly. It was the memory of hope, brought out and looked at, the way you take a photograph out of a drawer to check that the person in it is as gone as you remember.

"Della," he said.

"Marvin."

"They tell me it's real." He said it low, so the widow wouldn't have to hear him want it. "A real one. Coming down on the Coyle ground."

"It's real," Della said. "It's three-tenths of an inch and it belongs to a company and it's landing here because here is empty. Don't build anything on it, Marvin. It's a sample. They're showing us the pump works."

He nodded, slow, and did not stop looking at the sky, and Della let him look, because letting a man look at a sky was most of the mercy the county had left in it.

---

At 2:20 the birds went quiet.

Della felt it before she heard it stop, the way you feel a room empty behind you. The meadowlarks that had been working the fence line all afternoon simply were not there anymore, and the widow's cattle stopped bawling all at once, forty throats going still together, and in the new quiet there was a pressure that had not been there, a heaviness on the top of the head and the flat of the chest, and Della's own body, twenty-two years trained on this county, read it before her mind would say the word.

Junie said, "The barometer's—" and stopped, because the needle in the little brass case was moving.

It was moving. Della watched it over the girl's shoulder. The needle that had stood at FAIR in the wall case on her office wall for fourteen weeks, that she tapped every morning the way you check a dead thing for a pulse — its twin here in Junie's shaking hands was coming *down,* dropping off the top of the dial in a slow steady fall that had nothing of weather's ordinary hesitation in it, no gust, no tease, no maybe. It fell like a thing being lowered on a rope. It fell on a schedule.

"That's it coming," Della said. Her voice was flat and exact and she made it stay that way because there was a girl reading a number and the number had to be read right. "Write the time you first saw it move. That's the front bending in. Now watch the west."

They watched the west. Reyes watched the west, cranking her own instrument, her mouth a straight line. Marvin took his hat off. The widow Coyle put her hand on the top rail of her fence and gripped it.

The front came in low from the west, over the dead pivot circles, a bruise-colored line laid straight across the sky as though a rule had been held to it — no scud, no ragged leading edge, no honest thunderhead building its anvil over an hour. A wall. It came on at the speed of a truck and you could see, under it, the exact place where the dry ended, a curtain of gray hanging down from the line to the ground and moving toward them across the section, and where the curtain touched the ground the ground went dark.

"It's coming down the section line," Junie whispered.

It was coming down the section line. It wetted the far fence post first — Della watched a single cedar post two hundred yards out go from bone-gray to black between one breath and the next — and then the next post, and the next, the darkness walking the fence toward them post by post on a straight front you could have set a watch by, and it was, Della thought, it was exactly setting a watch by; somewhere a clock was running and the rain was keeping it, arriving on the Coyle section the way a freight train arrives, announced, scheduled, indifferent, a thing you could hear coming and be standing in the wrong place for.

It reached them at 2:40.

It came over the fence and over the four of them and the widow all at once, a hard clean sheet of it, cold, cold in a way the day had no business allowing, cold the way the Ridley alfalfa had smelled in the truck vents, and it soaked Della's shoulders and ran off the brim of Marvin's hat where he held it against his chest and drummed on the field kit and turned the dust of the Coyle section to a dark running mud in the space of a held breath. It smelled of ozone and cold stone and the far-off idea of rain made suddenly and horribly near. The cattle in the lot did not run to it or from it; they stood in it, heads down, taking it, as bewildered as everyone.

And it was, God help her, *beautiful,* and Della stood in it and felt the two things at once and could not get them apart — the animal gladness of water on skin after fourteen weeks of dust, the body's dumb yes to it, and under that, in her wrists, in the same wrists that took the jar of the stamp, the old wrongness turned all the way up, because this rain had a timestamp and a volume and a basin of origin, because this rain had been *lifted off some other field* two counties from here and carried here and let down on the Coyle ground at 2:40 on a Tuesday to show a widow and a broker and a dying farmer that Meridian could make the sky do this, and would, and the water on her face was the proof of it running cold into her collar.

Junie was reading the volume off the kit, water in her eyes and her voice steady, God bless her, the girl had spine — "Point-three-oh at the gauge, holding, point-three-oh—" and Reyes was cranking her barometer and watching the needle bottom out, and Marvin stood bareheaded in three-tenths of an inch of somebody else's rain with his face turned up into it and his eyes shut, a man letting a stranger's water fall on him because it was the only water there was, and Della looked at him and had to look away.

It stopped at 2:52. Not tapered. Stopped. The curtain walked off the east fence line the way it had walked on, post by post going from black back to gray behind it, and the front went on east and got small and the sun came back onto a running muddy section and a scatter of stunned cattle, and the birds did not come back for a long time. Point-three of an inch. Twelve minutes. A demonstration.

---

"Read me your final," Della said, and her voice shook once and then did not. "Volume at receipt."

"Point-three-oh acre-inches," Junie said. She had it written, the pencil-marks blurred where the rain had got the page. "Timestamp two-forty to two-fifty-two. Basin of origin—" She stopped. She looked at the transfer slip clipped in the kit, the one the Authority had provided, and Della watched her read the line, and watched the girl's face when she read it, and knew what it said before Junie said it, because she had been avoiding reading it herself since the mail came Monday.

"Basin of origin," Junie said again, quiet now, "is Ridley Fork."

The word sat in the wet air.

"Ridley Fork," Junie said. "This came off Ridley. This — the widow's three-tenths, this little demonstration one, it came off—" She looked up at Della. "That's your sister's basin. That's where you served the—"

"Read it as it's written," Della said. "You certify what the slip says. Ridley Fork sub-basin, point-three-oh, received Coyle section fourteen-forty hours to fourteen-fifty-two, no shortfall. Sign it and I'll co-sign it."

Junie signed it. Her hand was not as steady on the pen as it had been on the gauge. Della took the slip and read it through — three-tenths of an inch of her sister's sky, lifted off the Ridley Fork bottomland where the washing hung white on the line, carried thirty miles west, and let down on a widow's empty section to prove a point — and she uncapped her pen and co-signed it under the girl's name, her own bonded signature going onto the record of it, because certifying volume at receipt was the broker's plain duty under the Act and had nothing to do with whether the broker had signed the transfer, and that was the thing about the duty, that was the whole cold beauty of it: you did not have to have signed the theft to be required to sign the receipt.

She capped the pen. Her wrist ached where it always ached.

Reyes had come up beside her, silent on the wet ground, and was reading the slip over her arm. "Three-tenths off Ridley Fork," Reyes said, low, not for the others. "For a demonstration." She wound her barometer down and put it in its case and looked at Della with the flat forensic patience that was the closest thing to kindness she had. "They don't demonstrate for a widow, Della. They don't spend three-tenths of a sub-basin's water to impress the Coyle place." She latched the case. "They demonstrate for the person who has to sign the big one. This was a letter. It was addressed to you."

---

The car was on the highway before Della saw it — a clean dark sedan wrong for gravel, slowing at the Coyle drive, and Voss got out of it in his good shoes and stood at the edge of the wet field with his hands in his pockets, not coming closer, letting the water and the mud be a thing that had happened to other people. He had timed it, of course. He had timed all of it. He lifted a hand to Della across the running section, unhurried, sorry, and she crossed the mud to him because the alternative was to make him cross it and she would not give him the pleasure of ruining the shoes on purpose.

"Della." He looked past her at the widow's darkened ground, at Marvin standing in it with his hat still off, and something moved in his pressed face that a person who did not know better would have called feeling. "It came in beautifully. Right on the window."

"It came in punctual." Della's coat was heavy with the rain of her sister's sky. "You spent three-tenths of Ridley to wet a widow's fallow and put on a show. That's an expensive letter, Kellan. What's it say."

"It says it works." Voss said it gently, the way you'd tell a frightened person the surgery is routine. "It says the water can come home to Hale, exactly like that, on any section you name — the Ott place, the whole east block, Larkin filled back to the boat ramp. That was three-tenths. I have the rest bonded and waiting." He watched her take it. "That's what interim custody means, Della — you'll have read the notice. The storm's already ours. We've bonded it into storage. Title vested when we posted the bond." He said the next part without any change in his voice at all, which was how she knew it was the whole point of the afternoon, the sentence the three-tenths had been written to carry. "It doesn't abate for your objection. Your seal never stopped it, Della. It only decides how gently it comes down.
