Whether Hale gets its deluge in one clean signature — or whether it comes the ugly way, a demonstration at a time, off your sister's basin, three-tenths here and three-tenths there, while you make everybody wait." He let it sit exactly as long as she would have. "The rain is coming home. You're just deciding how much of Cass it costs to get here. I'd hate for it to be retail."

Behind Della, on the Coyle section, Marvin Ott put his wet hat back on his head and stood in the last of the running mud looking at the place the rain had been, and the cattle began, one by one, to bawl again, and the barometer in the little brass case in Junie's hand climbed slowly back toward FAIR, and thirty miles east, on a line strung between two posts, Della could not see and could not stop seeing the washing going stiff and white and clean in a sky that had, three-tenths of an inch ago, on schedule, for a demonstration, begun to be spent.


---

# Chapter 15 — Form 31-B: Confidential Informant (Diversion)

> *The Authority may treat with a diverter to prevent greater harm; and no information rendered in confidence shall bind the Authority to spare the informant.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, Reg. 31-B.

The rain from the interim-custody delivery had dried out of the dust by the third morning, which was the wrong word for what it did — it did not dry, it *withdrew*, the way a bruise goes from purple to yellow to gone while the bone underneath still remembers being hit. For three days the pivot-circles east of town had held a darkness in them that was not green, only wet, and then the sky went back to its apologetic blue and took the wet with it and left the ground looking guiltier than before, and Della stopped going to the window to check.

She had a discipline now about the window too.

Reyes came in at nine with two coffees she'd bought at the Trestle, which was not a thing an auditor did, and set one on the counter in front of Della and kept the other, and this was how Della understood that the shape of the day had already been decided somewhere Della had not been.

"You're buying me coffee," Della said.

"I'm cold." Reyes had her collar up against a heat that stood at eighty already. She was always cold; she carried the coast around inside her like a stone she couldn't set down. "And I want to sit at your counter for an hour and read a thing, and it's easier if you don't watch me do it like I'm going to steal the ledger."

"You are going to read it like you're going to steal the ledger."

"Yes." Reyes almost smiled. She did not, which Della respected. She unzipped a soft folder and laid three pages on the counter, squared them with two fingers, and turned them so Della could read along, which was the courtesy of a person who did not need to hide the knife because the knife was the law. "Second imbalance in the Kettle sub-basin in nineteen days. Point-six acre-inches that should have fallen on the north townships and didn't. No filed transfer. No offset. The sky is missing rain it was assigned and there is no form that says why."

Della read the field notes without touching them. Junie, at the ledger, had stopped writing.

"That's not a delivery," Della said. "That's a pull."

"That's a pull." Reyes tapped one line with a nail bitten to the quick. "Same signature as the June instance. Fast off-take, dirty edge, a front bent hard and cheap by somebody who doesn't care about the seam it leaves. A runner." She looked up. "You know who runs the Kettle bed."

Della did know. She had known for two weeks, in the way you know a thing before you've decided what it costs to say it, and she had spent those two weeks turning the knowledge over in her right coat pocket where the chip was, learning its edges. Toby Renn worked out of the dead channel below the Larkin pan, out of a grain truck that hummed wrong, and he had come to her — not to the counter, to the alley door after dark, twice — and offered her Hale a storm off-book from *a basin that can spare it*, and she had said no, and had not reported him, and the not-reporting was the first thing she had done in twenty-two years that the ledger had no line for.

"I hear the bed's got somebody in it," Della said. "Grain truck. Comes and goes."

Reyes waited. That was her whole method: she asked the question under the question by not asking it, and let the silence do the work an interrogation couldn't. Della had used the same silence on Marvin, on the Webber girl, on a hundred people at this counter, and she recognized being on the wrong side of it the way you recognize your own voice on a tape and hate it.

"Reg. 31-B," Reyes said finally, mild, as if reading a weather report. "The Authority may treat with a diverter. If your runner would rather talk to me than to a magistrate, there's a chair for him. I'm not hunting the man. I'm hunting the hole. He gives me who's *buying* the pulls, who's routing them, and I can be very forgetful about the truck." She sipped the coffee she was too cold to want. "You could tell him that. If you knew him. Which you don't."

And there it was — the door held open, courteous, at exactly the width Della needed. She could feed Reyes Toby. She could feed Reyes Toby and it would be *true*, it would be information given to the law by a bonded officer of the law doing her sworn duty under §13, clean-handed, correct. It would keep the Authority's one honest auditor pointed at the black market and away from the frosted-glass door with COUNTY RAIN BROKER on it. And Toby had told her, in the alley, in the dark, that Meridian's off-book side ran the pulls he ran — that the runner and the corporation were the same hand in two gloves — so if she pointed Reyes at Toby she was, in the same motion, pointing Reyes at Meridian, gumming the works, buying Hale another week of the transfer not happening.

All of it true. All of it legal. And under it, so quiet she almost didn't catch her own hand doing it, she was reaching for a man who had come to her for help and turning him into a lever.

"If I knew him," Della said, "I'd tell him the Authority keeps its word about as well as the sky does."

Reyes looked at her a moment too long. "That's not a no."

"It's not anything. I file forms, Marisol. I don't run informants." Della slid the field notes back across the counter, squared them the way Reyes had, a small mockery neither of them acknowledged. "But if a person in that bed wanted to make his troubles smaller, and I happened to be somewhere he could hear it, I'd say the same thing I say to every farmer who thinks a company's about to save him. There's no clean basin. There's just the one the Authority hasn't looked at yet."

Reyes gathered her pages. She did not thank Della, because thanking was for people who thought a transaction had been friendly. At the door she stopped with her hand on the frame.

"Your barometer still stuck?"

"Still FAIR."

"Mine moved Tuesday. Off my belt, the hand-crank." Reyes said it without inflection, a fact laid down like a stamp. "For about forty minutes, right when your interim-custody delivery came in on the north edge. Wall units don't feel a bent front till it's on top of them. The field ones feel it coming." She looked at the dead brass unit on Della's wall, the needle that had not moved off FAIR in fourteen weeks. "You ought to get one that tells you the truth. That one's not reading fair. It's just stuck reading fair, which is a different thing, and if you stand under it long enough you stop knowing the difference."

She went out into the blue. The bell rang her gone.

Junie waited until the truck had pulled off Main before she spoke, and when she spoke she did not look up from the ledger, which was how Della knew the girl had learned something in the last ten minutes and hated that she'd learned it.

"You know who's in the bed," Junie said.

"I know who people say is in the bed."

"You said *grain truck*. She didn't say grain truck. You said it first." Junie set the good pen down in the gutter of the ledger, careful, parallel to the spine. "I keep the transfer log, Della. I know what's come in and what hasn't. There's a runner working the Kettle and you know his name and you didn't give it to the auditor, and then you told her a thing that sounded like you did." She finally looked up. Her face was doing the thing Della remembered her own face doing at twenty-two, before she'd learned to still it — the open honest hurt of a person discovering that competence and goodness were not the same skill. "Are we still on the right side of this? Of the Act? Because I can't tell anymore where we're standing, and it's my column too. My hand's in this book now."

The heat sat on the office like it sat on the house at night. Della looked at the girl's careful hand in the ledger, the round unblooded letters going up the column in ink that would outlast all of them, and she thought about the true answer, which was: *No. I stopped being on the right side of it the night I let a criminal walk out of my alley without a call, and I have spent two weeks deciding that the right side was never a place you could stand anyway, and I am about to teach you to believe that so you can keep breathing in this room, and the teaching is worse than the lie because you'll carry it forty years and file it into someone else at a counter like this one.*

She did not say that. She said the other thing.

"We're on the side of the county," Della said. "Which is legal, and which is the Act, because the Act is the only thing standing between Hale and a company that would take the last inch off Marvin Ott's alfalfa and call it stewardship. Sometimes doing right by the Act means using every corner of it. Delay is a corner. Information is a corner. I'm not lying to the auditor, Junie. I'm not telling her everything I suspect about a man I've never been introduced to, because everything I suspect isn't the same as everything I know, and the day a broker starts reporting her suspicions is the day this office becomes an arm of whoever's asking. That's not bending the Act. That's *guarding* it."

It was a good answer. It was so good it frightened her, how fast it came, how clean, each clause offsetting the last one like a transfer balancing to the third decimal — and how much of it was true, and how the truth of it was what made it a lie, because a lie you can catch and a rationalization you can live inside forever, and she had just built the girl a room to live inside.

She watched Junie take it. That was the part she would remember — not the saying, the *watching*. She watched the hurt in the girl's face resolve into something worse, into relief, into the wanting-to-believe of a person who needed the woman training her to be one of the good ones, because if Della wasn't, then Junie's whole future in this chair was already dirty and she was only twenty-two. Junie picked the pen back up. Her hand had steadied.

"That makes sense," Junie said, and it was the saddest sentence Della had heard in that office, sadder than Marvin's, sadder than the Webber girl's *there's no way*, because it was the sound of a conscience being taught to file itself down.

"Log the imbalance in the auxiliary column," Della said. "Time of notice, source, no action pending." She turned to the terminal so she wouldn't have to watch the girl write it. "That's the record staying clean."

"Record staying clean," Junie repeated, and wrote it, and the two of them sat in the dry office recording the fact of a stolen storm in a careful hand, two women keeping an honest ledger of a thing neither of them was being honest about.

She waited until noon, when Junie went to the Trestle for the sandwich the girl ate at the counter every day, and then she took her coat off the hook — the chip a small weight against her ribs — and drove out to the Kettle.

She did not park at the BOAT LAUNCH sign. She went past it, down the maintenance track that ran along the top of the dead channel, to where the grain truck sat in the shade of the trestle the town was named for, and she got out into a heat that came up off the cracked pan like a hand, and she walked down into the place the water wasn't.

Toby was under the truck doing something to it she made a point of not looking at, and he slid out on a creeper when he heard the gravel, wiping his hands on a rag that only moved the grease around, grinning the grin that was mostly nerve.

"The broker," he said. "In the ditch. Somebody's going to talk."

"The auditor was in my office this morning," Della said. "Point-six off the north townships. Nineteen days after the last one. She's got your signature — the seam, the dirty edge. She knows there's a runner in the Kettle. She doesn't know your name yet and she's not looking for it very hard, because what she wants is who's buying."

Toby's grin didn't move but something behind it did, a needle finally coming off FAIR. "And you drove out here in the heat of the day to tell me the weather's turning. That's neighborly."

"I drove out here to make you a proposition," Della said, and heard herself say *proposition*, heard the flat exact broker's voice she used at the counter come out of her mouth in the bottom of a dry river to a man who stole rain, and did not stop it. "You said Meridian's off-book side runs your pulls. That the runner and the company are the same hand."

"I said too much, that night. You had that look. People tell you things. It's a talent, probably ruins your evenings."

"If it's the same hand," Della said, "then every pull you *don't* run for them is a delivery they can't schedule. Every time the seam shows and Reyes has to open a file, it's a week their transfer sits in a technical corner instead of on my counter. I can't stop Meridian legally anymore. My noes are all used up. But you can make their logistics ugly, and I can make sure the ugly points anywhere but at me, and if the auditor comes to treat with a diverter under 31-B — and she will, because she's honest and she's tired and it's the shortest line to the buyer — you go in and you give her Meridian's routing and you give her nothing about a broker who never introduced herself to you. And you walk. Ruined truck and all."

She stopped. The trestle threw its shadow across them both. Somewhere up on the road a car went by, oblivious, and its sound came down into the channel changed, hollow, the way sound comes across water that isn't there.

Toby looked at her for a long moment, and the nerve went out of his grin and left something older in it, and he wiped his hands again on the rag that would never get them clean and said, quiet, "You know what you just did?"

"I made you an offer."

"You *ran* me. You just ran me the way they run me. You worked out what I'm worth to you as a lever and you drove out to a ditch to pull it, and you did it in that voice, that county voice, like you're doing me a favor with a filing fee on it." He shook his head, and it was not angry, which was the worst of it — it was recognition, one professional to another. "I steal storms and I know exactly what I am. I make the widow's rain not come and there's no form for it and I don't pretend the form would've made it kinder. You — " he pointed the rag at her, at the frosted-glass-door primness of her standing in his dry river — "you're going to hand me to the auditor to save your county and your seal and you're going to tell yourself it was the Act. You're going to sleep tonight. That's the difference between us, Della. It was never the stamp. It's that you can sleep."

She did not answer that. There was no answer to it a person could give and keep her breakfast down, and she'd had that thought before, standing at her own counter, and it had been about Marvin then and it was about this now, and the fact that it fit both was the whole of what was wrong with her.

"Think about the offer," she said, and turned to go back up out of the channel.

"I don't have to think about it." Toby said it to her back. "I'll do it. Not because it's smart. Because you came down into the ditch to ask, and that means you're one of us now, and I'd rather go down owing a favor to somebody in the ditch than to the company up on the road." He picked up a wrench, and before he slid back under the truck he said the last thing, and he said it lightly, which was how the doomed said the hard things, the way Marvin said them looking at a dead barometer. "You want to know the tell? When they made me a runner, they told me there was nothing to sign."

Della stood very still in the bottom of the dry Kettle with the heat coming up through her boots.

*Nothing to sign.* The same three words the company had used on a man in a ditch that it used on a broker at a counter, the same soft hook baited up the road and down in the river both, and she stood there hearing it in a storm thief's mouth — a man who'd swallowed it years ago and was already, cheerfully, the thing she had spent all summer telling herself she would never become.

She climbed up out of the channel and drove back to the office under the apologetic blue, and Junie was at the ledger with the pen going up the clean column, and Della hung her coat with the chip in it on the hook and did not touch it, which was its own kind of touching, and she thought about how she had walked into the office that morning a woman who filed forms, and would walk out of it a woman who ran a runner and lied to an apprentice and let an auditor believe a courtesy was a confidence — and how not one line of any of it would ever appear in the book that Junie kept so carefully, because the record only recorded transfers, and there was no form, and no column, for what she had moved today.

"Anything come in?" she asked.

"One small one," Junie said, not looking up, her hand steady now, steadied by the thing Della had taught her at noon. "Sixty-one to twenty-two. Hollis moving his own rain off the fallow again. I logged it as clean."

"Good," Della said. "Log it as clean."


---

# Chapter 16 — Form 9-C: Interbasin Reassignment (Ruling) — MIDPOINT

> *Where the Act is silent, the market shall be presumed correct; and the broker's objection, once overruled, shall not be reasserted as to the same transfer.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §1(c)

The ruling came in on the feed at 8:41 on a Wednesday, the way rulings did now, as a header and a docket number and a word that meant a season. Junie read it out before Della had her coat off.

"Basin Authority. Ruling in the matter of the nine-C, Ridley Fork to upper Kettle." Junie's careful hand had stopped over the ledger, the pen lifted, a drop of ink hanging. "It says *lawful and authorized.*"

Della hung her coat and felt the chip swing in the right pocket against the wall and did not touch it. The dust was on the sill already. The sky over the elevator was the spent apologetic blue it had worn for six years, and it looked exactly as it had the day before — which was the horror of it, that a thing decided in a district office ninety miles away could change what a woman thirty miles east would haul in off her line, and the sky would not so much as flinch to mark it.

"Read the rest."

"Objections exhausted." Junie was reading fast now, the round hand forgotten, her thumb going down the screen. "The hydrology cure — they refiled, it's inside three water-years now. The affiliation flag — Holdings and Acquisitions filed the affirmation, one entity for the Act. The bond inquiry —" She stopped. "Bond found good. All three of the broker's grounds without merit. The delay is *terminated.*"

Three delays. Della had built them like a man builds a dam out of his own furniture, form by form, knowing the whole while there was more river than furniture. She had bought Cass five weeks of a *not yet* she had let herself, in the worst hours, call a *no.*

"Is that all of it?"

"No." Junie turned the screen so Della could see it, which meant she didn't want to be the one to say it. "There's a second part. Addressed to you. By name."

Della read it standing, one hand flat on the counter, her reading glasses still on their cord so that she held the screen out to the length of her arm, an old woman's distance. Two paragraphs. The first said the transfer was authorized and would proceed under Meridian's filed schedule. The second said the broker of record for Hale County — that was a phrase, *broker of record,* and it was her, and it was also her father, and it was also Junie in three years — would affix her seal to the executed 9-C within the statutory window, *or,* upon her refusal, the Authority would find her in dereliction under §2(h), appoint a bonded broker in her stead, and that broker would affix the seal, and the transfer would proceed, and her license would be suspended pending revocation.

She read it twice. The second time she read only the small word in the middle, the joint the sentence turned on. *Or.*

"So." Junie's voice had gone thin. "You sign it and it's over. Or you don't, somebody else signs it, and it's still over, and you're out." Della watched her arrive at the thing, watched it come up in her face the way weather comes up on a plain, from a long way off and then all at once. "Your no doesn't stop it anymore. It just decides who holds the pen."

"That's what it says."

"That can't be right." Junie said it the way you say a thing you haven't yet been burned by. "The broker's a *gate.* You always said. Without the seal it isn't legal—"

"The gate only works if there's one gate," Della said. "They found a second one. §2(h). Been in the Act since seventy-four. A broom for a broker who won't sweep." She set the screen down. "Log the ruling. Timestamp it. Window closes Friday week."

Junie logged it. Her hand had gone bad — the round careful column went crooked on the date, a slur in the seven — and Della watched the girl's whole belief in the record take that small wound and did not comfort it, because there was no comfort that was not a lie, and she was done handing the girl lies she'd have to unlearn in the flood.

She got through the morning. A deed pull, a bond confirmation, the Coyle widow in about buying back a week of the July she'd leased away, and could not — the work of any Wednesday, filed down small, and under it the ruling on the feed like a stone dropped in a dry well that has not yet found the bottom.

At noon she took the study out of the bottom drawer, from under the certified copies. Voss's feasibility study, the one he'd left five weeks ago and told her to keep, *I'll leave the study,* with the little courtesy of a man who has already read the last page of the book he is handing you. She had marked it DECLINED across the cover in her own hand. She laid it open now to the page she had not let herself look at long — the offset schedule, the parcel line, PRINE, C. & PRINE, W., the volume built to the acre-inch off a hundred and sixty acres of Ridley Fork bottomland, off a line strung between two posts where clean washing dried white and stiff because Ridley had kept its rain.

She was still looking at it when Marisol Reyes came in.

Reyes came the way she always came, without the bell seeming quite to have rung, cold in the dry heat with her collar up and her field barometer in its scuffed case slung across her body — the hand-crank unit she trusted over any wall needle in the county. She had the forensic look, a woman who had stopped smiling to be liked so long ago she'd forgotten it was a thing people did. She saw the study open on the counter and did not pretend she hadn't.

"You heard the ruling," Reyes said.

"At 8:41."

"They didn't waste the morning." Reyes set her case down on the counter, careful, the way she set everything down. "Send the girl to lunch, Della."

Della looked at her. Reyes did not send people to lunch. Reyes conducted her business in front of whoever was standing there and let the letter of it fall where it fell.

"Junie," Della said. "Go get something at the Trestle. Take an hour."

Junie understood she was being got out of a room, and took her coat, and went, and the bell rang her gone into the blue, and then it was the two of them and the dust on the sill and the dead needle at FAIR and the study open between them like a body on a table.

"Off the record," Reyes said.

"You don't say that."

"I'm saying it." Reyes uncrossed her arms, which was, for her, a violence. "Three weeks in the transfer files. The whole Ridley package. I went looking for whether your delays had merit — whether the broker's obstructing in the public interest or in her own." She let that sit half a second, and Della understood it was still a live question between them. "And I found the thing under the thing. The reason Meridian's paying to green up a dead county it has no earthly reason to love."

"Tell me."

"They don't want Hale watered," Reyes said. "They want Ridley dry."

Nothing in the room moved. Della kept her hand flat over the parcel line, over PRINE, C., and felt the old wrongness go up her wrist, the animal flinch, and kept the flat exact face she gave the counter. "Go on."

"The deluge for Hale is real," Reyes said. "That's what makes it clean on paper. They'll green Larkin, bring the east block home, run the pivots, Marvin Ott'll get his season. All true. All filed. And the offset comes off Ridley Fork, off your—" She stopped, and chose the word, and it was worse for being chosen. "Off the parcel. Lawful. Balanced to the decimal. Except the volume they filed isn't the volume that offsets Hale. It's more. It's built with a margin." She put one finger on the study, on a number three lines down that Della had read and filed and marked declined and not run all the way to its floor. "That margin runs Ridley Fork to within an inch of critical. One dry window, one bad delivery, one hot dome, and the whole basin drops below floor. And when a basin goes critical, Della, what happens to the allotments?"

"They collapse," Della said. Her mouth was dry. "The floor's gone, the deed-rights aren't worth the paper, the holders can't service the bonds—"

"A distressed-basin buyer picks them up for cents," Reyes said. "At critical. Cheap. Every acre-inch on Ridley Fork, out of the hands that kept it forty years, into the hands that engineered the drop. And there's a standing buyout order already lodged with the district — contingent, dated, waiting for the trigger. It's Meridian." She took her finger off the number. "The deluge was never the deal. It's the receipt they wave while they gut the county next door. Green one valley loud enough and nobody hears the other one die."

"You're sure."

"Sure of the numbers. The intent I can't file. But you asked me, and I'm telling you what I know, off the record, once, because—" Reyes did the thing she never did, which was hunt for a word that wasn't in the Act. "You're the only other person in two counties who understands the machine. I'd rather you understood it before you signed than after."

The shape of it turned over in Della slowly, the way a heavy thing turns in cold water, and when it had turned all the way she saw it was old. It was her father's shape. Kettle Basin had bought Hale's future forty years ago the same way, cheap off a dying basin, and Meridian was Kettle Basin in a better suit come back down the same road — except this time the basin they meant to run to nothing was the one their first buy had greened, the one the money had made safe, the one Cass breathed in. The company that had bought a county to save her sister was going to run her sister's county to critical to buy it again.

And the offered choice — *sign and save Hale, or refuse and let Hale die* — had never been the choice. Signing the 9-C did not save Cass's basin. It opened the door and held it while they came for it.

"The offer's a lie," Della said. It came out flat, filed, the exact voice, and under it something she did not let reach her face. "Not the deluge. The frame. *Your valley or your sister's* — there is no version where I sign this and Cass keeps her rain. Signing it is how they get to gut her."

"That's the part I can prove," Reyes said. "The volume runs her to critical. That's arithmetic. Whether Voss knows what the margin does—" For once there was something in the forensic face that was almost mercy and did not become it. "He knows. He does his homework. He knew whose parcel it was before he shook your hand."

Della stood with her hand on the study and the whole five-week war she'd fought with forms lying dead at her feet, and understood the thing her life had been keeping from her. She had thought she was the gate — that without her stamp it wasn't legal, that her refusal was a wall. It had never been a wall. It had been a *not yet* the machine had waited out, because the machine had all the time there was and a second door, and now it had walked through the second door and left her at the first one holding a pen and a fiction.

There was no neutral position. There never had been. Her father had at least chosen. He had picked up the pen and known what it cost and paid it and never once said sorry, because he wasn't. She had spent fifty-four years proud that she was not him — that she only filed, that filing was not doing. He signed. She let it happen and called her hands clean because someone else's were on the pen. That was worse. It was so much worse she could taste it, coppery, the taste of the tap water in the dark.

Reyes was watching her. "What are you going to do."

"I'm not going to sign it," Della said.

"Then they appoint a broker and it happens anyway and you're suspended by Friday."

"I know what §2(h) does."

"Then you know your refusal doesn't stop anything." Reyes said it not unkindly, the way you correct a person about the weather. "It only decides that it isn't your seal on it. That's not a wall, Della. That's a preference about whose name goes in the ledger."

"I know that too." Della closed the study slow, squared the pages, and put her hand flat on the cover where DECLINED stood in her own hand from five weeks ago, when she'd thought declining a thing was the same as stopping it. "Refusing to sign doesn't save Cass. Nothing I do at this counter saves Cass. That door's already open." She looked up. "But I'm not going to be the one holds it. If it happens, it happens over some kid the Authority sends who never met the woman under the sky. Not mine. Not with the seal my father pressed into my hand."

"That's sentiment," Reyes said. "That's you choosing your clean hands one more time. You're a smart enough woman to see it's the same clean hands that let it happen."

And that was the knife, and it went in where Reyes meant it to, because it was true — refusing the pen was one more way of keeping her hands out of it, one more *no* that changed nothing but the name in the record — and Della stood inside it and did not fall, because there was a thing she had learned in eleven years of not-drinking that the auditor did not know: that sometimes the only power a person has is to hold the specific thing, know exactly what it will do, and simply not do *this,* here, with her own arm, tonight. The refusal was not innocence and would not be mistaken for it, least of all by her. You did it anyway, one night at a time. It was the only *no* still hers to say.

"It's the same clean hands," Della agreed. "I'm not saying they're clean. I stopped saying that at 8:41." She took the ruling off the feed printer and set it beside the study. "I'm saying I won't put the seal down. They can have the license, they can have the chair, they'll appoint their broom and it'll go through and I'll have changed nothing but a name. And I'd rather change nothing than sign the thing that opens the door on my sister's rain and call it saving her." The flatness of her own voice did not comfort her. "Write it up. Dereliction under §2(h). Broker of record declines her seal. Suspend me."

Reyes did not move for a moment. She looked at Della the way she looked at a number that balanced on paper and did not, she was certain, balance in the world. Then she slung the field barometer across her body, careful, and looked once at the wall unit with its dead needle at FAIR, and said the thing she'd said the first day she walked in.

"That barometer isn't reading fair. It's stuck. You keep it up there like it's telling you something."

"It tells me something," Della said. "Tells me nothing's moved."

"Something's moved." Reyes put her hand on the door and did not open it yet. "The ruling's the thing that moves, Della. Not the needle. You've been reading the wrong instrument for six years." She opened the door and the dust and the flat blue came in around her. "I'll file the dereliction this afternoon. You'll have the suspension by Friday. Don't touch the seal before then. If you affix it in the window it's valid, and I can't unsuspend a valid seal, and you'll have handed them exactly what they want with your name on it. Once it's down there's no taking it up. There never is."

"I know what the seal does," Della said. "I've known since I was fourteen."

Reyes went out. The bell rang her gone.

Della stood alone with the ruling and the study side by side on the counter and the dead barometer and the safe behind her with the brass in it, her father's brass, forty years and her own twenty-two worn shallow at the rim. She did not go to the safe — not because she'd told Reyes she wouldn't touch the seal, though she had, but because whether she touched it or not the transfer was through the second door and moving, and the only thing left in her power was the name in the ledger and the shape of her own damnation. Those were not nothing. They were not enough. There was no third thing.

The bell went. Junie came back from the Trestle with a wax-paper packet she wasn't going to eat and stopped just inside the door with her coat half off, reading the room the way she was learning to, which was the one skill Della had truly taught her.

"The auditor's filing on you," Junie said. It wasn't a question. "You're not going to sign it."

"No."

"And it goes through anyway."

"Yes."

Junie stood there. She had believed in the Act the way you believe in a thing that hasn't burned you, and Della watched the last of that belief go — not all at once, not with any drama, just a needle coming off FAIR in a girl who'd trusted the instrument. Junie hung her coat on the hook next to Della's, the older and the younger, and the chip swung once in Della's pocket against the wall and was still.

"Then who signs it," Junie said quietly, "if they suspend you and the window's Friday week."

Della looked at the girl's hands, the ink on them, the fourth pair of ledger-hands the county had trained — Doss's and hers and now this careful crooked column that had slurred a seven when belief went out of it. She looked at the hands and then at the safe, and did the arithmetic the girl had not done yet, and it came up the way all the arithmetic in this office came up: balanced, clean, monstrous, meeting at the third decimal.

"There's one other bonded seal in this county," Della said.

Junie followed her eyes to the safe, and understood, and went white.

"You'd be the broker of record," Della said, "the day they suspend me."


---

# Chapter 17 — Form 7-R: Broker's Bond (Revocation, Pending)

> *Suspension does not vacate prior seals; nor shall it excuse the office from operation, the county from its broker, or the record from its keeping.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, Reg. 7-R.3

The letter came by courier on the Friday, the way the endings came now, in a stamped envelope a boy in a district-fleet truck set on the counter and asked her to initial for, and she initialed for it, because you signed for the thing that ended you the same as you signed for anything else. Della Okafor-Prine, Broker of Record, Hale County. She read the two paragraphs standing, glasses out at arm's length. The first said the Authority had found her in dereliction under §2(h) and suspended her license effective on receipt, revocation pending a hearing to be scheduled. The second said the seal of the office was suspended with the licensee but the office was not, that a bonded broker would be designated to serve Hale during the pendency, and that the ledger, the safe, and the county's active files were to remain in the office and in working order, the record being the property of the Act and not of the broker.

She read the last of it twice. *The record being the property of the Act and not of the broker.* Forty years of her father's hand and twenty-two of her own, and it had never once belonged to either of them. They had only kept it, the way you keep a fire you did not light.

"Do I sign that too," Junie said. She had come around the counter and was standing close, reading it upside down, which she was good at now.

"No. It signs me." Della set it flat and squared the corners out of habit, the way she squared everything. "You initial the courier slip. That's the county receiving it. Timestamp it."

Junie timestamped it. Her hand had steadied since Wednesday — it does, Della thought; the first cut is the one that shakes you; after that the pen learns the wound — and the round careful column came out true across the date. Then Junie stopped, the pen still down, and said the thing she had not said since the room went white on Wednesday.

"They'll designate me. Won't they." She said it flat, reading it out the way you read a ruling off the feed. "There's no other bonded seal in the county, and I've got a probationary card — which does exactly one thing without a full license. It seals what a designated supervising broker directs. So they'll send someone to run the desk, and he'll hand me the nine-C, and he'll direct me, and I'll put the seal down. My hand. My column."

"Maybe."

"Not maybe. You did the arithmetic Wednesday. I watched you do it." She was not crying. Della would almost rather she had cried. "You spent five weeks building a dam out of your own furniture so it wouldn't be your name in the ledger. And it's going to be mine instead. That's the whole thing you bought. You bought it being me."

There was no answer to that that was not a lie, so Della gave her the true thing instead, which was worse.

"Yes," she said.

Junie picked up the day's certified-copy queue and went to work at the ledger, because the office was not suspended, only the woman, and someone had to keep the record the Act owned. Della watched her a moment. The fourth pair of ledger-hands — Doss's, then hers, now this careful column bending itself to a wound, learning what her own had learned at fourteen: that the work does not stop for the person doing it, that the column goes on down the page whether or not the hand believes in it, and that this is not a comfort but will pass for one if you let it.

She took her coat off the hook. The chip came with it, a small round weight in the right pocket, and she did not touch it.

"Lock the safe when you go," she said. "The seal stays where it is. Neither of us touches it until they send you paper that says to. Somebody comes in with a badge and a directive, you call me before you open that safe, and if you can't reach me you read the directive twice and then a third time, and you make them wait while you do — because making them wait is the only power the desk has left, and it is not nothing." She heard her father in it, word for word, *you do not leave the seal where a want can reach it,* and let it stand, because it was true even out of his mouth. "You hear me, Junie."

"I hear you." The girl did not look up. "You're not coming back tomorrow."

"No."

"Then who tells me what to make them wait for."

Della had no answer for that either, and she went out into the blue, and the bell rang her gone from her own office for the first time in twenty-two years.

---

The Sump was three doors down and across, on the shaded side of Main, and she had not been inside it in eleven years and she stood in front of it now the way you stand in front of a barometer, checking a dead thing for a pulse out of a habit older than hope.

The door was propped with a cinderblock against the heat. Inside was the dark she remembered, the long dark of a bar at four in the afternoon, cool the way the office was cool, cellar-cool, the only cool in town that did not cost a pivot's worth of power to make. She could smell it from the sidewalk — the beer-soaked wood, the cigarette ghost in the paneling, the sweetish under-thing that was every spilled year the boards had drunk. Her body knew the smell before her mind named it. Her mouth knew it. There was a specific thirst that lived behind her back teeth and had lived there for eleven years, patient as an allotment, and it woke now and put its face to the glass.

She had lost the office. That was the plain fact, filed. She had lost the one power she'd had, small and absolute, a gate — and the gate had turned out to have a second door in it since the Act was written, and the market had walked through it with all the time there was, and left her out here with a suspended license and a chip in her right coat pocket that said *no* on a face worn soft by eleven years of saying it.

The whole trick of staying sober, she had told herself ten thousand nights, was that you could hold a thing in your hand and know exactly what it would do to you and simply not do it, one night at a time. She had believed the trick was a wall. Standing in the Sump doorway she saw it had never been a wall. It was a *not yet* — a *no* she renewed every morning because the wanting did not go away, only waited, patient as a company, patient as her father. Eleven years was not a fortress. It was a bond she paid daily, and one missed payment and it was through the second door.

She had run her whole life on the same physics she enforced. She was a gate. There was no wall. There was only the daily seal, or its refusal, and the refusal decided nothing but whose name was on the day.

She did not go in.

She stood there long enough to know it was a choice and not an accident, long enough that a man she half knew came up behind her wanting his afternoon and had to say *scuse me, Della* and edge past her into the dark. She stepped onto the hot side and let him by and turned the chip over once in her pocket, around and around, the way a tongue works a sore tooth, and did not go in.

It was not a victory. It was not innocence and would not be mistaken for either, least of all by her. It was only the one *no* still hers to say, and she said it, and it changed nothing but the day. Then she walked to her truck and drove east out of Ainsley Hale toward the dry Kettle, because there was a *yes* she had decided to go say, and it was the worst one of her life, and it was the only one left that might change more than a name.

---

The BOAT LAUNCH sign pointed down its ramp into the gray pan the way it always had, and she drove past it and did not slow, because she was going where the water wasn't and the sign had never pointed anywhere else. Past the launch the road left the top of the channel and the channel deepened and turned, a dry cut in the plain with bleached driftwood snagged in the cracks of it, and half a mile down where the old Larkin overflow had scoured a hollow big enough to hide a thing in, Toby Renn's grain truck sat in the shade of the cutbank with its nose pointed out, the way a runner parks, the way a man parks who has learned to leave.

She left her own truck up on the lip and walked down into the channel. The ground changed under her boots from plain to riverbed, from dust to something older, a fine gray silt that had been mud once and remembered it, and the walls of the cut rose on either side and took the town and the sky down to a strip, and she felt the change in her chest that the men here felt at the cession fence — a hum that wasn't quite a sound, ozone and hot metal, the wrongness the birds refused to nest inside of. There were no birds. She'd noticed that the first time. There were no birds in a quarter mile of Toby Renn.

He was sitting on the running board eating something out of a can with a folding spoon and he watched her come down the whole way and did not get up.

"Well," he said. "The gate walked down into the ditch." He set the can on the running board beside him. "I heard. Whole county heard by noon. Della Okafor-Prine, suspended, license pending. Twenty-two years." He shook his head, and the grin was there but it was a runner's grin, mostly nerve. "They finally routed all the way around you. I'm sorry. I mean that. You were the last honest gate in three counties and I made my living going through you and I'm still sorry."

"You didn't go through me. You went past me. There's a difference."

"Is there." He looked genuinely to be considering it. "You file it, I run it, the rain still leaves somebody. I never could find the difference and you never could show it to me." He nudged the can with his boot. "But you didn't come down into a riverbed with no birds in it to argue theology with me. You came down here to ask me something you can't ask up on Main." He spread his hands, grease in the creases, the same permanent dirt Marvin had, ground gone into a man past washing. "So ask it. I've been down here two years waiting for the honest people to run out of honest. Ask it."

She stood in the dry channel with the walls taking the sky down to a seam, and she asked it, in the flat exact voice, the voice she gave the counter, because the counter was the one voice of hers the wanting had never gotten into.

"Meridian's going to run Ridley to critical," she said. "The nine-C's not the deal. The deal's the buyout — they green Hale loud enough that nobody hears them gut the basin next door, they pull Ridley below floor, they buy the collapsed allotments for cents. There's a standing order already lodged. I can't stop it legally. My license is gone and my noes are used up and the transfer's authorized and the window's open." She heard herself say the rest of it, the thing she had driven half a mile down into a lawless hole to say, and her wrist ached where the stamp had lived for twenty-two years, ached for a brass thing she had left in a safe she was no longer allowed to open. "So I want to out-steal them. I want you to pull a storm to Hale off-book — a controlled one, enough to green Larkin and break their economic logic, so there's no dead basin to buy and no reason to run Ridley under. If Hale's already got its rain, the whole play falls apart. No nine-C. No seal. I steal Hale's water instead of signing it."

Toby did not say anything for a moment. He picked the can up and set it down again. When he spoke the grin was gone entirely and what was under it was the look of a man who has already stood where you are standing and knows what's at the bottom.

"Off-book," he said. "Controlled. A storm big enough to green a dead reservoir and break a company." He nodded slowly. "You know what a storm that size is, in acre-inches. You've filed a thousand of them. That's not a pull. That's a heist. And a pull that size doesn't come off *nowhere,* Della — conservation of storms, you taught it to me your own self, from the wrong side of a counter. It's got to come off a basin that has it to give." He looked up at her, up the gray wall of the cut and back down, and did not name it. "You know the basins that can bleed a whole valley and not scream for a week. You've had the acre-inches on your terminal all summer. Go home and count the ones inside my truck's reach that fit. I'll wait. I'm not going to be the one that says it. This is your ditch now, not mine."

She had known. She had known it walking down into the channel, the way you know a shape on the horizon before it has a name, and had come down anyway, had made herself not-know it, had let it lie where it lay in the dry bed with the other things the water left when it went. She had come down into a riverbed with no birds in it to ask a thief to steal her a storm, and driven half a mile into a lawless hole precisely so a man other than herself would say whose sky it hung over — and he would not say it. He was leaving it in her mouth. That was the mercy a thief had for her, and it was worse than cruelty, because it made her the one who had to know.

"There's got to be another one," she said. It came out steady and it was not steady. "Farther. West of the fork. If distance decays it, you go the long way and take less."

"You go home and look," Toby said, gentle, which was worse. "You look at the map you've had memorized twenty-two years, and you count them honest, and if there's a second one I'll pull off it and gladly. But we both know how many fit, and I've watched you drive out here around a hole in the road for two months so you wouldn't have to." He stood up off the running board, slow, not threatening her, only near. "There's no clean basin, Della. There's just the one you can't see the faces in. Up on Main you couldn't see them, so you called it neutral. But you can see these. That's the whole of your trouble, and no map's going to fix it for you tonight."

The channel was very quiet. The hum went on in her fillings. Somewhere above the seam of sky the flat apologetic blue held over Hale, and thirty miles east under the same afternoon a woman she had not spoken to in eleven years was hanging washing white and stiff and clean on a line strung between two posts, and the only way Della had found to keep her sister's sky was a company gutting it with a form and a thief lifting it without one, and both roads ran to the same line, and the woman under it had Della's own hands.

"I'll think about it," Della said, which was the first time in eleven years she had said the sentence she used to say to a bottle.

Toby heard it for what it was. She saw him hear it. He picked the can back up and looked at her over it, and the grin came back, the nerve of it, the doom of it, a young-old man in a dead river who had already run out of honest and knew exactly the sound a person made running out.

"That's the sentence, all right," he said. "That's the exact one. Welcome down, Della. Watch your footing on the way up — the bank's soft where the water used to hit it. It always is, right where the water used to be."

She climbed back up out of the channel, and the bank was soft where the water used to hit it, and it took her boot, and she caught herself with a hand in the old silt that had been mud and remembered it, and stood a moment on the lip with the gray of it drying on her palm — the color the wash went on her mother's line, on every line in Hale before you'd finished hanging it, the color, she understood with her hand full of a dead river, a thing turns the exact moment you decide there's no clean version of saving it.

She wiped her hand on her coat and it left a gray print there, over the pocket where the chip was, and she did not clean it off. She got in the truck. Above her the sky held its apology. Thirty miles east it was still, for a few days more, someone else's to trust.

She had not said yes. She wanted that on the record, in whatever ledger the future kept — the one that was the property of the Act and not the broker. She had not said yes.

She had said *I'll think about it,* which she had learned eleven years ago was the same thing wearing a coat.


---

# Chapter 18 — Form 31: Report of Suspected Diversion (Second Instance)

> *A second diversion in a basin shall be presumed intentional, and the presumption shall not be rebutted by the diverter's ignorance of the origin.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §13(d)

The Kettle at high summer had a smell the dry channel had never had before the drought — not water, the opposite of water, a mineral flatness like the inside of an old kettle scrubbed and left, hot dust and rust and the ghost of a river cooked out of its own bed. Della came down the ramp on foot with the truck left up on the road, past the BOAT LAUNCH sign whose arrow had rusted to the color of the pan it pointed at, and the heat came up off the concrete through her boots the way it came up off everything now, from below, as if the ground had its own weather and had decided to keep it. She had turned in nothing yet. The bond was still hers on paper, suspended pending, a license in a drawer at the district that no longer let her stamp anything but was not, until Friday's filing cleared, technically revoked. She was still a broker. She had come down into the channel to stop being one.

Toby's grain hauler sat in the deepest cut of the old riverbed where the banks would hide it from the road, nosed in under a lean of dead cottonwoods, and around it the air did the thing she had felt at the cession station and had not felt anywhere else: a hum she took in her fillings before her ears, a low tooth-ache of a sound with no source, and under it the ozone-and-hot-metal reek that made the back of her throat close. No bird sat in the dead cottonwoods. Birds knew. Birds had known before there was an Act to write it down.

"You came," Toby said. He was up on the running board of the truck doing something to a fitting she made herself not look at, because looking at it was the one thing she had promised herself she would not do — not out of squeamishness, out of law, because a broker who could describe the mechanism was a broker who could testify to it, and she was still, for four days, a person who might be made to testify. "I figured you for a woman who'd think about it a week and not come. You came in two days. That's the drought talking. The drought's a hell of a closer."

"They suspended me Wednesday," Della said. "Files Friday. I've got a window and no seal to fill it with. So I'm here."

"You're here." He came down off the running board and wiped his hands on a rag that did not make them cleaner and stood in the hum, and in the flat white light he looked younger and older at once, the way he had in the spring, a man of thirty-one with a runner's leanness and a runner's flinch and a sunburn gone to leather at the neck. "Say the rest of it. I like to hear a broker say it out loud. You people never say it out loud. You file it."

"I want you to pull a storm to Hale." The words came out in the flat exact voice, the counter voice, and she gave it to herself as much as to him. "Off the books. No debit, no offset, no 9-C, no seal — nothing for Meridian to route through, nothing for the Authority to rule on, because there won't be a form to rule on. Enough to green Larkin bottoms and carry Marvin Ott's alfalfa through the season. Enough to break the arithmetic that says Hale needs Meridian. If Hale doesn't need the deluge, the offset doesn't get filed, and the storm doesn't come off Ridley." She heard herself and did not flinch and hated that she did not. "I file suspected diversions for a living. Now I want to buy one."

Toby whistled low, one note, appreciative, the way Web might whistle at a well-set fence.

"There it is," he said. "The broker wants a bank robbery. And she wants it clean." He grinned, and the grin had the old delight in it, the delight of a man who has waited all his life for the respectable person to come down into the ditch and admit the ditch was where the water was. "See, that's the part I can't do for you. The clean part. I can pull you a storm. I can't pull you a clean one. There's no clean rain, Della. You taught me that. You just wrote it down neater than I say it."

"I don't need it clean. I need it survivable." She stepped closer than she wanted to, into the worst of the hum, and made herself hold there. "You dropped point-three on a dead cornfield in the spring and it greened up and told on itself. You dropped point-six off the north townships nineteen days later and Reyes read it in an afternoon. Two diversions in one basin, Toby. You know what §13(d) does with a second one? It stops presuming accident. It presumes you *meant* it. Every pull you've made this year has drawn the Authority tighter around this channel, and the next one lands on a county under Critical Watch with an auditor already living in the Trestle. If we do this it has to come off a basin with margin. Somewhere fat. Somewhere that can lose the volume and not go under and not go noticed for a week." She was a broker to the end of her hands; she laid it out like a transfer, plus against minus, meeting at the decimal. "Give me a basin that can spare it and I'll take the diversion and I'll live with it. I only need one that can spare it."

Toby stopped wiping his hands.

He did not answer right away, and in the not-answering she felt the channel's heat and the tooth-ache hum and the far dry crack of the pan settling, and she watched him do the arithmetic she'd watched him do in the spring, some ledger of his own she couldn't read, about what he owed and to whom, and this time it went on longer, and when it broke it broke wrong. He did not grin. He looked up the channel, east, the way the dead river ran when it had run, toward thirty miles of nothing that ended in green.

"There's exactly one," he said. "You already know which one. You've known since spring. You've been driving out here around it for two months like it's a hole in the road."

"Name a different one."

"There isn't a different one, Della, that's what I'm telling you. You want fat, you want margin, you want a basin that can bleed a whole valley's worth of rain and not go critical and not scream for a week — there's one basin in a hundred miles that fits and it's the Ridley Fork, and you know it fits because you've had the acre-inches on your terminal since the day Meridian slid the study across your counter." He said it plainly, without cruelty, which was worse than cruelty. "I offered you Ridley in the spring off the top of my head, out of a basin I didn't know from Adam, because it was the fattest sky in the county and it hung its washing out white and never looked up. I didn't know whose it was. You did. You stood in this channel and let me offer to rob your sister and you never said a word, you just took a long time saying no." He tipped his head. "So don't come down here asking me for a clean basin like there's a stranger's rain sitting somewhere you haven't already looked. Conservation of storms. You wrote the report a thousand times. Somebody's always downwind. There's no clean basin. There's just the one you can't see the faces in — and you can see every face in that one. That's your whole problem. That's the only problem you've got left."

The channel was very quiet under the hum.

She had come down here to escape the shape and the shape had followed her down. That was the thing about the shape; it was not a thing that lived in the 9-C or in Voss's study or in the Authority's ruling; it lived in the physics, in the one law, in the fact that you could not make a drop of rain and could only carry it off one roof and set it on another, and the fattest roof in a hundred miles of dying was her sister's. Meridian had not built the trap. Toby had not built it. Her father had not built it, though he'd walked her into it forty years ago with his hand on the block. It had been in the sky since before the Act, waiting, the way the dry channel had been waiting under the water the whole time the water ran. She had thought the illegal path went *around* the trap. It went through the center of it. It went through the exact same door, and left the seal off the paperwork, and called that a difference.

"There's got to be another way to figure it," she said, and heard how her own voice had changed, and hated it — the small ugly note of a person trying to buy back a fact with wanting. "A dozen small pulls off a dozen basins. Spread the loss out. Nobody feels a tenth of an inch."

"A tenth of an inch a dozen times is a diversion a dozen times," Toby said. "You want to trade one big theft for twelve small warrants. And distance decays it — the rain bends, it doesn't jump, I pull from what's close and full or I pull from nothing. Close and full is Ridley. It's always been Ridley. You didn't come down here to find that out. You came down here hoping I'd tell you a lie you could sign." He wiped his hands again, slow, and there was no delight left in him at all now, and that absence was the most frightening thing she'd seen since the ruling. "I'm out of lies, Della. That's the one thing this life left me."

She looked at him then, really looked, past the grin that wasn't there anymore, and saw what she had come too focused to see: that a man who had laughed at her in the spring, delighted, certain, easy in his own crime, was not laughing now, and that the change had a cause, and that she had been so busy walking herself into the ditch she had not asked the ditch how it was doing.

"Toby," she said. "Why me. Why now. You've been running this channel for years — you told me you ran a load past my father and he never caught it. You don't need a suspended county broker to sign off on a theft. There's nothing to sign." The three words came out of her mouth and she felt them land wrong even as she said them, felt the floor of the conversation shift, because they were not her words, they were Voss's words, the fountain-pen words on the cream card under the ledger corner in the spring — *nothing to sign* — and she watched them land on Toby and watched Toby flinch the way she flinched at the stamp, up from the wrist, closer to the bone.

"Say that again," Toby said, quiet.

"Nothing to sign."

He laughed, but it was the north-townships laugh now, the one that had stopped being for anybody. He climbed back up onto the running board, not to work the fitting, just to put the height of the truck between them, a man getting behind a counter of his own.

"You want to know why now." He looked down at her, and the ozone came off the truck in a wave she felt in her teeth. "Because I don't run for myself anymore. Haven't since last fall. A man runs enough storms, he gets into the kind of debt that doesn't take a truck for payment, and the kind of people who buy that debt aren't the kind you file a 31 on. They've got a suit. They've got good stationery." He let it sit, watching her get there, patient as a form, patient the way she'd been patient with Marvin, giving her the dignity of the sentence. "The pulls this spring? The point-three, the point-six? I didn't sell those to a widow, Della. I was told where to pull and told where to drop. I run a route now. I'm somebody's truck." He tapped the fitting she would not look at, once, with a knuckle. "You've been fighting a company all season with your little delays and your bond irregularities, and the company's had the only storm-runner in the county on a string since October. Every diversion you filed a 31 on — you were reporting your own enemy's work and calling it a stranger's. They own the legal path. They own me. There was never a way around them, because the way around them was *me,* and I've already been bought."

The hum went on. The dead cottonwoods held no birds. Somewhere up on the road the truck ticked, cooling, though nothing in the channel was cool.

Meridian owned the runner. She stood at the bottom of the dry Kettle and let the fact go down her the way the second delivery had gone down Reyes's field barometer, on a schedule, a thing lowered on a rope. The loophole was not a loophole. It was a longer hallway back into the same room. If she took Toby's storm to save Hale, she robbed her sister off the books — with Meridian's own instrument, on Meridian's string — and the moment the imbalance showed in Ridley Fork it would be a *second* diversion in that basin, presumed intentional under §13(d), untraceable to a company that had bought its deniability by the yard and traceable, if it traced to anyone, to a suspended broker with a grievance and a sister on the downwind side. She had come to out-steal them. There was nothing to out-steal. They had already stolen the thief.

"I file a 31 on you again," she said slowly, "and it's a second instance. Presumed intentional. Only now I know it's not you who meant it. You're the truck. And I can't put in a report that says *Meridian meant it* because there's no form for a company, there's only a form for the hole and the man standing nearest it, and that's you." She was thinking out loud, the broker's habit, laying plus against minus in a channel where nothing balanced. "If I do this with you, I'm running the diversion. If I *report* this, I'm handing the Authority the man who does the company's stealing — and the man goes down and the company writes it off and hires the next truck, and Ridley still bleeds, only now on Meridian's timeline instead of mine." She looked up at him on the running board. "There's no move here that isn't your grave or my sister's sky. Or both."

"Now you're a runner," Toby said. "Welcome down. Took your daddy years to figure the ditch out. He never came down into it — that was his one virtue, he did it all up on the road where it was legal. You did it in a season." He said it without malice, almost with affection, the way you'd tell a woman her father had a good arm. Then the affection went out of it, and what was left was the flat, clear, doomed thing that was the truest part of him. "I'll pull it if you want it. Off Ridley, because there's no other Ridley. I'll do it in the window. But hear me before you nod, because I'm the only man in your life who tells you true — the second you say yes, it's not a report anymore. It's a plan. And the sky over your sister's washing goes the color yours has worn for six years, three-tenths of an inch at a time, on a schedule, arriving like the freight train arrives, and you'll have set it running with your mouth the way you used to set it running with your hand. Same rain, Della. You just took the stamp off it. Same rain."

She did not say yes. She stood in the dry Kettle in the hum with the dead cottonwoods over her and the pan cracking in the heat and did the thing she had built a whole sober life on doing — held the specific thing in her mind, knew exactly what it would do, and did not, this second, do it. The chip was a small round weight in her right coat pocket and she did not reach for it, which was its own kind of reaching. Eleven years of *no,* worn soft. She had learned to keep from picking up the thing that would ruin her by simply not closing her hand. She had never once been asked to keep from *setting down* the thing that would save her, and she stood there and understood that these were not the same muscle, had never been the same muscle, and that the second one had never been tested and might not hold.

"I'll tell you Thursday," she said, which was not a no, and both of them heard that it was not a no, and both of them — the thief on the string and the broker off the leash — wrote it down in whatever ledger each of them kept, timestamped, un-take-back-able.

She went up the ramp out of the channel and left the truck's hum behind her a foot at a time, and the tooth-ache in her fillings faded, and the ozone let go of her throat, and up on the road the world was only dry again, only hot, the flat apologetic blue holding over the elevator that held no grain. She stood on the top of the bank with her boots in the dust the river had left and looked east down the dead line of the Kettle, toward the fork she could not see, toward the green she could not see, toward a line strung between two posts thirty miles off where a woman she had not spoken to in eleven years was hanging washing out white and stiff and clean into a sky she had not yet learned had a buyer, and a runner, and a suspended broker standing over the dry bed of the same river with the word *Thursday* in her mouth like a coin she had not yet decided which way to spend.

Her wrist ached where the stamp would have gone. There was no stamp. There was, for four more days, nothing to sign — and she stood in the channel her father had never come down into and understood, at last and too late, that *nothing to sign* had never once meant nothing to do.


---

# Chapter 19 — Form 3-A: Allotment of Record (Cassandra Prine)

> *The register does not record intent; it records only the transfer, the date, and the hand that made it.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, Reg. 3-A.1

The certified copy she pulled that morning was Cass's, and she had not gone looking for it.

She'd gone into the archive for a Section 12 fraction on the Delp place, back where the old brown ledgers stood in their county-colored leather, and her hand had found the wrong volume in the dust-dim the way a tongue finds the tooth it's been told to leave alone. Ridley Fork allotments, cross-filed to the Hale office from the years before the split, when the two counties shared a river and a registrar. PRINE, C. & PRINE, W. An inch and a hundredth of honest bottomland rain, old, severable and never severed, and beside the volume the register said what it always said, which was nothing — a box for the acre-inches, a box for the date, a box for the seal, and no box at all for why. Della stood with the book open in the back of a suspended broker's office — her license pending, her seal a thing she was forbidden now to bring down — and looked at her sister's rain in a clean column, and thought: *somebody is going to take this, and it will go in the ledger without comment, and that is the only mercy the ledger has — that it will not say what it was for.*

She closed the book. She did not close it hard. She had learned this summer that the sound a closing ledger made was worse than the stamp, softer and more final, and she'd gotten so she closed them slow to keep from hearing it.

Then she drove east, and did not tell Junie where.

---

She crossed the Kettle at the county line a little before noon, and it was the same as it had been in the spring and it was worse, because now she knew what she knew. On the Hale side the channel was a gray cracked pan with the BOAT LAUNCH arrow rusting down its ramp into dust. On the Ridley side the current ran — brown, unhurried, ordinary — the same river, one side robbed and one side not, thirty feet of failing bridge between a dead bed and a living one. She slowed on the span the way she always slowed, and did not examine why, and looked at the running water and thought about how it would look when it stopped.

Because it was going to stop. Either road led here — legal or stolen, Meridian's hand or her own, the storm came off Ridley because Ridley was where the storm was, and conservation of storms meant somebody thirsted, and the somebody had her sister's face. She had decided at three that morning, holding the chip and not drinking, that the one thing left in her power was to warn Cass before it happened instead of after — even though there was no version of the truth that did not also read as a confession. She turned off the highway onto the bottom road and the alfalfa came up green on both sides of her, drying in windrows, the honest green smell of a county that had kept its rain coming in the window like a thing she was not allowed to have.

The farm was where it had always been. The white house, the dairy barn, the pump shed. The line strung between two posts in the side yard where Cass hung washing because she could.

Della stopped the truck at the top of the drive and did not get out yet, because of the line.

The washing was up — sheets and a row of Web's shirts and the dish towels, pinned oldest to the sun the way Cass had always pinned them. But it was not white. It was not the stiff bright flag-white it had been in the spring, the white dust had never gotten into. It hung a little heavy, a little dull, gone the faint gray of a wash brought in too many times, and above the line the Ridley sky — the deep wet confident blue she had envied at the county seam in April, the blue on Voss's card — had a thinness in the west, a flatness coming up over the far ridge like a stain lifting into paper, the first pale edge of a color Della knew in her own teeth. Apologetic. Moving east. The sky over her sister's line had begun, one demonstration at a time, to learn how to apologize.

Cass came out onto the porch before Della had the door of the truck open.

She stood at the rail with her arms crossed, broad and brown and gone gray at the temple, and watched Della come up the drive without a word or a step to meet her, and the resemblance between them stood in the dooryard like a third woman neither of them would look at.

"You didn't call ahead," Cass said.

"No."

"Web's at the co-op. Iris took the books to town." Cass said it flat, an inventory, telling her who wasn't here to soften it. "So it's just us. That's a first, in eleven years."

"I know how long it's been."

"I don't imagine you drove thirty miles to tell me you can count."

---

*He had gone down slow, at the end, over a spring, and she had been the one at the house because she was the one always at the house, the durable one, the strong-lunged one, kept in the dry place for exactly this.*

*The office had already been half hers — she'd been broker two decades — but the stamp still lived in his safe at the home place, the brass he'd never handed over because handing it over was admitting a thing he did not admit. That last week she sat by the bed with a glass in her hand more often than not; this was before, the thing she got sober from right after, when the whiskey still lied the way it lied — that she was managing, that a person could pour to the same line every evening and call it holding still.*

*He was clear at the end. Doss was always clear. Clarity was the one grammar he had.*

*"You'll take the office proper now," he said. Not a question. His hand on the coverlet was the hand from the ledger, the big exact hand, gone to paper. "The girl at the county'll want to reappoint. You let her. You're bonded, you're clean, there's no reason."*

*"There's the reason there always is," Della said. "That I don't want it."*

*"Wanting it was never the qualification." He turned his head on the pillow. "You think I wanted forty years of standing at a counter telling people the price of the sky true so they'd at least know what they were losing when they lost it. It's not a thing a man wants. It's a thing he does because he can survive doing it. Same as you. That's not a curse, Della. That's what you're for."*

*She had heard it her whole life in one shape or another, and had never once heard him say sorry for the shape of it, and did not hear it now.*

*"Cass called," she said. "She's not coming till the end."*

*Something moved in the ruined face that in another man would have been grief and in Doss was only accounting. "She won't forgive me. The Ridley buy. Forty years and she never has — thinks I sold a county to save a girl." He stopped there, at the edge of it, and did not walk out onto the rest, and Della, at the bedside with the glass going warm behind her, felt the rest waiting in the room the way you feel a pressure drop before the delivery, a whole further thing he could have said and chose, even now, even dying, to leave in the safe. His eyes found hers and held. "You'll want to know why it was you kept here and not her. You'll ask it your whole life. Don't ask it in this room. I haven't got the days to answer it right, and you haven't got the sober to hear it."*

*"You kept me here to run your office."*

*"I kept you here because you could be kept." He let that stand, flat, unpretty, and did not soften it or extend it, and the not-extending was its own black weight. Then he reached past her hand to the drawer of the bedside table and took out the stamp — heavy brass, the seal worn shallow, forty years of it — and pressed it into her palm and closed her fingers over it with his ruined ones, the way you close a child's hand around a coin so it won't drop it in the street.*

*"You do not leave the seal where a want can reach it," he said, the old drill, and let go.*

*She had taken it. That was the thing. He had told her plain that he'd sold Hale to keep one daughter, and not-told her the harder figure under it — the one he set at the edge of the bed and would not name, the one she'd spend eleven years walking toward the way she walked toward the Sump door — and she had opened her hand and taken the stamp anyway and carried it back and put it in the safe and turned the dial, and had not understood, would not understand for a long time yet, that the taking was the transfer. That in that room, with a glass going warm, she had signed for the office and the ledger and whatever the unspoken figure was, all in one motion, without comment, and the register of it, had there been one, would have recorded only the date and the hand.*

---

"Come in or don't," Cass said. "I'm not going to stand out here and be looked at."

The kitchen was cool and smelled of coffee and cut alfalfa and, under it, the deep animal smell of the dairy that got into a farm's every room. Cass did not offer coffee. She stood at the counter with her hands flat on either side of her, and Della saw the dirt worn permanent into the creases the way it was in Marvin's, in her own — the Prine hands over a different county's counter — and thought she had better say it fast, before the kitchen made her a guest.

"Meridian Sky is coming for your rain," Della said. "The allotment on this place, the whole inch and a hundredth. There's a transfer that pulls it off Ridley Fork to green up Hale, and the volume they filed runs your basin to within an inch of critical, and once it's critical they've got a buyout order lodged and waiting — they take the collapsed allotments cheap and this county goes the way mine went. That's the shape of it. I came to tell you before it happens instead of after."

Cass did not move for a moment. She was a woman who did not perform anything, least of all surprise, and she took the sentence the way she took weather, from a long way off and then all at once, and her face did not break. It set.

"How long have you known," Cass said.

"Since the spring."

"Since the spring." Cass turned that over. "And you drove out here in April with a notice in your hand about an objection window and you stood in my yard and you said not one word of this."

"I couldn't."

"You couldn't." The flatness went thinner, went to an edge. "You've been the county broker my whole life. You knew in the spring a company was going to run me dry, and you filed your little forms about it for four months, and you come out here in July to *warn* me." She said the word like a thing spoiled in the back of the icebox. "That's not a warning. A warning's a thing you give in time to do something. This is a receipt."

"It's late and it's the truth and it's all I've got."

"Then tell me the rest." Cass came off the counter, one step, and the hands came up and crossed again, the guard she'd built forty years ago and never once put down. "You didn't drive out here to tell me a company I've never met is going to dry me out. You'd have mailed that. You'd have let some kid at the Authority mail it. You drove." She looked at her sister the way Reyes looked at a number that balanced on paper and did not balance in the world. "Something's already wrong with my sky and you know what it is."

Della said nothing. Out the window over Cass's shoulder the line hung in the side yard, the dull sheets not moving, the thin stain of the wrong blue coming up over the west ridge, and she thought: *she's already smelled it.* Of course she had. Cass had breathed weather her whole life, had nearly died of the lack of it, had a cough deep in her that was a receipt for the exact thing happening again. She would know a bent front the way Della knew a stuck needle.
