"Two weeks ago," Cass said, slow now, reading Della's face the way the men here read a barometer, "we had rain come in on a Tuesday that wasn't right. Web'll tell you. Straight line off the west, hard, wet the near forty and quit at the fence like somebody'd drawn it with a ruler. Barometer dropped off the wall Monday night and the dog wouldn't come off the porch. And now the tank on the Fork's down four inches with no draw on it, nothing to account for it, and the county man says he'll look into it." She had not raised her voice. She never raised her voice. "I've lived in the one green county in this whole dry country for forty years, and I know exactly what it looks like when a place starts to lose its rain, because I grew up watching it happen to yours. And it's happening to mine. And you knew before you came."

"Cass—"

"Is it you?"

The kitchen was very quiet. The dairy hum came up through the floor and the sheets did not move on the line and the wrong blue kept lifting into the west, and Cass Prine stood in her own clean kitchen with her arms crossed over the guard and asked her sister the only question that had ever mattered between them, in four words, in the plain grammar their father had left them both.

"Is it you," Cass said again, not louder, laying it down flat and exact, the way you lay down the card that ends the hand. "You're the broker. Somebody stamps a piece of paper and my sky changes color thirty miles off and my washing comes in gray. Same as him. He stamped a thing and I lived and this whole county died and he never said sorry, and neither will you. So I'm asking you the one thing I've got a right to ask. This transfer that's taking my rain — when it comes down—"

She stopped. Her voice did not break. It went perfectly level, the level of a woman who has already decided the answer and needs only to watch her sister confirm it.

"—is it going to be your hand on it."

And Della, who had come thirty miles to tell the truth, who had held the chip at three in the morning and drunk the water instead, who had spent a lifetime paying out the flat exact word like the only clean coin in the drawer — Della did not answer.

She could not say no, because it was not no. Every road on the map she'd stared at for a week ran through her sister's sky and out the other side with Della's name somewhere in the ledger — applicant or broker or diverter, she did not yet know which, only that it would be one of them, only that the honest answer to *is it going to be your hand* was not no. And she could not say yes, because she did not have the yes yet, because the shape was still turning in cold water and had not finished. So she stood with both true words dead in her mouth and gave Cass the only thing she had left, which was the flat exact face and the silence under it — the broker's silence, the not-yet the machine had taught her to mistake for a no.

She let the quiet run one beat too long. She heard herself do it. She could not stop.

Cass read the silence the way she read the wrong rain and the dropping tank and the stain in the west, from a long way off and then all at once. Something went out of her face that had not, in forty years of contempt, ever entirely gone — some last thin filament of *maybe* that had let her keep asking. It went out the way a needle comes off FAIR. What was left behind it was not rage. Rage would have been mercy. It was a terrible level certainty, a woman finally told a thing she has known her whole life and free now to stop pretending she doesn't.

"I see," Cass said.

She turned to the window, to the dull sheets and the stain coming up over the ridge, and she did not cross her arms now; her arms came down and hung, and that was the worst thing Della saw all summer — her sister's guard coming down not because it was safe but because there was nothing left it was protecting.

"You should go," Cass said, to the window. "I've got washing to bring in before it goes any grayer. No sense leaving it out under a sky like that." She did not turn around. "You'd know about a sky like that. You've stood under one your whole life. Turns out you carried it."

Della stood a moment in the humming kitchen with her sister's back to her and the truth she'd driven thirty miles to tell lying unsaid because the only true version of it was a silence, and understood — the way you understand a thing your body knew before your mind would take it — that she had come to warn Cass and had confessed instead, and that the wall between them, which she'd thought could not go higher, had a course of stone left in it, and she had just laid it, with her own hands.

She went out. Through the screen she heard the small ordinary sound of her sister bringing in the wash — the *thup* of a pin, and another, the sheets coming down heavy and dull under the first apologetic edge of a Ridley sky — and she got in the truck and drove west toward her own dead county, and crossed the Kettle where the water still ran on Cass's side, for now, and did not stop on the bridge.


---

# Chapter 20 — Form 22: Emergency Transfer (Delivery Scheduled) — THE TRANSFER THAT KILLS

> *The Authority is not liable for acts of God or of transfer, nor for the coincidence of the two.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §14

The barometer moved at 4:11 in the afternoon, and Della was thirty feet away with her hand on a truck door and did not need to look at it to know.

She knew it the way she knew a smell had changed in a room she'd been sitting in — the pressure going out of the day all at once, the flat high-summer stillness that had sat on Hale for a week suddenly draining out through some hole she couldn't see, so that her ears did the small dry click they did on the grade coming down out of the mountains. The dog that lived under Marvin's porch came out from under it and stood in the yard and looked at the west and did not bark. That was the thing she'd remember. The dog came out and looked west and its whole body went wrong, ears and tail and spine, a low animal listening, and it did not make a sound.

"You feel that," Marvin said. He'd come out onto the step with the coffee she hadn't wanted. He was in his shirtsleeves, the hat off, and he tipped his gray head at the sky the way he'd tipped it at the dead barometer in her office three years running, checking a thing for a pulse. "Pressure just fell out of it."

"Get in the house, Marvin."

He didn't. He was sixty-six and four generations on this ground and he had never once in his life run from weather, because weather here did not come to you, it went to somebody else while you watched, and a man learns the posture of watching and it becomes the only posture he has. He came down off the step instead, into the yard, into the failing light, and stood beside his dog and looked at the same west the dog was looking at, and Della, who had come out here to tell him a thing and had not been able to make her mouth do it, came around the front of the truck to stand beside him because there was nothing else her body knew to do.

She had come to warn him. That was the errand. She had told herself it was the errand the whole drive out, the chip going around in her right pocket, eleven years worn soft under her thumb — she was going to stand at Marvin Ott's counter, which was a kitchen table, and tell him plainly that a thing was moving in the basin that she had helped set moving, that she had gone down into the ditch with a man named Renn and told him to gum up Meridian's off-book routing, and that gumming up a machine that pulls storms is not the same as stopping it, it is the same as standing in front of it, and that she did not know anymore whose weather was going where or when. She'd meant to say it. She'd sat at his table and drunk the coffee she hadn't wanted and said instead: *You holding on out here.* And he'd said: *Ground's about done asking.* And she'd let the true sentence die in her throat the way she let the sister's name die, because there was a discipline to it, and the discipline had become the whole of her.

Now the pressure was gone out of the day and it was too late to say it, which was, she understood standing there, exactly the arrangement she had made with herself without admitting she was making it. You could hold the true thing in your hand and know precisely what it would do and simply not do it. She had built a life on that. She was very good at it.

To the west, over the dead reach of the Kettle, the sky was doing something the sky over Hale had not done in six years.

It was building. Not the flat apologetic blue that had worn on the county like a verdict — a real thunderhead, gray-bellied and rising, an honest-looking anvil of a cloud shouldering up over the mountains' foot, the green-black kind that meant water, that meant the smell of rain, that a person in a green county would call weather and a person in Hale would call somebody else's mail. Except it wasn't drifting. It wasn't building the slow honest way, the way Della had watched clouds build over Ridley from this exact dust her whole childhood, wanting them. It was *arriving.* It had an edge to it, a hard clean forward edge, and the edge was coming on across the sky at a speed that had a schedule in it, and Della had witnessed enough deliveries by now to feel the punctuality in her fillings like a filling gone bad.

"That's rain," Marvin said. His voice had a thing in it she'd never heard in it, not in three years of the hat. Hope is an ugly sound in a proud man. "Della. That's rain coming."

"That's a delivery," she said. "That's not yours."

"Whose, then." He didn't take his eyes off it.

She didn't answer, because the honest answer was *I don't know,* and the answer under the honest answer was *I helped,* and there was no version of the second one she could say to the face of a man watching water come toward his dying alfalfa with hope making him ugly. She got her phone out. She called Renn. It rang and rang out in the dry channel where he lived and it did not pick up, and she stood in Marvin Ott's yard with a dead phone against her ear and watched a stolen storm come on across the county on a schedule nobody had shown her, and she understood, in the cold procedural part of her that never fully switched off, that a call ringing out was itself a piece of information. He wasn't answering because he was working. Somebody was pulling, and somebody was pulling against it, and the two pulls were fighting over a front the size of a county and neither one of them was steering.

The barometer in her chest fell off a cliff.

The wind came first — not the delivery, the wind that ran ahead of it, a hot dry front-runner that lifted the dust off the yard in a sheet and drove it flat sideways so that she tasted the county in her teeth, the same gray flour that got into the butter and the wash, and Marvin's dog broke and ran back under the porch, and Marvin put his arm up against the grit and did not go with it, and Della grabbed his sleeve.

"Inside," she said. "Now."

"My field's the other way." He pulled loose, not hard, an old man's absent strength, and turned toward the draw — toward the low ground east of the house where his last watered field sat in the elbow of the dry Kettle channel, the alfalfa he'd leased eight-hundredths of an inch to keep alive, the field that was the cows, that was the place, that was the generation that didn't lose it. He was already walking. "If it's coming I want to see it hit the ground that's been asking."

She should have gone with him. She would tell herself for the rest of her life that she should have gone with him and she would never once be able to say what she'd have done differently in the dark and the water except be a second body it took. She stood in the yard a half-second too long doing the arithmetic her father had trained into her hands, the arithmetic that ran even now, even here — *how much, how fast, where does it land* — and the arithmetic came back wrong, came back with a comma in the wrong place, because a delivery is bent in over hours, slow, a degree at a time, and this was not being bent, this was being *dropped,* a whole storm's volume pulled fast and let go, and let go here, over a channel that had held no water in the memory of anyone under thirty, a channel graded and hardened by forty years of dry into a chute.

"Marvin," she said, and then she was running.

The sky came down.

It came down the way transferred rain came down, in a hard clean wall, a front you could see the edge of walking across the field post by post, wetting one fence post dark before the next, punctual as a freight, except there was too much of it and it was coming too fast, no schedule she'd ever certified, the acre-inches arriving not over an afternoon but over minutes, and the ground that had been asking for water for six years could not take it, the sun-cured pan of the Hale dryland shedding it like a roof, and all of it, every drop that hit the high ground, running downhill to the one low place, the dead Kettle draw, the chute.

She heard it before she saw it. It was not a sound the county had made in her lifetime. It was a low freight-train building of water coming down the dry channel from the high ground west, brown, roped, carrying the driftwood and the dust and the whole bleached inventory of everything the river had left behind when it went, and it came around the elbow of the draw where Marvin's field sat, and Marvin was in the field.

She was on the lip of the draw. She could see him. He'd waded out into his own alfalfa in the first of the rain with his gray head bare and his hands out, an old man standing in the middle of his last field getting wet, the first rain on his ground in fourteen months and it was somebody else's, routed through him by no form she had signed and every form she had failed to, and she saw him lift his face up into it, and she saw both of those things be true at once, the water on his face and the water in the channel, and she screamed his name into a wind that took it sideways and gave him nothing.

The flood took the field in the time it takes to say it took the field.

There was no wave, or there was, but not the kind a person pictures — the draw simply filled, the way a bathtub fills, faster than filling, the brown water spreading up out of the channel into the low field in a skin that was ankle then knee then not, that lifted the cut alfalfa off the ground in mats and turned the field into the river the river used to be, and Marvin, who had turned at last, who had heard it, who was moving now the way a stooped man moves in water that is pulling at him, was thirty feet from the high ground and then he was not moving toward it anymore. The water had decided. Conservation of storms: the rain that would have greened a valley, subtracted from where it was owed and added to where it wasn't, arriving all at once in the one place a body could drown, and a body did.

She went in to the knees before her own legs stopped her, some animal in them that wanted to live overruling the animal that wanted him, and the current took her feet and she went down and got a fence line and hauled herself back and lay in the mud on the lip of the draw with the rain coming down clean and punctual and endless on her back, and she watched the brown water where Marvin Ott had been and it gave her nothing back, no gray head, no hand, just the water going where it had been sent, and the rain kept coming down on schedule because a schedule does not stop for what it lands on.

Then, exactly, it stopped.

That was the worst of it, the part her body would not let go of after: the rain shut off. Not tapered. Shut off, the way a delivery shuts off when the window closes, a hard clean back edge to it walking east across the drowned field the same way the front edge had walked west, one fence post going from streaming to still, then the next, then the next, and the wind died, and the pressure came back up into the day, and her ears clicked, and over the mountains the last of the honest-looking thunderhead was already thinning out into the flat spent apologetic blue of a sky that had done a thing and could not take it back. Twelve minutes, maybe. She would never know the certified window because she would never let herself pull it. Twelve minutes of the most water Hale County had seen in six years, and it had killed the one man on the last watered ground, and drained down the channel toward the low country, and left behind it a smell — she registered it on her hands and knees in the mud, gulping air — the smell she had spent her whole career being unable to smell in Hale, the green wet smell of rain on ground, the smell of the thing everyone here wanted, rising off a field with a drowned man in it.

The dog came out from under the porch. It came down to the lip of the draw and stood beside her and looked at the water and made, at last, a sound, one long low note, and then was quiet.

Her phone was still in her hand. It had rung out. She looked at it and it said RENN and under that the call had ended and she did not know if she had ended it. She got up. Her legs did what legs do. She stood on the lip of the Marvin Ott place with the mud sucking her boots and the barometer in her chest that had fallen off a cliff not yet climbing back, and she made herself do the thing she knew how to do, the only thing, the thing her hands did when the rest of her could not — she started to file it. Time. Volume, estimated, high. Basin of origin, unknown, west, upper Kettle, pulled. Casualty. She got that far, *casualty,* the broker's word, the flat certified word, and it went up her wrist and did not stop at the wrist, went higher, closer to the bone than the stamp had ever gone, because there was a body under this form and she had watched it go under, and the word for a man she had known thirty years, a man who had told her *you're a good broker, Della, Doss'd be proud,* the word the Act had for him was *basin event.*

She did not have the stamp with her. It was in the safe on Main, behind the tumblers, where a want couldn't reach it. She found she was glad of that, and then she was sick in the mud, on her knees, the green wet smell of the rain all around her, and when she was done she wiped her mouth with the back of a muddy hand and she did not reach for the chip in her right pocket, because reaching for it now would be a lie she'd have to live with, would be asking a dead instrument for a pulse when she already knew, and instead she knelt there in the smell of everybody's wanted rain and did the arithmetic one more time against her will, the arithmetic that would not switch off, and it came back the way she had known it would come back since the pressure went out of the day at 4:11.

Somebody pulled a storm too fast off a full basin. Somebody pulled back against it. Two pulls, near together, both just after four, fighting over one front and letting it fall where nobody was steering, and it fell here, on the one low place, and the man in the low place had four generations of never selling his rain and it drowned him in somebody else's. And she had told a man to gum up a machine, down in the ditch — told herself the telling was information, the telling was guarding the Act, the telling was anything but a hand on a tiller — and the machine had not stopped when she gummed it. It had only run without a driver. Here was the receipt, face-up in the mud, in a hand she could not read as her own and could not read as anyone else's.

Sirens, eventually, from the Ainsley Hale side. Somebody had felt the pressure fall in town and called it in, or heard the water in the low country, or seen the one green field in a brown county from the road and known that green in Hale was a thing that told on itself. She stayed on her knees until the first county truck came bumping down the section road with its bar lights turning, and she raised her muddy face to it, and she was, she realized, already composing the sentences, already filing, because that was what she was, that was what she'd been trained to be at the counter that was her father's before it was hers — the one who stands still and gives the thing its accurate name while the water goes where it was sent.

The deputy came down the lip of the draw and slid the last of it and caught himself and looked at the water and looked at her.

"Della? God almighty. You hurt?" He got a hand under her arm. "Whose field is this?"

She looked at the brown water going flat and quiet now over the drowned alfalfa, taking on the flat apologetic color of the sky above it, the two of them matching, water and sky, spent.

"Marvin Ott's," she said, in the flat exact voice, the only mercy she had that didn't lie. "It's Marvin Ott's field. He was in it."


---

# Chapter 21 — Form 40: Casualty Report (Basin Event)

> *A casualty of transfer shall be recorded as a basin event; the record shall note volume, timestamp, and basin of origin, and shall not require the name of the deceased where the name does not bear on the balance.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §15(a)

The draw was still running when they brought Marvin up out of it, which was the wrongest thing Della had seen in a county full of them, because the Kettle draw had not run in the memory of anyone under thirty and here it was, a brown cord of water in a channel that had been dust the day before, going somewhere with the confidence of a river that had always been there. It smelled of mud, a smell Hale had lost. Under the mud, faintly, of ozone. They laid him on the bank above the waterline on a section of hog panel and someone put a tarp over him, and the tarp did not lie flat, and Della stood at the top of the cut with her hands in her coat pockets and did not go down, because there was nothing a broker did at the bottom of a draw. There was a thing a broker did at the top of it. She was already, without meaning to, timing the flow.

Point-nine of an inch, she thought, watching it. Maybe an inch. Dropped fast and all at once into a channel with no give in it, six years of sun-cure making the ground hard as a table so the water couldn't sink, could only run — down the old bed to the low draw where a man had been walking his fence in the cool of the evening to see if the wire had held, and it took him the way delivered water took everything: on a schedule, in a hard clean front, arriving where it was sent.

She knew the volume before the Authority did. That was the part she would not be able to unknow.

Reyes reached the top of the cut a little after her, the hand-crank field barometer still on its strap across her chest, and stood beside Della and looked down at the tarp and said nothing for a while. The sun was going. The apologetic blue drained off toward the dark it drained into every night, never a sunset, and laid a flat sourceless light over the running draw and the tarp and the two men and the hog panel.

"When did it come in," Reyes said. Not gentle. Reyes was never gentle. But not the auditor's voice yet either. Something in between, the voice of a person deciding which voice to use.

"I don't know," Della said.

"You were counting the flow."

"Habit."

"So was I." Reyes cranked the barometer, once, a small grind that sounded obscene on the bank above a body. She read it. Her mouth did a thing. "It moved this afternoon. Twice. Two pulls, near together, both just after four. Mine felt them coming both times." In the failing light her face was tired past forensics, and she said the thing that was going to be the whole rest of it. "There's no filing for this, Della. No offset. I checked the feed on the way out. That water didn't come off anybody's allotment. It came out of nowhere with a timestamp and it landed on Marvin Ott."

Nowhere with a timestamp. That was theft. Reyes did not say the word and Della did not say it and the word stood between them on the bank like a third person.

"Somebody pulled it," Reyes said. "Somebody pulled a lot of it, fast, and dropped it wrong, and it came down here." She was quiet. Down the cut the water went on running, indifferent, delivered. "I have to find out who."

---

She did not sleep and did not drink, which she counted separately now, two acts of not-doing done in the same hot dark on Depot Street with the dead lilac black against the window and the heat on her chest, a weight the body could not file its way out of. She took the chip out of her right coat pocket and held it in her palm and did not turn it. She had turned it ten thousand nights, the muscle of *no* worn smooth into the bronze. Tonight she held it still and pressed the flat of it hard enough to leave the ghost of the number, and she thought about the fact that she had set Toby Renn in motion — gone down into the dry channel and run the runner, told him which pulls to gum and which to let go, called it *information,* called it *guarding the Act,* called it anything but a hand on the tiller of a truck that dropped storms where the arithmetic told it to. She had moved him the way Voss moved her. And a storm had come down on the man who had begged her to make the Meridian deal — *let it be him, make it somebody else's turn* — killed by the alternative she'd chosen so it would not have to be her hand.

Near morning she understood she was going to have to go to the office, and there was going to be a form for this.

There was a form for everything. That was the county's one reliable mercy and its cruelest.

---

Junie had the office open when Della got there, which she should not have, because it was Sunday, and she was at the ledger with the good pen and her face was wrong. She had been crying and had stopped and the stopping showed worse than the crying would have. On the counter, squared to the edge the way Junie squared everything, was a single sheet with a heavy black rule across the top, and Della did not have to read the header to know it, but she read it anyway because you read everything, you were a person who read everything.

**FORM 40 — CASUALTY REPORT (BASIN EVENT).**

"Prewitt sent it over from the district before six," Junie said. Her voice was steady and that was worse too. "Said the Authority requires it filed by the broker of record within twenty-four hours of a basin event. Said—" She stopped. She looked at the form. "It's got boxes."

"I know it's got boxes."

"A box for volume. A box for timestamp. A box for basin of origin." Junie's careful round hand had filled the top line, the county and the date, before she'd hit the boxes and stopped. "There isn't a box for his name, Della. I looked. There's a line at the bottom for *decedent, if bearing on the balance.* If bearing on the balance." She said it the second time like she was hoping she'd misread it. "Marvin doesn't bear on the balance. There's no allotment involved. The form doesn't have anywhere to put him."

Della came around the counter and looked at the form, at the black rule and the boxes and the parenthetical cruelty at the bottom, *if bearing on the balance,* written by people who golfed, who had sat in a room somewhere in the seventies and decided which facts a casualty needed to carry and left the man himself off it as surplus, as a thing that did not affect conservation. Volume. Timestamp. Basin of origin. Three facts, and a man was none of them.

"Give me the pen," Della said.

"I can do it. It's the broker's—" Junie's hand tightened on the pen. "It's the broker of record's filing. I'm not the broker of record. But you don't have to be the one who—"

"Give me the pen, Junie."

The girl gave her the pen. Della sat down on the stool that had been her father's stool, pulled the form square, clicked the pen, and did not, for a moment, write anything. Outside the window the elevator stood against the apologetic blue, the same blue, exactly the same — a man drowned in the dry county and the sky had not so much as flinched. The machine's face did not change when it killed you.

She filled the boxes.

*Volume:* she wrote *approx. 0.9 acre-inches,* and then, because she was a person who could not make herself write a lie even now, even here, she scratched *approx.* and wrote *est. 0.9–1.0,* the true uncertainty, the honest range, and hated the steadiness of her own hand doing it.

*Timestamp:* blank. She knew it to the hour — two pulls just after four, Reyes's field needle falling twice inside the minute — knew it because she had set the man in motion who ran the truck. She wrote *pending determination* across it, told herself it was procedure, and knew it was the first fact she had ever refused to file because filing it accurately would file her.

*Basin of origin:* blank. *Pending determination.* The same lie in the same procedural coat. She could guess it close, and the guess had a farm on it, and a woman on the farm, and a line strung between two posts, and she would not write it — not here, not on this, not yet.

And at the bottom, on the line that read *decedent, if bearing on the balance* — Della looked a long time. Junie had left it empty, out of a decency the form did not deserve. Della put the pen to it and wrote **MARVIN OTT,** in full, the way you write it on a stone and not on a receipt, and then, in the small space the box allowed, *four generations, Hale ground, allotment never severed* — which bore on nothing, which the form had not asked, which balanced against no number in any column, and which she put down anyway, because a man who had kept his family's rain for four generations against every reason to sell it had drowned in rain that was stolen, and the record was going to say so.

She looked at what she had written. The clean boxes and the two she'd left as wounds. The name where the form wanted a balance.

"That's not how you fill it," Junie said quietly. "The *decedent* line's supposed to be blank unless he held the allotment the water came off. That's what *bearing on the balance* means. Prewitt'll kick it back."

"Let him kick it back."

"He'll say you editorialized a casualty report."

"I filed an accurate one." Della set the pen down. The small *thunk* of it went up her wrist anyway, the old wrongness living higher than it used to, up in the forearm now, closer to the bone. She had not touched the stamp. A casualty report was recorded, not sealed — a fact logged, not a transfer authorized — and she was sickeningly grateful that this one horror had not required her to bring the brass down on it. She did not examine the gratitude. It was its own kind of touching.

---

Reyes came in at ten, and Della knew from the way she came in — squared her shoulders in the doorway, did not sit, kept the field barometer on its strap — that the in-between voice from the bank was gone and the auditor had chosen.

"I need to ask you some questions," Reyes said. "As the Authority. I want you to know that's what this is before I ask them, because you and I have been — because I'd rather you knew."

"I know what it is," Della said. "You're an auditor. This is what an auditor is."

Something crossed Reyes's face. Della had said back to her the thing she'd said to Junie in the first week of all this — *the auditor's always coming, that's what an auditor is* — and neither of them said so. Reyes took out a notebook, the paper kind, and clicked a pen, and the click was the same small sound the stamp made and Della's wrist answered it before her ear did.

"Two pulls yesterday," Reyes said. "Both just after four, near together. Both off the upper Kettle sub-basin as near as I can triangulate. Small runner's signature — one truck, one hand, retail. Same signature as the point-three in the spring and the point-six in the north townships. You reported both of those. You're good at reporting imbalances, Della. You've filed every one in this county for twenty-two years." Reyes looked at her. "You didn't report the pull yesterday. Either one."

"I found out when you found out. On the bank."

"Maybe." Reyes wrote something. "Here's what I have to reconcile. You reported the first two thefts inside the seventy-two hours, by the book, to the minute. Then the runner's still running — I can see the truck's work in the field readings — and the reports stop. A broker who reports two thefts and then stops reporting a thief who's still working has either lost her instrument or started managing him. And you don't lose your instrument. I've watched you. You're the most careful hand I've ever audited."

Della said nothing. There was nothing to say that was both true and survivable, and she had spent fifty-four years choosing true, and she could feel the choice bending under her the way the whole county's ground bent under water that couldn't sink.

"I liked you," Reyes said, flat as a finding. "One other person in two counties who understood the machine and hated it and enforced it anyway. I thought we were the same. Broker and auditor, telling ourselves the letter of the thing exempts us." She closed the notebook. "I still think we're the same. That's the problem. The letter says a broker who stopped reporting a live diversion, in a basin where that diversion just killed a man, is someone I have to consider a party to it. Not because I want to. Because the letter doesn't care what I want, and neither do you. That's the whole reason I trusted you."

She put the notebook in her coat and looked, for a moment, at the Form 40 squared on the counter with its blank timestamp and its filled name — the two *pending determination* boxes and the *four generations, Hale ground, allotment never severed* crammed onto the balance line — and her forensic face did a small human thing and put itself back.

"You left the timestamp blank," Reyes said.

"Pending determination."

"You knew the volume on the bank before I did. A broker who can eyeball point-nine of an inch running in a draw knows to the hour when it came in." Her voice did not rise; it never rose. "You left it blank because filling it in accurately is a thing you can't afford to have in your own hand. I've never once seen you leave a box blank you could fill, Della. Twenty-two years. That blank box is the most honest thing on this form and it's going to hang you."

She went to the door and stopped with her hand on it, the way Marvin used to stop with his hand on the brim of his hat, and did not turn around.

"I'm going to find the runner," Reyes said. "When I do, he's going to tell me who was routing him — the buyers, the off-book side, all of it — because a runner facing a manslaughter finding tells everything to keep it off his own neck. And Della." Now she turned. "Some of what he knows is going to be you. I can see the shape of it already and I haven't found him yet. So I'll give you the one thing the letter lets me — a day's warning I probably shouldn't. When the diverter's statement gets taken, it's going to have your name in it. Decide, before then, what you want your own to say."

The bell rang her out into the blue.

---

Junie stood at the ledger a long time after the door settled. She had not written anything since Della came in. Her good pen was capped in her hand. Della waited for the question — the four flat words, *is it you,* the ones Cass had laid down in Ridley two days ago and the ones the girl had a right to ask now, the ones Della had trained her to say without looking up. She braced for them the way you brace for a stamp.

They did not come. Junie looked at Reyes's chair, and at the Form 40 with its two white boxes, and at Della, and she uncapped the pen and turned to the ledger and did not ask. That was the worse thing. A month ago she'd have asked. She had learned, this summer, at this counter, from this woman, that some questions you do not put to a person you still have to work beside — that you leave them off the record, ruled and blank, *pending,* and you keep filing. Della watched the girl choose silence over the question and knew exactly where the girl had learned it, and that it was the last lesson she'd meant to teach her and the one that took.

"Log the casualty report," Della said, because it was the only thing left she could hand the girl. "Two boxes pending. Mark the time it came from Prewitt and the time we filed it back. Keep it accurate to the minute, Junie. Whatever else is true, the record stays true."

Junie nodded, and wrote, and did not ask, and the not-asking sat in the office louder than any answer.

She took the chip out of her right coat pocket and set it on the counter, next to the Form 40, next to the black rule and the boxes and the name written where the form wanted a balance. Eleven years, bronze, worn soft on both faces from a lifetime of *no.* She had built a life on being the woman who could hold a thing in her hand and know exactly what it would do to her and not do it. The muscle of one refusal, lying next to the wreckage of all the ones she hadn't made. She did not pick it up, and she did not do the other thing, and she stood there not-doing all of it at once while a man's name dried in her own hand on a form that had no box for him.

Then she opened the ledger to the day's page and picked up the pen to log the filing, because that was the job, because there was always the next line, and she saw that Junie, who kept the record now, who kept it careful and round and true, had already entered it above where Della's hand would go — one line, dated, exact: *Form 40 recvd. Basin event, Kettle draw. Casualty: M. Ott.* — and under it, left blank, ruled off and waiting, the next line, and the next, in a hand that was still too careful, that still believed the record mattered, that would keep believing it long after Della was gone from this stool and this was the girl's stool and the girl's ledger and the county's next clerk was already trained.

Della put the pen to the blank line under Junie's and it hung there, not writing, while the barometer on the wall held at FAIR over a county where water had run for the first time in six years and killed a man. The record would stay true. It always did. It would stay true on every line but one — the box on the Form 40 where the time went, the time she knew to the minute and could not afford to know in her own hand, the one blank in twenty-two years she had left open on purpose. She logged the filing time in the ledger, careful, exact, to the minute. Then she looked at the other timestamp, the one still empty on the form beside her, *pending determination,* and understood that the truest thing she had ever filed was the box she'd left white.


---

# Chapter 22 — Form 3-A: Allotment of Record (The Funeral, Certified)

> *No seal may be affixed under duress; and duress shall be presumed absent.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §2(g)

They buried Marvin Ott on a Thursday, which was a market day, so the transfers came in on the feed all morning while the county went to the ground to put him in it, and the office ran itself with nobody there but the numbers, offsetting and balancing and holding to the third decimal in an empty room the way the county filed itself down while you slept.

Della stood at the back of the Lutheran churchyard in the flat white glare, in the one dark coat she owned, and did not go up to the grave. There were maybe forty of them, which was a good turnout for a man who had held on by his fingernails, and they stood in the apologetic blue with their hats in their hands and their backs to the elevator that held no grain, and the pastor read the part about the valley of the shadow, which was the only valley the AAA had never found a form for, and the dust came up off the fresh dirt and did not settle because there was no rain in the air to bring it down. It hung. It got into the flowers somebody had driven ninety miles to buy. It grayed the white lilies at the edges before the box was even lowered, and Della watched it do that, because she had learned a long time ago that if you looked at the sky at a funeral you would say something you couldn't take back, and if you looked at the dirt you would only think it.

The casket went down. It was closed. There had been reasons for it to be closed and nobody said them.

She had signed the release that let them bring him up out of the draw. Not the death — there was no form for the death, the death had gone into the ledger as a *basin event* under §15(a), a casualty of transfer, no defendant, a line Junie had written in her careful round hand three days ago and cried while she wrote and did not stop writing, because Della had taught her that you did not stop writing. But the release, the little procedural mercy that let the county have the body — that had needed a bonded seal, and Reyes had stood in the office doorway with her hand-crank barometer under her arm and her coat on in the heat and watched Della bring the stamp down on it, and had said nothing, and the nothing had been the loudest thing anybody had said to Della all week.

Because Reyes was reading the trail now. Reyes knew the pull that filled the draw had a shape, and the shape had a route, and the route had, somewhere back up its length, a broker who had gone down into the ditch and set a runner in motion and told herself it was information.

Marvin had begged her to make the deal. She had said no, and been so proud of the no.

Iris Prine-Halloran was there. Della hadn't expected that and didn't understand it until she made herself understand it: the girl had come thirty miles from the green county to stand at the grave of a Hale farmer she'd never met, because Iris had read the register and knew whose water had drowned him and knew it hadn't been an act of God, and someone from the family that kept the ledgers ought to stand where the body went into the ground and know it too. She stood apart, near Della but not with her, her phone for once in her pocket. She did not look at Della. She looked at the dirt. She had learned that already, at twenty-six, the looking at the dirt. It was the fastest anybody had ever learned it in front of Della, and it broke something in her to watch, because she recognized the posture. It was her own.

Cass had not come. Of course Cass had not come. Cass did not cross the line for the living; she had crossed it once, for the dead, eleven years ago, and Della had been at the other end of that crossing, and she stood now in the churchyard dust with the pastor's voice going on and felt the two funerals slide together the way the two skies slid together at the county-line bridge, the same channel, one of them dry.

*

Eleven years ago they had buried their father in the same churchyard, in a wetter year, though *wet* in Hale had already stopped meaning what it meant anywhere else. There had been more people. Doss Prine had been the county broker for forty years and the county broker is the man who tells you the true price when you have come to sell the last thing, and a lot of them had come to say a hard thanks for that, and a few had come to make sure he was in the box.

Della had not been sober. She wanted that on the record, in her own ledger, where she kept the things there was no form for: that she stood at her father's grave with a pint of the county's cheapest in the coat pocket where the chip would later live, and that she had touched it through the fabric the way she would later touch the chip, checking it, and that the difference between the two objects was the whole distance she had traveled since — traveled starting the next morning, because of what got said at the graveside, because of Cass.

Cass had driven the thirty miles from the district seat past the county line, past the Kettle already going to gravel on the Hale side, and she had stood across the open grave from her sister and her face had been the Prine face, their mother's bones under their father's flint, aged in a different weather. Browner. A cough she kept swallowing. She had the strong sun on her and Della had the dust, and they had not spoken in longer than a decade even at that graveside, and the whole county watched them not speak over the hole where the man who split them was going down.

The pastor said the same thing about the valley. There was more green on the flowers that year.

Afterward, when the county had shuffled off to the church basement for the ham and the weak coffee, the two of them had been left at the grave the way you are left, the family, to stand a minute while the men with shovels waited at a polite distance smoking. And Cass had looked down into the grave, not at Della, and said the first thing she had said to her sister in eleven years.

"He should have let me die."

Della had the pint in her pocket and the dust in her teeth and forty years of the durable child in her spine, and she had not said *don't,* and she had not said *Cass,* and she had not reached across the hole.

"He should have let me die," Cass said again, evenly, as though correcting a figure. "Then there'd be one honest thing in this family."

And here was the thing Della had never told anyone, the thing the whole eleven years of silence had been built to bury, the reason she got sober the next day, because you cannot say a thing like this drunk and go on drinking, you have to either take it back or become a person who never says such things again, and she couldn't take it back, so she became the other person, the flat exact one, the one who did not pick things up.

She looked at her sister across the grave. She thought about the valley fever and the lamp she'd held as a girl of fourteen while Cass drowned on dry land in the next room, and about the block sale her father had built at the counter that was now Della's, section by section, neighbor by neighbor, selling the sky off Hale to buy one green life, and about being the one told *you've got the strong lungs,* which meant *you can stay,* which meant *you are the part of this that can be left in the dry place and go on working.* She thought about eleven hundred forms she had not yet stamped and the twenty-two years she had already stamped them. And she said, in the voice that would be her voice for the rest of her life, the true and unforgivable thing.

"I've filed a thousand of these," she said. "Transfers. Rain off one place onto another. I've done it my whole life." She was not crying. That was the part that could not be forgiven, that she was not crying. "You're the only one of his I'd take back. If I could un-sign one transfer he ever made, Cass, it wouldn't be Hale. It'd be you."

The men with the shovels shifted at their polite distance.

"I wish he'd let you die," Della said. "Not because I hate you. Because it cost me everything and it didn't even buy one honest thing. You're right. There isn't one. You'd have been it, and he spent you like he spent the rest."

Cass did not flinch. That was the horror of them, that neither of them flinched; they were their father's daughters and they took a figure in the face and squared it. She looked at Della a long moment across the grave, and something moved behind her eyes that was not hurt, because they were past hurt, it was *recognition,* the recognition of a person hearing a true thing said, the worst thing, the thing she had thought in the dark for forty years and never once believed anyone else had the nerve to think back at her.

"Well," Cass said. Their father's word. "At least one of us can say it out loud."

And she turned and walked to the rented car, coughing the cough that was the receipt for the rain that bought her, and drove the thirty miles back east to the green county and the white washing and the sky that still meant something, and she had not spoken to Della since, and Della had understood, driving home that night past the BOAT LAUNCH sign to a house where she poured the pint down the sink and never bought another one, that they had not been estranged by a misunderstanding.

They had understood each other perfectly. That was the estrangement. Two women had told each other the truth over their father's grave, and both truths were correct, and Cass had gotten in the rented car and driven back to the one green county her breathing had cost a hundred families their rain.

*

The lilies had gone all the way gray at the edges by the time the pastor finished.

Della came back to herself in the Hale churchyard eleven years on and colder, and Marvin's grave was mostly filled, the men working easy in the dust, and the forty had thinned to a dozen, and Iris was still there at her little distance looking at the dirt. The apologetic blue held over them, the six-year color, the finding the sky could not stop finding against the county, and Della thought — the way she had learned not to think at funerals and thought anyway — that the blue had begun, this month, to reach.

She had seen it in the interim-custody deliveries. Ridley's sky spent three-tenths of an inch at a time, going the color of a verdict a demonstration at a time, the apologetic blue starting its thirty-mile crawl east onto the county where a woman hung washing white on a line and had not yet learned, or had learned and could not stop, trusting a thing that was for sale. The color of Hale was coming for Cass. Della had signed the receipts for it because the receipt was the broker's plain duty, and had told herself, again, that the duty was not the doing.

Iris came over when the dozen had thinned to six. She stood beside Della and looked at the grave, not at her, and Della understood that the girl had learned that from her too, or from the same weather.

"They filed him as a basin event," Iris said. It was not a question.

"Section fifteen-a. A casualty of transfer, recorded." Della's voice did the flat thing. "There's no defendant on a basin event. That's the point of the section. The rain came, a man was under it, the Act isn't liable for acts of transfer." She heard herself. "There's no box on the form for what he was."

"My mother wouldn't come."

"No."

"She said—" Iris stopped, and started again, careful, the way you handle a thing you've decided to carry. "She said she wasn't going to stand in Hale dust to mourn a man who died of Hale's rain being somewhere it shouldn't. She said that's what Hale does. Kills people with water that's in the wrong place." Iris looked at her aunt for the first time all day. "She meant you. She thinks you signed it."

"Did I," Della said. Not quite a question either.

Iris didn't answer that, which was its own answer, the same non-answer Della had given her sister across a grave eleven years ago, the family's oldest form, and the two of them stood in it together, aunt and niece, three generations of Prine hands that knew what a non-answer certified.

"I read the funeral," Iris said finally, quiet. "Not this one. Granddad's. There's a record — there's a record of everything, that's the horror of your whole family — the church logs who's in attendance. You and my mother were the last two to leave the grave. Twenty minutes after everyone else. The sexton noted it because they had to wait to fill it." She wasn't accusing. She was reading, the way she read everything, laying the fact on the counter to see what it weighed. "What did you two say to each other for twenty minutes over your father's grave, that neither of you has spoken since?"

The dust hung. The men with shovels waited at their polite distance, the same distance, the same men or their sons.

Della looked at the girl who had come thirty miles to stand where a body went into the ground, who had learned in a season the thing that had taken Della forty years, the looking at the dirt, the non-answer, the reading of the record — the girl who would carry the sight now, the clear terrible sight, in the green county, over the water that was starting to go — and she thought about telling her. The whole family had one honest thing in it and it was a grave that never got dug, and here was the one Prine who might have the nerve to hear that said out loud.

"We agreed on something," Della said.

Iris waited.

"That's the worst thing two people can do to each other, at a funeral. It's not fighting. It's agreeing." She looked at Marvin's filling grave and the graying lilies and the blue that was crawling east toward her sister's line. "Don't ever agree with somebody over a grave, Iris. You can't take it back and you can't un-know that they meant it. There's no form to reverse it." Her wrist ached where the stamp had lived that morning, up past the wrist now, closer to the bone. "Ask your mother what we agreed on. She'll tell you. She's braver than I am — she'll say it out loud. I only ever say it to graves."

Iris studied her a long moment with the Prine eyes, and Della watched the girl decide not to press, watched her file it, watched a conscience learn to leave a line blank and mark it *pending* — and hated that she had taught another one that, the way her father had taught her at a counter with her hands too small for the brass.

The last of them drifted off to the cars. The pastor shook Della's hand and said the county was lucky to have a broker who came to the graves, and did not know what he was saying, and she let him not know it. Iris walked to her truck without another word and drove east toward the line, toward the wet county with the color coming for it, and Della stood alone at the back of the churchyard in the apologetic blue with a dead man in the ground in front of her and a live sister thirty miles behind her and understood that she was going to lose them both, that she had already lost them both, that the ledger did not have un-signing in it and neither did she.

She reached into her right coat pocket and took out the chip and held it in her palm in the churchyard glare. Eleven years, worn soft on both faces. She had held it at her father's grave as a pint and at her mother's line as a clothespin and now here, over Marvin, as the small bronze weight of every *no* she had ever managed. She had built a whole life on the muscle of refusing. She had refused the drink for eleven years and refused the deal for eleven weeks and been so proud of both, and a man was in the ground because her refusing had a route out the back of it that she had walked down herself, into the ditch, in the dark, telling herself she was still the one who says no.

She turned the chip once, the way a tongue works a sore tooth, around and around.

Then she put it back in her pocket, and she did the thing she had been dreading all week, the thing she had come to the funeral partly to make herself do, the thing that was not the funeral. She took out her phone and she found the number she had never once dialed in eleven years, the Ridley Fork number, the one she'd copied off Cass's emergency-contact card and never used, and she stood over Marvin's grave with the dust in her teeth and she pressed it, because Ridley's sky was going blue and her sister was under it hanging washing white and the only person left in two counties who might believe Della when she said *they are coming for your rain and it may be me who brings it* was the one who had already told her, over a grave, that she should have been allowed to die.

It rang once. It rang twice.

Cass picked up, and did not say hello, and there was a silence on the line the exact shape of a sky going spent, and then her sister's voice, flat and exact and unforgiving as their father's, said the thing that turned the whole eleven years like a stamp coming down:

"I know it's you. I can hear the dry."


---

# Chapter 23 — Form 9-C: Interbasin Reassignment (Reinstated, Compelled)

> *A broker may be compelled to serve where no other is bonded; and service so compelled shall carry the full effect of service freely given, the record making no distinction between the two.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §2(k)

The letter came by courier this time, not by feed, because the Authority had learned that a thing which arrives on a screen can be argued with and a thing which arrives in a man's hand cannot. He was young, sunburned across the nose, in a district-office polo that had never seen a field, and he stood in the door of the office Della was no longer licensed to sit in and asked her to sign for the envelope, which was its own small joke, and she signed with the pen she was still allowed to hold and not the seal she wasn't.

The office had a smell it hadn't had three weeks ago. Under the paper and the machine-oil there was river now, the wet-mud reek of the Kettle come back into a channel it had been out of for longer than Junie had been alive — the flood had gone down but the mud stayed, and the mud smelled, and the smell got into the office off their boots and would not file itself down. It was the smell of Marvin's draw. It was the smell of eight days ago.

She opened the envelope standing at the counter, the counter that was Junie's now on paper, and read it once, and set it down, and read it again.

Junie was watching from the ledger. She had been watching everything for eight days, the way a person watches the road after a wreck, unable to stop, learning nothing.

"What is it," Junie said.

"Reinstatement." Della heard her own voice go flat and exact, the armor closing, and let it. "Under §2(k). A broker may be compelled to serve where no other is bonded." She turned the page. "The Authority finds that Hale County requires a bonded seal to execute the corrective transfers arising from the — " she read the phrase off the page rather than say it in her own mouth — "the basin event of the twenty-second. So they're compelling me back into the chair to clean it up."

"They suspended you Friday week ago."

"They un-suspended me this morning. Same signature, near enough." She set the letter flat and squared it with two fingers, the old reflex, paper into order because nothing else would go into order. "I refused the seal on the Meridian nine-C and they took the license for it. Now there's a mess only a licensed seal can sort, and I'm the only license, so they gave it back. Not because they forgave the refusal. Because they need the arm." She looked at the young man still standing in the door, waiting for something. "You can go. I'm not going to argue with the paper. Nobody argues with the paper."

He went. The bell rang him out into the blue, the same spent apologetic blue it had worn for six years, a sky that had watched a man drown in a draw eight days ago and not so much as darkened to note it.

Della stood with the letter and did the thing it did to her, which was close a loop she had spent her whole life pretending was a line.

She had refused. She had stood at this counter with Reyes and said *write it up, suspend me,* proud of the refusal in the cold way she was allowed to be proud of nothing else — proud that the seal her father pressed into her hand would not come down on Cass's sky. And the refusal had held for exactly as long as the machine had no use for her. The day it needed her arm it took the arm back, un-suspended, compelled, and now she would stamp the forms that swept up the flood — the remediations, the casualty offsets, the paper that turned eight days ago into a filed and balanced event — and every stamp would be legal, and every stamp would be hers. The Act had a form for grief and a form for a drowned man and now a form for a broker who tried to keep her hands clean: it waited until it needed her, and then it made *clean* a thing it could revoke at will.

"They're making you file the cleanup," Junie said. She had worked it out. She worked things out fast now, which was the wound. "The flood. Marvin. You have to sign the paper that —"

"Puts it in the book. Yes." Della went to the terminal and woke it and the corrective queue was already there, already loaded, the Authority not wasting a morning: eleven items, remediation transfers and offset corrections and one line at the bottom that read, in the flat grammar of the machine, *CASUALTY OFFSET — BASIN EVENT — OTT, M. — pending broker seal.* She looked at it a long moment. A man's whole four generations on Hale ground, reduced to a line that needed a stamp so the basin's arithmetic would balance around the hole where he'd been. "The county doesn't stop for the fact that its clerk has a conscience, Junie. I taught you that. I taught you a lot of things I'd take back."

Junie didn't answer. The pen was in her hand and she wasn't writing with it. Eight days ago she had held the same pen and entered Marvin's death as a Form 40, in her round careful hand gone crooked, because Della had stood behind her and could not make her own hand do it — one more thing she'd take back and couldn't, because the record didn't revert any more than the rain did.

The bell went.

Marisol Reyes came in the way she came, cold in the heat, the field barometer in its scuffed case across her body, and she stopped just inside the door and read the room the way Junie was learning to read it and Reyes had always known how. She saw the letter squared on the counter. She saw the corrective queue glowing on the terminal. She had known it was coming; she had probably filed half of it.

"You got the reinstatement," Reyes said.

"An hour ago."

"They didn't waste the morning." It was the same thing she'd said the day of the ruling, and they both heard it, the machine's punctuality worn into the auditor's own mouth. She set the case down, careful. "Send the girl to lunch."

"She was at lunch for the ruling. She can be at lunch for this." Della didn't look up from the queue. "Junie. The Trestle. Take an hour."

Junie put the pen down in the crease of the ledger, exact, the way she'd been taught, and got her coat, and at the door she stopped and said, to Della's back, "You don't have to do the Ott one. I'm still bonded on paper till they file the reinstatement. I could —"

"No," Della said. "You don't put your hand on that one. Go."

The bell rang her gone. Then it was the two women and the mud-smell and the dead needle at FAIR and the queue between them like a body on a table, which, in the case of one line of it, it was.

"Off the record," Reyes said.

"You keep saying that like it means something."

"It means I'm about to tell you a thing I'd have to act on if you made me say it twice." Reyes uncrossed her arms. "The imbalance behind the flood. The pull that filled the draw. I traced it. You knew I would."

Della kept her hand flat on the counter beside the terminal and felt the old wrongness go up her wrist, the animal flinch that eleven hundred stamps had never worn out, and gave the counter the flat exact face. "Go on."

"It was a runner's pull. Off-book, no filing, no offset — a hole, not a record. Somebody bent a volume off the upper Kettle and dropped it fast and it went where fast water goes, down the dead channel, into the draw." Reyes's voice didn't change; that was the horror of her, that she could lay a killing out in the cadence of an inventory. "Too much, too quick, wrong basin, wrong hour. Not a storm. A flood. And the truck that ran it lives out of the Kettle bed below Larkin, and the man who runs it is Tobias Renn, and I've had his signature on two prior diversions this summer." She let it sit. "I picked him up yesterday. He's in the district lockup. He's not going anywhere."

The room did not move. Somewhere under the office the mud smelled. Della had put Toby in motion — had gone down into the ditch in her good coat and *run* him, flipped him to Reyes under 31-B to gum up Meridian's logistics, told herself it was all still just information — and the pull that killed Marvin had come off the collision of everything she'd set moving, and now Toby was in a cell and Marvin was in the ground and both of those things were downstream of a woman who had believed to the last that filing was not doing.

"He tried to abort it," Della said. It came out before she chose it. She had put it together in the eight days, from the volume and the timing and the kind of man Toby was under the grin. "The volume was wrong and he saw it and he tried to pull off it and couldn't. You bend a front, you can't unbend it fast. He couldn't stop it."

Reyes looked at her for a moment that lasted. "You can't know that."

"I know the man. I know what the volume does. He tried."

"Then he can say so at his hearing." Reyes said it flat, and it was mercy and it wasn't, because they both knew what a diverter's statement did to a runner in a captured agency. "Here's why I sent the girl out, Della. Here's the off-record." She put one finger on the counter, the same gesture she'd used on the study five weeks ago, pointing at a number that meant a life. "To reinstate you clean enough that your seal is good in the window — good enough to survive challenge — the Authority needs the basin event closed. Cause of the imbalance, on the record, sworn. A diverter named. And the only party who can name the diverter and make it stick is the broker who reported the diversions in the first place." She took the finger back. "You. Your 31-B filings. Your statement puts Renn in the frame for the flood, whole. It makes him the man who killed Marvin Ott. And it makes your seal valid, and your license clean, and your reinstatement bulletproof, because the broker who caught the runner can't be the runner."

Della understood it the way she understood everything in this office, all at once and too slow both. "You're telling me I can save myself by handing you Toby."

"I'm telling you what the letter of it requires. Cooperate — give the compelled statement, name Renn, close the event — and you're reinstated clean, your seal survives, you keep the chair long enough to do whatever it is you're going to try to do about Ridley." Reyes's face did the thing it did, the almost-mercy that never became mercy. "Or protect him. Sit on what you know, refuse the statement, and the reinstatement's dirty — a broker under a cloud, a seal that won't survive a challenge, and you go down with him, because your 31-B filings are already in the record and a broker who ran a runner and then shielded him from a killing looks, to a hearing, exactly like a broker who was in on it."

"I *was* in on it," Della said.

"Don't say that to me twice." Reyes said it fast, hard, the first crack in the forensic she'd shown in three weeks. "I have to act on the second time. Say it once and I can call it a woman's grief. Say it twice and it's a statement." She breathed. "You ran him to gum up Meridian. That's grey. It's not the flood. The flood was a pull gone wrong, and if Renn ran the pull, Renn's the frame, and the Act doesn't have a box for the woman who set him near it. There's no line on the form for you. There's only the line for him."

And there it was, laid on the counter in the cold inventory voice: the machine offering Della, one more time, the thing it had offered her all her life. Clean hands. There was no box for her. There was only the box for Toby — a man who had, she was near certain, tried to stop the very water that killed Marvin, and couldn't, and would now be the man who killed Marvin because the form needed a name and his was the name the form could hold.

The stamp was in the safe six feet behind her. She had not touched it in eight days. She could feel where it was the way you feel a tooth. To take up the chair — to do the one thing left that might freeze Cass's basin out of Meridian's reach before the window closed — she needed the seal good. To make it good she needed the reinstatement clean. To make it clean she needed to swear that Tobias Renn killed Marvin Ott. Every door opened onto the next door and the last door had a man behind it.

"He said a thing to me," Della said. "In the ditch. He said, *you ran me the way they run me, only difference is you can sleep.*" She looked at the corrective queue, at the line that read *OTT, M.* "He was wrong about the sleeping. But he had the shape of it. He does illegal what I do legal, and now the Act's going to make him the murderer and me the broker who caught him, and the only true difference between us is going to be that I get to keep the stamp."

"That's not a legal position," Reyes said. "That's a feeling. Feelings don't close basin events."

"No." Della squared the letter again, though it was already square. "They don't."

Reyes waited. When Della didn't go on she picked up the case and slung it across her body, careful, and looked once at the wall barometer with its dead needle at FAIR, and did not say the thing she always said about it, which was somehow worse.

"I need the statement by Thursday," Reyes said, at the door, "or the reinstatement lapses and the window closes and whatever you meant to do about Ridley dies with it. Meridian's got their contingent order lodged and dated. When Ridley hits critical, they buy, and there's no seal in the county valid enough to stop them, because you'll have thrown yours away protecting a runner who's going down regardless." She put her hand on the door. "You can't save him, Della. I want you to hear that plain. He's caught. The statement doesn't decide whether he falls. It decides whether you fall with him for nothing, or stand up and use the chair for the one thing you might actually change." She opened the door and the dust and the flat blue came in around her. "I'd take the chair. I'd hate myself and I'd take the chair. That's the whole of what I've got for you."

"You'd take the chair," Della said, "because you're the same animal I am. You chose the letter over the person a long time ago and you keep choosing it and you call it enforcing. Two women who picked the rulebook over the woman standing there. In different chairs."

Reyes stood half in the door with the blue behind her. "Yes," she said, and there was nothing in her voice at all, which was her way of bleeding. "And the book refuses to say which of us is worse. I've read it front to back looking. There's no such finding." She went out. The bell rang her gone.

Della stood alone with the letter and the queue and the mud-smell and the dead barometer and the safe with her father's brass in it, forty years and her own twenty-two worn shallow at the rim. She went to the terminal and looked at the eleven lines and put her hand on the mouse and did not open the Ott casualty offset, because opening it meant taking up the chair, and the chair meant the statement, and the statement meant Toby. She found, for once, that the arithmetic did not come up clean. It did not balance to the third decimal. There was a man in a cell who had tried to stop the water, and a man in the ground the water had taken, and a seal in a safe that could save her sister's basin only if she was willing to put the first man permanently under the second — and there was no column in the ledger for the distance between those two, no place to enter it, no fee, no timestamp, nothing.

The bell went. Junie came back from the Trestle with a wax-paper packet she wasn't going to eat and stopped just inside the door with the mud on her boots and read the room.

"You have to give them Toby," Junie said. Not a question. She had worked it out. She worked everything out now. "To get the chair back. To do whatever you're going to do about Ridley."

Della looked at the girl a long moment — the fourth pair of ledger-hands, the round careful column she'd taught to slur when belief went out of it, the one person left in the county she might still spare something. "Log the reinstatement," Della said. "Timestamp it. And Junie — the Ott offset stays in the queue. Don't touch it. That one's mine when it's anybody's."

Junie went to the ledger and picked up the pen, and then she stopped, and did the thing that told Della the girl had gone past her, had seen the whole shape and gone one step past where Della could protect her from it.

"When you give the statement," Junie said, not looking up, her pen over the open book, "will it be true?"

Della didn't answer.

Junie nodded slowly, the way Marvin used to nod, the way the people here nodded at weather they couldn't argue with, and wrote the reinstatement into the column in a hand that had stopped being careful and started being exact, which was a colder thing, and Della watched the girl she was training to be the next clerk of the apocalypse enter the fact of it into the record — *broker reinstated, compelled, §2(k)* — and understood that the lesson she was teaching, right now, standing here, was how a person signs the thing that damns somebody and calls it the only chair left to stand up from.


---

# Chapter 24 — Form 3-A: Allotment of Record (Iris Prine-Halloran)

> *The next generation inherits the register as it stands, and shall take it subject to all prior entries, whether or not the taker had notice of them.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, Reg. 3-A.15

The first ledger in the county's run was the oldest thing in the office, older than the safe, older than the barometer, and Della had not opened it in eleven years. It lived on the bottom shelf where the damp couldn't reach it and the light couldn't fade it, a wide book bound in gray board gone the color of the county, and she took it down that morning because the compelled reinstatement required her to certify the whole chain of the Kettle sub-basin's transfers back to origin, and origin, in Hale County, was her father's first page.

She set it on the counter. The spine cracked the way old spines crack, a sound like a small dry thing giving up, and the book fell open near the front to a page she had not chosen, because a book that has been opened to one place ten thousand times will keep opening there, and this book, in her father's forty years, had opened most to the middle, to the block sale, to the fee ruled off neat in the margin. But she thumbed backward past all of that, past the seventies, past the drought that started it, back to the first entries, to a young man's hand.

She had forgotten that his hand had ever been young.

*Transfer of record,* it said, in an ink gone brown, *0.15 acre-inches, Section 9 to Section 9, Ott to Ott, offset intra-parcel, this the fourth day.* No year that meant anything now; a decade the world hadn't kept. And under it, in a hand that had not yet learned to be cold, that still rounded its letters the way Junie rounded hers, the way a person writes when they believe the record matters: *Verified deed. Verified allotment carries. Balanced. Filed.* And then, because he was twenty-five and proud and had done a true thing well on a good day, he had drawn, in the margin, a small mark that was not required by any regulation — a short straight line under the word *Balanced,* a young man underlining his own competence, the way you'd underline a grade.

It was the same transfer. It was an Ott moving a fraction of his own rain off his own fallow ground onto his own crop, the same shape as the Hollis transfer she had stamped in the spring on the morning Voss's card came under the door, the same clean closed arithmetic that touched no one but the man who signed both sides of himself. The machine had started here, in a good clerk's good morning, in a boy underlining the word *balanced* because he had verified the deed and confirmed the allotment carried and made the plus meet the minus at the third decimal, and had felt the *thunk* go up his wrist, and had thought it was the sound of something being kept safe.

She looked at the little underline for a while.

Then she turned the pages forward through his life — through the years getting drier, the entries getting more, the rounded letters going square, the margin marks going away — until she came to the fee ruled off on his own family's rain, and she did not stop there, because she had stopped there before and it had not helped, and she closed the book on all forty years of it and it made the softer, worse sound, the sound she had learned to hate more than the stamp, and put it back on the bottom shelf where the light couldn't reach.

"Certified to origin," she told Junie. "Log it and mark the chain complete."

Junie logged it. Junie had been logging everything since the reinstatement, quietly, in her round believing hand, because the machine did not stop for one clerk's conscience and Della's conscience was the only thing that had stopped; the office ran on Junie now, and Della sat in the chair the Authority had compelled her back into and put her seal to the cleanup of a flood that had killed a man she'd set in motion, and the girl watched her do it and wrote it all down, four generations of ledger-hands narrowing to this one straight column.

The bell over the door went at eleven.

Della knew before she looked. She had seen the little car pull up outside, a city car, low and gray with mud on it from thirty miles of the county road, and she had thought, with the part of her that thought in forms, *Ridley plates,* and then she had made herself stop thinking it. Iris came in the way she did everything, fast and certain and already talking, except she was not talking; she stood inside the door with her phone in her hand and did not raise it, and that was how Della knew this was not the hearing, not the porch, not the prosecutor.

"Junie," Della said. "Go pull the Coyle bond confirmation. It's in the district office. Take the truck."

Junie looked between them, twenty-two and not stupid, and understood she was being sent away from something, and got her jacket and went, and the bell rang her gone into the blue.

Iris waited until the truck turned off Main. Then she came to the counter and set her phone down face-up on the wood, a screen full of small type, columns and dates, the way you'd set down a warrant.

"I've read it all," Iris said. "I want you to know that before I say anything else. I've read the whole thing. The block sale in the seventies. Kettle Basin Land and Rain. The four name changes down to Meridian. My grandfather's fee — he took it on his own allotment too, did you know that, he charged the family commission on the family's own rain, it's right there, ruled off in the margin like a grade. I found the doctor's affidavit that said my mother had to be somewhere green. I found the deed to our farm. It was bought with the money from Hale's sky." She said it flat, no heat, the way Della said the price of July rain, and Della understood, standing there, that this was the thing she and this girl had in common and nobody else in either county did: they could both hold the whole shape of it in their hands and not drop it. "I know what he did. I know it saved my mother. I know it's why I'm alive." A small pause, the only place her voice moved. "And I know you're the one who inherited the office that does it."

"I know all that too," Della said.

"I'm not here for that." Iris turned the phone a quarter turn, toward Della, an offering. "I'm here because you're the only other person who understands the machine, and I've found the one thing in it that isn't already lost, and I can't work it alone. I don't have a seal. You do."

"I'm suspended."

"You're compelled," Iris said. "It's not the same. They put you back in the chair because there's no other bonded broker and the law needs a seal. That means you can still sign. You just can't refuse." Her eyes went hard and clear and very young. "That's what I need. Somebody who can't refuse."

Della looked at the screen. Then she reached under the counter, out of a habit older than hope, and her hand found the barometer's wall casing instead of the terminal, and she took it back and left it flat on the wood. On the terminal the basin feed ran the way it always ran, the county's live volumes ticking in their columns, and her eye went to Ridley Fork out of the same habit and found the acre-inch figure and watched it, in the time it took to breathe, drop a hundredth.
