Not a delivery. A bleed. Somewhere east a truck she couldn't see was working a front, and the number under RIDLEY FK stepped down again while she watched, patient, unhurried, a tap left open.

"Say it plain," she said. "You didn't drive thirty miles to tell me what your grandfather did. You know what he did. So do I. What did you come to say."

"Meridian's going to run my mother's basin to critical." Iris said the word *critical* the way you'd name a diagnosis someone else was refusing to hear. "You know that. Reyes told you off the record, the buyout — they don't want Hale watered, the deluge was the receipt, they want Ridley dry so they can buy the collapsed allotments for nothing when the town evacuates. And the flood — Marvin — that didn't stop them. That *slowed* them, because it burned their runner and it put the auditor on the whole sub-basin, but the contingent buyout's still lodged with the district, it's still dated, it's still sitting there waiting for the trigger. I pulled it. It's public. Any person may pull it." She almost smiled, and there was nothing in it. "Reg. 3-A. I've gotten very good at Reg. 3-A."

"I know the buyout's still live," Della said.

"Then you know it triggers when Ridley Fork drops below floor." Iris did not look at the terminal; she did not have to; the number was on Della's screen and on Iris's phone and going down on both. "That's the whole play. Pull the basin under the floor, the town leaves, the allotments sell for nothing. And it's dropping right now — you're watching it drop, I can see you watching it." She put one finger on her own screen and the columns scrolled and stopped. "So I went looking for a way to hold it above the floor where Meridian can't reach it. There's one. There's exactly one."

Della waited. She had learned, in twenty-two years at this counter, that the terrible things came to you plainest if you did not reach for them, if you let a person set them down.

"A defensive bond," Iris said. "You bond the allotment to a filed transfer and it locks. Encumbered rain can't be futured, can't be lifted off-book without leaving a record, can't be bought at a critical-sale. Meridian can't touch it. Nobody can." On Della's screen the number under RIDLEY FK stepped down another hundredth, small, exact, while the girl talked. "I ran the math four times. The basin holds. The town stays. Meridian moves on to the next county, because there's nothing left here to buy."

"That's a good move," Della said, and meant it, and felt the whole cold weight of what was coming down the sentence toward her before it arrived, the way you feel the pressure drop before you understand it's a delivery. "There's a cost in it. You wouldn't have driven here if there weren't. What's the cost."

Iris did not look away, which Della respected, and which was the worst thing she could have done.

"To freeze the rest," Iris said, "you have to move some of it. That's the reg — there's no locking rain in place; you lock it by sending a piece away and bonding the deed against the sending. So to save my mother's basin" — she said it evenly, a girl reading a price true, the way her aunt read prices true — "somebody files a nine-C that pulls a controlled volume off Ridley Fork. Off my mother's ground. Enough to bond the rest. Not enough to sink it. A knife's-edge number, and it moves every minute her basin drops, because the floor's coming up to meet it. I've got the number for right now. It won't be the number tomorrow." She touched the screen once more, where the figure had stepped down again. "I need a seal on the form. Today, if you can. This week, or there's nothing left above the floor to bond."

The office was very quiet. Somewhere out on Main a truck went by, not Junie's, and the sound came in through the frosted glass and went out again, and the dust settled on the sill the way it did while you slept and while you didn't. Della stood with her hand flat on the barometer casing and felt her wrist, the old wrongness, the ache that had lately started living higher than the wrist, up in the forearm, closer to the bone — felt it now without a stamp in her hand, felt it at the idea of one.

"You're asking me," she said slowly, "to broker a transfer that takes rain off your mother's farm."

"I'm asking you to save it by taking a piece of it." Iris's voice did not shake and that was the thing that undid Della, that the girl had already done the crossing Della had spent the whole season refusing, had already understood that clarity was not clean hands, had already looked at the only move on the board and seen that the only way to keep her mother's sky was to be the one who reached up and took part of it down. "There's no version where nobody loses the rain. You taught me that. Not you — the register taught me that, but it's your register. Conservation of storms. Somebody thirsts. I did the math and the somebody is my mother, a little, now, on purpose, by us — or my mother, all of it, later, on the courthouse steps, by them. Those are the two. There isn't a third." She straightened. "I'd rather it be a little, now, by somebody who has to look at her."

"By me."

"By you," Iris said. "You're the only one who can. I'm the holder-of-record's daughter, I can't broker my own family's basin, and even if I could I don't have a seal and I never will now, because I've spent a year learning exactly what a seal does and I would rather cut off my hand. My mother can't do it. Web can't do it. Junie" — she said the girl's name the way you name a thing you've considered and set aside — "Junie's got a seal coming, she's bonded next spring, but she's twenty-two and it's not her sin and you'll never let it be, I could see that the second you sent her out the door. So it's you. It has to be you. The broker who's compelled and can't refuse, whose father did it first, whose sister's under it. The register couldn't have built a cleaner form for you if it tried." She picked the phone back up, and the offering became a fact laid on a counter, unretractable. "The window's short. Meridian's pull is going to start bleeding Ridley inside the month, and the bond has to be filed and sealed *before* the number goes under, or there's nothing left to lock. You have days. Maybe two weeks."

Della looked at the screen the girl was holding out, at the knife's-edge number, at the columns that meant her sister's rain, some of it, taken by her sister's sister, on purpose, to save the rest — the same shape, the exact same shape, rain pulled off a Prine to save a Prine, entered without comment, one generation down. She did not reach for the phone. She thought of the young hand in the gray book on the bottom shelf, the boy underlining *balanced,* not knowing yet that the word would come to mean this, that a good clerk's good morning would come, forty years on, to mean his own daughter reaching up into his other daughter's sky and taking a piece of it down with the stamp he'd pressed into her hand while he was dying and glad.

"There's a rule," Della said finally, and her voice was very steady, and under the counter her right thumb had found the chip in her coat pocket where it hung on the hook, and was working it around and around, eleven years of *no* worn soft, the muscle of refusal she had built a whole life on, testing it now against a form she could not refuse. "Two-i. The broker may not be a party to her own transfer. Kinship's a party. Fee's a party. I bond your mother's basin, I'm both — I'm the sister and I take the broker's fee, there's no way to file it without a fee, the bond requires a compensated transfer. I can't be the one who signs it. It's the one thing in this whole machine I'm forbidden to do."

"I know," Iris said. "I read that too."

She set the phone down flat on the counter, face-up, the number glowing, and pushed it an inch across the wood toward Della's hand, the way you'd slide a form across for a signature, the way Voss had slid the study, the way Dana Webber had set down the deed like it weighed something.

"So either you find a way to sign your own family's transfer and break the one rule you've kept clean your whole life," Iris said, "or you hand the seal to that twenty-two-year-old and let *her* sign it, and it's her name in the ledger instead of yours, and her hands, and her sin, for the rest of her life." She looked at her aunt across the counter, across the dust, across forty years and thirty miles compressed into three feet of gray wood. "That's the form, Della. Pick the hand."


---

# Chapter 25 — Form 9-C: Interbasin Reassignment (Della, Applicant)

> *The broker may not be a party to her own transfer; kinship to a holder of record, or entitlement to a fee arising therefrom, shall each constitute an interest, and no seal affixed by an interested broker shall be valid.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §2(i)

She read the section three times before she let herself understand it, because understanding it closed the last door in the house.

It was six in the morning and the office was cold the way it was only cold at six, before the day came up over the elevator and turned the cold to heat with no hour of comfort between. Della had the statute open under the lamp, her father's annotated one, his pencil in the margins in a hand that got smaller the older he got, and Iris's bond math beside it in her fast slanting figures, and between them the whole thing lay out plain as a deed. To lock Cass's allotment in a bond deep enough that Meridian could not buy it out from under her when the basin went critical, somebody had to file a defensive 9-C. And a 9-C had to be sealed. And the seal had to be a broker's. And the broker could not be a party.

She was a party twice over. She was the holder's sister. And a bonded transfer carried a broker's fee she could not decline any more than she could decline the offset — the Act built the fee into the filing the way it built the flinch into her wrist, and a fee off her own sister's rain made her, in the flat language of §2(i), *entitled to a fee arising therefrom.* Interested. The word sat there in her father's book, underlined once, lightly, decades ago, by a man who had known exactly which sections would matter.

There was one seal in the county that was not hers.

She looked up. Junie was not in yet — it was six — but the ledger was on the shelf where Junie left it, squared to the edge, and the good pen lay across it, and Della looked at the pen for a while in the lamplight the way you look at a thing you are about to use to hurt someone.

Junie was bonded. Junie had taken the provisional seal in the spring, had stamped Hollis's inside-parcel transfer with it and glowed for an hour after. Junie was not a party to anything. Junie could bring her own clean provisional seal down on the 9-C that saved Cass, and Della's hands would stay out of it, technically, forever, the way they had stayed out of everything for fifty-four years — and the price would be a twenty-two-year-old's name in the ledger next to a transfer that took rain off a family to save it, entered without comment, the first line of a life spent learning that legal and clean were different words.

The last clean-hands option the county had left her. Soil the kid instead of yourself.

She closed the statute. The wall barometer read FAIR, as it had for eleven weeks and then some, the needle stuck fast on the good word over a county dying under it. She tapped the glass out of habit. Nothing moved. She hadn't expected it to. She checked dead instruments the way she checked the chip, to be sure the deadness was still there.

She would have to tell Cass. There was no version where the bond went in without the holder's consent, and no version where she asked for it by mail. She had left one notice on a porch rail eleven weeks ago and spent every week since watching what her silence cost. She would drive out and say the whole thing to her sister's face, entire, and let Cass decide whether to be robbed a little by the person who had spent forty years being the reason she'd been sent away.

She took the stamp out of the safe and held it and did not use it and put it back, which was its own kind of using, and turned the dial and left before Junie came in, so she would not have to look at the pen again.

---

She crossed the Kettle at the county line at nine.

The dry side was the dry side. The BOAT LAUNCH sign had lost another degree of lean since spring; the gray pan below it had cracked into bigger plates as the summer cured it, and a wind off the pivots pushed dust across the road in low fast rivers, the only thing in Hale that still moved like water. Then the bridge, and the seam under it she had learned to hate — the channel changing color mid-span, dust going to damp going to a real brown current on the Ridley side, the same river, one half robbed forty years before she'd had a vote and the other half running fat and ignorant toward her sister's ground.

Except.

She slowed the truck on the far side and looked east, over the Ridley bottoms, the way you look up when something in the light is wrong before you know what.

The sky over Ridley was the wrong color.

Not spent — not yet, not the flat apologetic blue Hale had worn six years, the blue of a thing that has done what it did and stopped pretending. But going. Along its western edge, over the county line, over the fork, the honest wet blue of Ridley had begun to thin, to pale, to take on at its rim the first chalk of a sky being drawn down a degree at a time toward somewhere else. She had watched three-tenths of an inch come off this basin in July and told herself three-tenths was nothing, a demonstration, a letter addressed to her. It had not been nothing. It had been the first of a subtraction, and here was the second thing subtractions did: they showed. The apology was crossing the line. She was watching it happen to her sister's sky the way she had watched it happen to her own before she was old enough to name it, and there was no form to blame, and no one to serve notice on, and nothing to do but drive under it.

Cass's washing was on the line.

It hung between the two posts by the porch where it always hung, where Della had first seen it eleven weeks ago and understood it as a fact from the past — white, stiff, clean, a woman trusting a sky she had not yet learned was for sale. It was not white now. It was drying wrong. The dust that never reached Ridley had begun to reach Ridley; the wind that carried Hale's pan east had found the Ridley bottoms at last, and the sheets on Cass's line had gone the faint gray of wash that dries slower than it should under a sky that has stopped keeping its side of things. Cass was taking them down. She stood at the line with a basket at her hip and a clothespin in her teeth and she was bringing the wash in early, before it had finished, because it was not going to finish, and she did it with the flat competent motions of a woman who had already understood the thing and was not going to say it out loud.

She had the Prine hands. Even at forty feet, taking down a gray sheet, she had their mother's hands and Della's and Iris's, three generations of the same pair folding the same failing cloth, and Della sat in the truck a moment before she made herself get out, because the resemblance was a knife that cut whoever held it.

Cass did not stop folding when the truck door shut.

"You'll want the rail," Cass said. "That's where you leave things."

"I didn't bring a form."

"No." Cass pulled a pillowcase off the line, snapped it once — the dust came out of it in a small gray breath — and folded it against her chest. "You brought your face this time. That's new." She fit the fold square and laid it in the basket. "Web's at the co-op. Iris is in the office with the books. If you came for one of them you can wait in the truck; it's cooler than the porch."

"I came for you."

Cass reached up for the next thing on the line and her hand stopped, just for a second, at the top of the reach, and then went on and pulled the sheet down. Della saw the cough come up in her and get swallowed — the deep one, the old receipt, the lungs that had cost a county its rain to save and had never once let Cass forget the price of her own breath. Cass folded the gray sheet.

"Say it, then," Cass said. "You've been driving forty years to say something to me. You're fifty-four. Say it while I've still got a line to hang out."

So Della said it. She said it standing in the yard in the wrong light with her sister's ruined wash coming down between them, and she said it the way she said the hard things at the counter, flat and exact, because a soft word would have been a second theft on top of the first.

She told her about Meridian. About the deluge that had never been a deluge — the rain-for-Hale that was only the honest-looking front on a plan to pull Ridley Fork to within an inch of its floor, run it critical, and buy the collapsed allotments cheap at the trigger, the way the same company under an older name had bought Hale cheap in the seventies. She told her the buyout order was already lodged with the district, dated, waiting. She told her the gray in the cloth on the line was the front edge of it. She told her about the flood — she did not spare herself the flood, the stolen water dropped fast down a dead draw, Marvin in it — and she told her, because Cass would find it in the record if Della didn't say it first, that Della's own hand had set the runner in motion whose pull had helped fill that draw. She told her Marvin was dead of it. She watched her sister's face take that in and give nothing back, which was worse than anything it could have given.

And then she told her the last part, which was the reason she'd come.

"There's a way to freeze your allotment," Della said. "A defensive bond. You lock the rain to the deed so deep the Act won't let Meridian touch it when the basin goes under. Iris found it. It works." She made herself hold Cass's eyes for it. "But to lock the rest of it, the bond has to move some of it. A 9-C. It pulls a piece of your sky off Ridley — controlled, survivable, enough to knock the basin below Meridian's optics but not below the floor — and bonds the remainder past their reach. You keep your basin. You keep the farm alive. But you lose the piece. Now. To the transfer." She said the rest of it in the same voice, because there was no other voice for it. "And the transfer needs a broker's seal. And I'm the broker. And the Act says I can't be the one who signs it, because you're my sister and there's a fee in it I can't refuse. So either the transfer doesn't happen and Meridian takes all of it a season from now, or somebody's hand comes down on a form that robs you to save you." She stopped. "That's the whole thing. That's what I drove out here to say."

The wind moved a gray sheet on the line. Somewhere behind the house a milk pump started up and ran and stopped.

Cass was quiet a long time. She finished folding the sheet in her hands and laid it in the basket with the others. Then she looked at Della, and the thing in her face was not softness and it was not fury; it was the plain terrible arithmetic of a woman handed a bill and reading it to the end.

"You want my name on it," Cass said. "You want me to sign so you can take my rain."

"I want your consent to the bond. The holder has to consent. I won't do it behind you. I did that once already, on that rail, and I've watched what it costs, and I'm not doing it to you again."

"You're doing it to me right now." Cass said it without heat, the way she said everything true. "You just want me to hold the pen while you do it. So it's mine too. So when it's done there's two names on the thing and I can't say it was only you." She set the basket down on the porch step. "That's what consent is, out here. You know that better than I do. It's the county making sure the person it robs signed for it. My whole life somebody's been getting a Prine to sign the thing that ruins them and calling it consent."

"Yes," Della said. There was nothing else to say to it. It was true.

Cass looked at the sky then, the pale rim of it over her own bottoms, and Della watched her sister look at it the way Marvin had looked at the dead barometer — checking a thing for a pulse out of a habit older than hope, a Prine reading a sky she'd already read the verdict off of. When Cass spoke again her voice had gone lower, the cough in it, held down.

"He sold a county to keep me breathing," Cass said. "And they sent me here so I could breathe, and I've breathed thirty miles from the dust for forty years and hated every clean lungful of it, because I knew what it cost and who paid it. You paid it. The whole time, standing at his counter learning to do the thing that dried out the place I couldn't live in." She looked back at Della. "And now the same company's come to finish it, and the only one who can stop them is you, and the only way you can is to do to me exactly what he did to Hale. Take the rain off a Prine to save a Prine. Take a fee. Enter it without comment." Her mouth moved, not a smile. "You didn't have to become him, Della. You could've just let it happen. You've been letting things happen your whole life. Why not this one."

"Because I can see your face," Della said.

It was the thing she had almost said to Marvin at the counter in the heat, and had swallowed, because he had asked her to make the victim someone he couldn't see and she had not been able to tell him the victim had a name. She said it now to the name.

Cass took it without moving. The wind came across the yard and lifted the corner of a gray sheet in the basket and let it down. For a moment neither of them said anything, and the thing that hung in the air between them was not forgiveness, and it was not the past finally spoken, and it was not two women understanding each other for the first time — they had understood each other perfectly for forty years, that was the whole wound, they had understood each other and been right and there had never been a version where nobody lost the sky. The most that was in the air was that Cass had heard it and had not spat.

"Iris says the number works," Cass said at last. Not softer. Just done considering. "You'd take the small piece. Not the floor."

"Not the floor. She's run it four ways. It survives."

"And if she's wrong. If your county's math is wrong the way it's always wrong, and the pull's too deep, and my basin goes under anyway." Cass picked the basket back up, settled it on her hip, the gray wash mounded in it. "Then you'll have robbed me for nothing, and killed my farm with your own stamp instead of theirs, and I'll have signed for it."

"Then you'll have signed for it," Della said. "I won't lie about the risk. I told Marvin the price true and it didn't save him. But I won't tell you a soft number to get your name. If it goes wrong it goes wrong with both our hands on it, and you'll be right that I did it, and there won't be anything either of us can un-sign."

Cass stood with the basket on her hip and the ruined wash against her and looked at her sister across the yard for a long moment, and behind her the line hung empty now, the two bare posts and the slack rope, and the sky over the posts going the color Della had driven under her whole life, arriving here at last, on schedule, punctual as an eviction, belonging now to someone Della could see.

"Bring the form," Cass said. "I'll sign for the piece. Not because I forgive you. Because I'd rather be robbed by the one who'll tell me the true price than by the one who golfs." She turned toward the porch, and then stopped, and did not turn back, and said it to the door. "And I want it in the record that I knew. Whatever you enter it as. I don't want it going in your county's book *without comment.* Put a comment. Put that the holder knew what her sister was and let her do it anyway. That's the one honest line I get out of this family. See that it's there."

She went inside. The screen door shut behind her, not slammed, just shut, the way a thing shuts that means to stay shut.

Della stood in the yard a while under the walking blue, next to the empty line, with the number in her head that survived four ways and might not survive the fifth, and the pen on the ledger squared to the edge where a twenty-two-year-old had left it clean. She did not have to soil the kid. She could keep her own hands out of it one more time, the last time, the way she'd kept them out of everything, and hand Junie the pen and be, forever, technically, the broker who didn't sign.

She got in the truck. She sat with her hands on the wheel and did not start it and turned the two hands over the way she turned the chip, around and around: her own brass, forbidden by the one rule she'd kept clean her whole life, the seal that would be void the instant an interested broker brought it down — or the girl's clean provisional pen, valid, unencumbered, and twenty-two. Cass wanted a comment in the book. Cass wanted it in the record that she'd known what her sister was and let her do it anyway. But Cass had not said whose hand. Nobody had said whose hand. The form did not care whose hand, only that a valid seal came down before the floor came up, and there were exactly two seals in the county that could do it, and one of them damned a child and the other one was against the law.

She drove back toward the county line and the dry side, under two skies going the same color now, and did not know yet which hand it would be. She only knew the window was closing, and that somewhere ahead of her the office was open and the barometer was stuck on FAIR and Junie's good pen lay across the ledger squared to the edge, clean, waiting, next to a safe with her father's brass in it, and that she was going to have to choose between them before Ridley dropped, and that she had not chosen, and that the not-choosing was itself a hand on the clock. She crossed the bridge over the seam in the river with the question still open in her mouth like a coin she had not yet decided which way to spend.


---

# Chapter 26 — Form 9-C: Interbasin Reassignment (Transfer Window Opens)

> *A transfer window, once opened, may not be extended; the broker is charged with knowledge of its duration, and the running of its time shall not abate for the readiness of any party.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, Reg. 9-C.20

The notice came in on the courtesy feed at 6:52 in the morning, before Junie was in, before the day had come up over the elevator, and Della read it three times the way she had read §2(i) three times — not because it was hard, but because reading it again was a way of not yet standing up.

**NOTICE OF DELIVERY WINDOW**, the feed said, in the flat capitals the machine used for the things that could not be argued with. Application 9-C-041. Origin basin: Ridley Fork. Destination: upper Kettle. And then the two lines that turned the whole book's slow arithmetic into a clock: *Window opens 0700 this date. Window closes 0700 the fourth day following. Deliveries scheduled within said window may not be extended nor recalled.*

Ninety-six hours. She looked at the wall clock, which read 6:53, and did the subtraction that any broker did in her sleep. Ninety-five hours and seven minutes, and running.

Under the notice, appended, a second document she had been waiting eleven weeks to see land and had hoped, in the animal part of her, never would. **CONTINGENT ORDER, LODGED — MERIDIAN SKY HOLDINGS.** A standing purchase order for the Ridley Fork allotments, dated three weeks back, priced at the critical-sale floor, *to execute automatically upon the declaration of Ridley Fork as a critical basin.* It sat there in the feed like a trap with the jaws already open and the trip-wire strung. All it wanted was for the basin to go under. And the delivery window that had just opened was the thing that would take it under.

She understood the shape of it entire, then, standing at the cold counter in the six-o'clock dark. Meridian would pull Ridley hard across ninety-six hours — legally, on the ruled 9-C, the offset she had been unable to stop — and pull it *past the trigger,* to critical, and the moment the basin crossed the line the order would fire and the collapsed allotments would sell themselves to the outfit that had collapsed them, cheap, without a person in the room. The whole machine had been built so that no one would have to be in the room.

Her counter had to happen inside the same ninety-six hours. Iris's defensive bond — the 9-C that pulled a controlled piece off Ridley and locked the rest past reach — had to be filed and sealed *before* Meridian's pull carried the basin under, or there would be no basin left to bond, only a critical declaration and an executed order and a green county gone the color of hers. She had to rob her sister before the market could, in order to keep the market from robbing her sister at all. There was a word for a thing like that and it was not *rescue,* and she did not reach for it.

She reached for the phone instead and called Iris, who answered on the first ring like a person who had also not slept.

"It opened," Iris said. Not a question. "I've got the feed too. I put an alert on the application weeks ago."

"Ninety-six hours."

"Ninety-five and change." A pause, the sound of paper. "I've been running it all night. The number holds, Aunt Della. If we file the bond and pull the small piece — I've got it at four-hundredths of an inch off the whole hundred-and-one, that's the piece — it drops Ridley below Meridian's optics, below the point where they'd bother contesting it, and locks the rest above the floor where their order can't reach. Four-hundredths. It survives. I've run it every way there is to run it."

"And if Meridian's pull is already moving while we file."

The pause was longer this time, and Della heard in it the one thing about Iris that made her the true inheritor and not just the clever one: the girl did not soften a number.

"Then it's a race in the same basin," Iris said. "Two pulls off one sky. If ours lands and bonds before their volume takes it critical, we win and Ridley keeps its farm and loses four-hundredths. If theirs takes it under first, our bond has nothing left to attach to and we've robbed Mom for nothing. It comes down to which transfer registers first. To the timestamp." She said it the way you'd read a thing off a gauge. "It comes down to a stamp coming down before a stamp coming down. Same as it always does out here."

*The timestamp.* Everything in the county came down to that in the end — a hole with no record, a delivery with one, a death filed as a basin event with the time left blank because filling it in filed her too. The whole apparatus was a machine for deciding whose form was stamped first, and the machine did not care that this time the two forms were both Prine.

"Cass consented," Della said. "To the piece. You know the terms she set."

"She told me." Something moved in Iris's voice that was not warmth — nobody in this family had warmth to spare — but was close to the country of it. "She said you have to put a comment in your county's book. That the holder knew what her sister was and let her do it anyway." A breath. "She made me swear I'd check it was there. She doesn't trust your Act to record the one true thing."

"She's right not to," Della said. "The Act's got no box for it." And then, because it had to be said aloud to the one person who could carry it if Della couldn't: "I need a valid seal by the window's close, Iris. That's the piece you can't compute. There's no bond without a seal, and there's no seal I'm allowed to bring down, because I'm the holder's sister and there's a fee in it I can't refuse. The Act won't let me be the broker of my own family's transfer. You know that."

"I know §2(i)," Iris said, flat. "I found it before you did. So it's Junie, then. Hand the kid the pen."

"Maybe."

"Maybe." Iris let the word sit, and Della heard the twenty-six-year-old decide not to fight her aunt about it over a phone at seven in the morning. "Get me the answer today. If it's Junie, she has to be under a supervising broker's direction to seal it, and you're suspended, and — the Authority has to reinstate you to *direct* her even if she signs, or reinstate you to sign it yourself. Either road runs through Reyes. There's no version of this that doesn't run through Reyes." A pause. "You know what Reyes wants for it."

"I know what she wants."

"The statement." Iris said it the way she said all the true cruel things, without appetite. "Toby's diverter statement. You give it, they reinstate you, you get a valid seal for the window. You don't, and there's no bonded broker in Hale who isn't suspended, and Meridian pulls Mom's sky under with nobody able to stop it. That's the wall." The line was quiet. "I ran the water four ways and it survives. I can't run that one. That one's yours."

Della looked at the barometer on the wall, the old brass-cased unit with the needle dead on FAIR over a county that had not seen an honest sky in six years, and she thought about the field hand-crank Reyes carried and how it had *moved,* in July, fallen like a thing lowered on a rope, on a schedule, and how the movement had been the horror and not the relief. Somewhere out ahead of her, thirty miles east, a real needle was going to start falling across the next ninety-six hours, and the falling would mean rain arriving on the wrong basin on schedule, and she would either have gotten there first or she would not.

"I'll get you the answer today," she said, and hung up, and stood up, because there was nothing left to lean against.

---

Reyes was already parked outside the office when Della came up Main, the county car pulled in nose-out the way careful people parked, and she was sitting on the hood in the early cold with a paper cup of the Trestle's coffee held in both hands like a woman getting what heat she could out of it. She did not smile. She never smiled to be liked.

"You saw the window," Reyes said.

"I saw it."

"And the order under it." Reyes turned the cup. "Lodged three weeks ago. They didn't even wait for the ink on the ruling to dry. They knew the window would open the day the corrective queue cleared, and it cleared yesterday, so it opened this morning." She looked up Main toward the elevator and the apologetic sky behind it, and her forensic face did the thing it did when it was reading a gauge that told it something it hated. "They built the trap and then they built the thing that springs it and then they lodged the thing that profits from it, all in daylight, all filed, all lawful. There is nothing in here for me to prosecute. The whole crime is that it's legal." She said it without heat, the way Della said the price of July rain, one competent woman to another across a system that had captured them both. "You've got ninety-five hours to file a bond that stops it. I read Iris's math. It's good math."

"It's good math," Della agreed. "It needs a seal."

"It needs a seal." Reyes stood up off the hood. In the cold she looked smaller than her reputation, tired, homesick for a coast she'd priced herself out of, and she looked at Della with the plain forensic honesty that was the only incorruptible instrument left in a corrupt agency. "You're suspended. Dereliction, pending revocation. You can't affix a valid seal, and you can't direct the Alcott girl to affix hers, until you're reinstated. And I can't reinstate you into a chair clean enough to hold a challenged 9-C — and Meridian will challenge everything, Della, they'll challenge the color of the ink — until you've discharged the compelled duty that's still open against you. The diverter's statement. On the flood. On Renn." She let it lie in the cold air between them. "It names him whole. You know what else it names, if a good lawyer reads it slow."

"It names me," Della said. "The stalling. The deal I made with him. The runner I set in motion."

"It half-names you," Reyes said. "It's enough to end you and not, by itself, enough to convict you, and I won't pretend I know which side of that line you fall on when it's over. I'm not offering you a clean thing. I'm telling you the price of the seal you need." She drank the last of the coffee and crushed the cup in one hand, a small economical violence. "You give me the statement — the true one, the whole one, Renn's part and yours — and I reinstate you today, in time for the window. You don't, and I've got no lawful road to put a valid seal in your hand or the kid's, and your sister's basin goes under on schedule with both of you watching, and the order fires, and Meridian owns Ridley Fork by Sunday." She met Della's eyes. "I'm not the villain in this. I keep wanting you to see that. I'm just the one who has to read you the price."

"I know what you are," Della said. It was the thing she'd almost said the day they met, when they'd recognized each other as the two women in two counties who understood the machine, and it landed differently now, in the cold, with ninety-five hours running: *You're me, in the other chair, telling yourself the letter of the law exempts you.* Neither of them said the rest of it. There was no need. They had understood each other perfectly for weeks, and been right, and that was the whole of the thing.

"When," Reyes said.

"By end of day. I have to see Renn first." Della got her key out and did not put it in the door yet. "You'll let me see him."

"I'll let you see him." Reyes went around to the driver's door. "It won't help him and it won't help you and I'd rather you didn't, and I'll let you, because I owe you that much for the July delivery I couldn't stop being a letter addressed to you." She got in. Then, through the open window, in the forensic flat: "The window doesn't care that they're both Prine, Della. It doesn't care whose stamp comes down first or why. It only records which one did. Get there first, or don't. That's the whole mercy the Act has in it. It keeps the ledger and it doesn't read it." She pulled out, nose-first, careful, and left Della standing at the frosted door with the key in her hand and the county waking gray behind her.

---

She let herself in. The office was cold the way it was only cold at seven, and the ledger sat squared on the shelf where Junie left it, the good pen laid across the top, and Della looked at the pen and then made herself not look at it, and went instead to the safe and turned the dial and took out the stamp.

She had not held it in eight days, not since before the flood, and it had gone cold in the dark of the safe, cold as the counter, cold as Reyes's hood in the morning, the county seal cut into its brass face and worn shallow at the rim from her father's forty years and her own twenty-two. It was heavier than she remembered, the way a thing gets heavier when you have decided what you are going to do with it. She stood at the counter with the brass in her hand and did not ink it and did not set a form under it, which was its own kind of using, and she thought about the two roads Iris had laid out. Hand Junie the pen. Or sign it herself, break §2(i) knowing, keep the crime hers and not soil the kid, and go down as the one who signed.

She had spent fifty-four years being the one who didn't sign. The one who filed. The one whose hands stayed technically out of everything while the harm went by in good order under her seal. It was the whole architecture of her life, and it had a name she had never let reach the page even in her own head, and she was going to have to take it apart with her own hand inside ninety-five hours, and she found she was not afraid of that part. She was afraid of the part where she had to buy the seal from Reyes with the true price, which was Toby, who had tried to abort the killing pull and couldn't, and who was going to go down whole for a death that Della's stalling had helped arrange, so that Della could keep a chair long enough to save a sister who would never forgive her.

There was no clean basin. There was just the one you could see the faces in. Toby had told her that in the dry channel, and he had been right, and she was about to prove him right with his own life.

The bell over the door went.

Junie came in with her jacket zipped to the chin against the cold and her breath showing and the earnest ink-stained carefulness of a person who still believed the record mattered, and she stopped just inside the door because Della was standing at the counter holding the stamp over no form at all, and even a twenty-two-year-old could read that.

"The window opened," Junie said. It wasn't a question either. Everyone in the county with a feed knew by now. She came to the counter slowly, and she looked at the stamp in Della's hand, and then she looked at Della, and she asked the thing she had been asking in one form or another since the first week, the innocent question that indicted, the question Della had spent the whole book deciding how long to let her keep asking. "Are you going to make me sign it?"

Della set the stamp down on the counter between them, the brass ticking once against the wood, and she looked at the girl's careful hands and thought of four generations of the same competence — Doss's, hers, Junie's, Iris's thirty miles east — all of it pride in a clean column, all of it the machine training its next clerk in the shape of a good day's work.

"No," Della said.

And the word came out of her the way *no* had come out of her ten thousand nights over a chip in the dark, the muscle of a lifetime of refusing — except that this time the refusal was not of the transfer. This time it was of the last clean-hands road the county had left her. She had said *no* her whole life to keep her hands out. She was saying it now to put them in.

"No," she said again, quieter, and picked the stamp back up, because it was hers, because it had always been hers, because her father had pressed it into her hand at his deathbed and told her *you do not leave the seal where a want can reach it,* and she understood at last that the want he'd meant had been her own, all along, the want to stay clean. "I'm going to sign it myself, Junie. When the form's ready. My hand. My name in the book." She turned toward the phone to call Reyes and buy the chair with Toby's life, and stopped, and said the rest to the barometer instead of the girl, the way the men here said the hard things. "And you're going to keep the ledger. And you're going to write, in your own hand, exactly what I did. Every line of it. You don't leave a blank."

The needle on the wall held dead on FAIR, over a county drying under it, ninety-four hours and change running toward a sky thirty miles east that was already beginning, on schedule, to be spent — and Junie picked up the good pen, because that was her job, and opened the ledger to the first clean line, and waited for her broker to give her something to record.


---

# Chapter 27 — Form 31-C: Diverter's Statement (Compelled)

> *A diverter's statement, once given, is irrevocable, and shall be received as the truth of the affiant against all persons named therein, the affiant included; no subsequent recantation shall unmake the record.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, Reg. 31-C.4

The district lockup was in the basement of the Basin Authority satellite, down a stair that smelled of the river-mud that had come up on everything since the flood — the flood's own smell, following her indoors, into a building thirty feet above the draw that had drowned Marvin, as if the water had decided to keep the appointment even where it couldn't reach. Reyes went down first with the keys. Della went behind her with the form in a manila folder under her arm, the way she carried every form, flat and squared, and ninety-one hours ran on the clock she now kept in her chest instead of the wall.

**FORM 31-C — DIVERTER'S STATEMENT (COMPELLED).** She had drawn a hundred forms for other people and this was the first one in twenty-two years she was going to have to feed, herself, to a person she had put in the chair by coming down the stair. There was no box on it for that either. There was a box for the affiant's name and a box for the diverter's and a long ruled field, three-quarters of a page, headed *NARRATIVE OF DIVERSION,* where the true and the whole and the irrevocable went, in the broker's exact hand, to be received against all persons named therein, the affiant included. She had read the reg three times before she came. Reading it again had been a way of not yet standing up.

Toby was behind a mesh in a concrete room with a table bolted to the floor and a chair bolted on either side of it, and he had gone from young-old to just old in the eight days since the flood, the runner's leanness turned to a scrape of a man, the grin worn off him entire. He still had the flinch. When the door went he came half up out of the bolted chair before the chain on his wrist reminded him where he was, and then he sat back down and looked at the folder under her arm and knew it the way a man who has stolen a thousand skies knows a form.

"There it is," Toby said. His voice was ruined, a runner's voice with the run taken out of it. "Come down into the ditch to fetch me up, did you."

"Toby."

"They tell you I've been asking for you?" He wiped his mouth on the back of the chained hand. "Not to get me out. I know I don't get out. I been telling them the truth for eight days and every true thing I say they write down as a worse thing than the one before, so I quit. I been waiting on you because you're the only one comes down here understands the arithmetic." He looked at the folder again. "That the thirty-one-C."

"That's the thirty-one-C."

"Compelled." He said the word like it tasted of the mud. "So they've got you too. That's how they do it. They don't hire a hand to build the gallows, they find the one man already bonded to carry lumber and tell him it's his lawful duty." He almost smiled and couldn't finish it. "Sit down. It's a long field on that form. I filled out the short version myself, they wouldn't take it. Said it lacked a broker's certification." Now the smile came, terrible. "Turns out even a confession's not real till you stamp it."

Reyes stood against the concrete by the door with her hand-crank barometer in her coat pocket where she always kept it and her forensic face giving nothing, because her part of this was to witness that the statement was freely given and unrecanted and to receive it into the record, and she would do her part to the letter the way Della was about to do hers, two women in two chairs telling themselves the letter exempted them. Della sat in the bolted chair across the mesh. She set the folder on the bolted table and squared it and did not open it yet.

"I have to take your statement," she said. "For the diversion of the eleventh of last month and the second of this. The flood."

"I know what you have to take."

"And I have to certify it. As broker of record. That's what makes it valid." She heard her own voice go flat and exact, the voice she kept for the worst deliveries because it was the one instrument in the office that couldn't be accused of feeling anything, and she hated that she was using it on him, and she used it. "You understand what a certified thirty-one-C does, Toby. It's not an arrest report. It's a finding. It names the diverter as the responsible party for the imbalance and everything the imbalance caused. Once I certify it, the flood is legally your storm. Every acre-inch of it. Including the ones that killed a man."

Toby was quiet. Somewhere up the stair a pipe knocked. He looked at his chained hand on the bolted table and then he looked at her and he said, in the voice of a man laying a thing down that he had carried a long way, "I tried to stop it."

"I know."

"No." He leaned as far forward as the chain let him. "You need to hear it, because you're the one signs it and I want you signing it with the whole thing in you. I had two pulls that afternoon. Mine and theirs. I felt theirs come on across the front and I knew it was too much and too fast and going to fall wrong, and I tried to *let go* of my end, get out of the way, quit pulling so the front wouldn't get fought over — and you can't, Della. You bend a front, you can't unbend it in twenty minutes. It's like hauling on a rope somebody else is hauling on. You both let go at once or you don't, and they didn't let go, because they're not a man in a truck, they're an *order,* and an order doesn't feel the volume go wrong." His ruined voice cracked on it. "It fell in the draw because two of us were fighting over one sky and neither one could put it down soft. I've dropped rain my whole life and I never once dropped it on a man. I saw the draw filling and I couldn't do a thing but watch, same as you."

*Same as you.* She had known it for eight days — Reyes had told her, and she had believed it before Reyes told her — and hearing it in his own ruined mouth was a different kind of knowing, the kind that goes higher than the wrist, closer to the bone. The man across the mesh was less guilty than she was about to make him, in a form, in her own exact hand, and he was telling her so, and asking her to sign it anyway with the whole of it in her.

She opened the folder.

"I have to write what happened," she said. "All of it. Where you pulled from. Who told you to. And the flood."

"Write it."

"The reg makes it against you and against everyone you name." She had to say it; the reg required the affiant be told; and she said it looking at the form and not at him, the way the men here said the hard things. "If you name Meridian's off-book side — the man who owns your truck, the one who told you where to pull and where to drop — it goes against them too. It's the one thing in here that could reach past you. It could put the flood where it belongs."

Toby laughed, and it was the worst sound the room had made. "Name them. Sure. I'll give you the name. It's a phone that don't answer and a company that owns another company that owns a lease on my grandfather's rain, and by the time your Authority walks up that chain the man at the top of it is Voss, and Voss has a study that says he never told anybody to pull anything off the books, and he's right, because he never *told* me. He owns my paper. Paper doesn't say a word." He shook the chain, once, gently, a man rattling a fact. "You can name them till your pen runs dry. It reaches me. It stops at me. That's what a runner's *for,* Della. I'm the part of the sky that don't have a timestamp. That's the whole job." He sat back. "You know this. You filed thirty-ones on my pulls all spring thinking you were fighting them, and every one of them was them, and you were the one keeping the count. We been on the same string the whole time. Only I'm the end that gets cut."

Ninety hours. She could feel them going. She thought about Iris's number, four-hundredths of an inch, run four ways and surviving; she thought about Cass's line going gray in a wind that hadn't reached it yet; she thought about the chair upstairs that she could not sit in until this form was fed and certified, and about the sister whose sky would go under on schedule with both of them watching if the seal did not come. Every one of those thoughts was a reason. Every one of them was the reason a man across a mesh was going to be made, in her hand, into a flood.

She picked up her pen. Not the good pen — Junie had the good pen, at the ledger, keeping the count Della had told her not to leave a blank in. Her own pen, the broker's pen, the one that had initialed eleven hundred boxes. She wrote the date at the top of the narrative field. She wrote *Affiant: Tobias Renn.* Her hand did not shake, which was worse than if it had.

"You don't have to write it," Toby said, quiet now, watching her hand. "You know that. You could get up. There's no bonded broker left in Hale but you, and if you get up off that chair there's no valid statement, and no reinstatement, and the thing dies here. You could save me by doing nothing. It's the one time in your whole life doing nothing would come out clean." He said it without hope, laying the last card face-up because it cost nothing to lay it. "Get up, Della. Let me be the honest thing in this. God knows this family could use one."

It was Cass's words in a runner's mouth — *one honest thing* — and she did not think it was an accident, because nothing in this country was an accident, only weather, arriving on schedule. She looked at him through the mesh. She let herself count the cost of getting up, entire, to the last decimal: do nothing, let Toby be honest and let Ridley go under and let Meridian own Cass's basin by Sunday and let the four-hundredths that would have saved it stay a number on Iris's paper, unrun, and keep, at the last, one clean thing — his innocence, or hers.

"I can't," Della said.

"You can. That's the hell of it. You just won't."

"I won't." She put the flat exact voice down and let the other one come up under it, the one no one in the county had heard her use, because he had earned it and because no one else would ever hear this part. "There's a woman thirty miles east that I said a thing to over our father's grave that I can't take back, and I've spent eleven years being sober enough to know I meant it, and the only thing left I can do for her is rob her a little so a company can't rob her whole. And I can't do it from the ditch, Toby. I can only do it from the chair. And the chair costs you." She held his eyes across the mesh. "I'm not going to tell you it's worth it. It's not worth it to you. It's worth it to her. That's the whole arithmetic, and it's the same arithmetic my father did, and I'm not going to hand it to him to carry anymore."

Toby looked at her for a long moment, and something went out of his face that had been the last of the nerve, the last of the grin's ghost, and what was left was only the clear thing, the philosophically clear thing that had made him her mirror since the dry channel.

"Same rain," he said.

"Same rain."

"You file it, I run it, the rain still leaves somebody." He said the old line without any music in it now, just the fact of it, the thesis of the whole ruined country stated by the man it was about to end. "Only difference was always that you get to keep the stamp. And now they're going to take mine and hand you yours back, and you're going to go up that stair and sign a piece of sky off your own sister, legal, with a fee on it you can't refuse, and it's going to be *cleaner* than what I did, on paper, forever." He shook his head, slow. "That's the joke I could never get anybody to laugh at. There's no clean basin. There's just the one you can't see the faces in. And you're the only broker I ever met who came down into the ditch to look at the faces before she signed. For all the good it did either one of us." He put his chained hand flat on the bolted table, the way Marvin used to put his hands flat on the counter on either side of his hat. "Write it. Get your chair. Save your sister a little. I'd rather be the flood in your book than have you get up and let her drown for my sake. I'm a thief, Della. I'm not a fool."

So she wrote it.

She wrote the eleventh and the second, the upper Kettle and the north townships, the retail signature Reyes's forensics had read — one man, one truck, one field. She wrote the two pulls of the afternoon of the flood, his and the order's, fought over one front. She wrote, because he told her to and because it was true, that the affiant had attempted to abate his pull and had been unable to unbend the front in the window — and watched Reyes note that it was received and that receiving it changed nothing, the trying going in the record and dying there, a line that filed itself down. She wrote the name Toby gave her, the phone that didn't answer, the company that owned the company, knowing it reached him and stopped there. She wrote the volume, est. 0.9 to 1.0 acre-inches, into a draw that could not hold it. She did not write Marvin's name — the casualty was on a Form 40 in her own county's book, timestamp still blank, pending determination, and this was the determination, and both forms would meet in the record now with nothing between them but a filing fee.

Then she got to the bottom of the narrative field where the broker certifies. She looked at the box. **I certify the foregoing to be the true and complete statement of the affiant, received freely and without duress, this date.** Duress shall be presumed absent. §2(g). The Act golfed.

She did not have the stamp with her — the stamp was cold on the counter upstairs, and a 31-C took a certification but not a seal, the seal being reserved for transfers, this being only a confession — and so she initialed the box the way she had initialed eleven hundred boxes, the small dry motion, and the old wrongness went up her wrist and did not stop at the wrist, went higher, closer to the bone than it had ever gone, and she set the pen down.

"There," Toby said. He was watching her wrist, the way Iris watched a gauge. "Feel that?"

"Yes."

"Good." His ruined voice had something almost gentle in it, which was the last cruelty of the day. "Long as it still does that, you're not him yet. He quit feeling it. That's how a man does forty years of it. You keep feeling it right up to the last one and then you find out feeling it never stopped a single storm from leaving somebody. Feeling it's just how you know you're awake while you do it." He looked at the initialed box through the mesh, his own name above it in her exact hand, the flood made his forever. "Now you're awake and it's mine. Go get your chair, broker. Clock's running on your sister."

Reyes came off the wall. She took the folder from the table and read the certification and the initials and the narrative in her forensic silence, and then she closed it, and she looked at Della with the plain incorruptible honesty that was the only instrument left in a captured agency, and she said, "It's received. It's irrevocable. He can't unsay it and neither can you." She said it as a fact, not a comfort, because she did not offer comforts. "You're reinstated, effective on my filing this, in time for the window. You can direct the seal or affix it. I'll process it within the hour." A pause, and the smaller, tireder voice underneath, the one homesick for a coast. "I told you it wouldn't help him and it wouldn't help you. I'm sorry I was right."

Toby laughed, once, at the ceiling. "Everybody in this county's *sorry they were right.* Whole outfit of you. Sorriest bunch of correct people I ever stole from." He looked at Della a last time through the mesh as Reyes opened the door, and he said the thing she would carry up the stair and into the chair and out onto the county-line bridge at the end of everything, the thing that was the book's whole argument stated by the man it had used up:

"Same rain, Della. It always was. You just get to keep the stamp."

She went up the stair with the smell of the flood following her and eighty-nine hours running and her wrist aching for a brass thing that was not in her hand, and behind her the bolted door shut on the honest man she had made into a storm to buy the seal that would let her rob her sister down to the last surviving inch — and the record, receiving it, did not read it, only kept it, in another woman's careful hand, without comment, forever.


---

# Chapter 28 — Form 3-A: Allotment of Record (Prine → Prine)

> *A transfer between persons of the same blood shall be recorded in the ordinary course and without comment, the register taking no notice of kinship, of motive, or of what is owed.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, Reg. 3-A.16

She came up out of the basement into the office with the flood's smell still on her and the reinstatement not yet filed, and the first thing she did, before she took off her coat, was go to the shelf where the oldest ledgers stood and take down the first one.

She had a reason a court would accept. A valid seal on a 9-C that pulled across the county line needed a certified chain of title back to origin — the audit would want the parcel's whole life proved clean before Della's stamp could go on the bond. That was true, and it was the reason she gave Junie, and it was not the reason. She had eighteen hours, and she wanted, before she spent them, to see the thing entire, once, in the hand that made it.

The book was the color of the county — gray board covers gone soft at the corners, the spine worn through to the cords, dust in the gutter that no shut cover had kept out, because nothing in Hale kept the dust out; it filed itself down through everything, through covers, through years. She set it on the counter and it made the small dead sound an old book makes, and Junie looked up.

"That's before my rows," Junie said. "That's before anybody's rows I've copied."

"It's before mine too." Della opened it. The paper was heavier than the paper they used now, gone the yellow of an old tooth, and down the columns ran her father's hand at forty and at fifty and at sixty, the square upright clerk's hand she had learned to make her own by, the hand that had pressed the stamp into her palm at the end and told her not to leave a seal where a want could reach it. She turned to the ribbon marker her own hand had laid there a lifetime of Tuesdays ago. She had known the page since she was fourteen and had spent twenty-two years not looking at it long.

She looked at it now.

---

The dust year came into the house through the seams no matter what her father nailed over the windows. Della was fourteen and had learned to breathe through a wet rag her mother had shown her before her mother died, and Cass could not breathe through anything — seventeen and folded on the cot by the stove because the warmth loosened her chest a little, coughing up a color a lung should not make, the valley fever gone down into her and the dust year sitting on top of it like a hand. The district doctor had come out the once and stood in the dark front room with his hat on and said the thing doctors said when they were done pretending. *She has to be somewhere green,* he said, *or you'll bury her by spring.* And her father had said, *There's no money to move,* and the doctor had gone out into the blowing brown and left the sentence in the room, where it stayed all autumn, with the rag Della breathed through and the color Cass coughed.

There was no money to move. There was the farm and the office and, severable from either, an inch and three-quarters of Hale rain that had fallen on Prine ground since before it was a thing a man could weigh and cart off. And there was a company buying, quiet, at the counter that would one day be Della's, in the person of a man in a gray suit — Kettle Basin Land & Rain, the card said — who was patient and pressed and remembered your name. She was fourteen and she carried her father his coffee and saw the good shoes wrong for the gravel, and heard, through the frosted door with COUNTY RAIN BROKER cut into it the same as now, her father doing the arithmetic out loud in the flat exact voice she would spend her life learning to make.

He did not sell only theirs. That was the part she had not understood at fourteen and understood entire now, looking at the page. A broker could assemble a block — go to the Hollises and the Coyles and say *one parcel won't move them but a dozen will* — and take, on the whole of it, on his neighbors' ruin and his own, the broker's fee the Act allowed him, ruled off neat in the margin to the third decimal, because the Act did not know the difference between a man saving his daughter and a man taking a cut, and had a box for the fee and no box for the reason.

He sold the block to buy Cass a green county. He sold his own family's inch and three-quarters to move her thirty miles east into the wet, into Ridley, into a farm on the Ridley Fork bottomland where a girl could take a breath that wasn't half the county. And he kept Della — because someone had to be at the counter, and because, the sentence he said to her once in the doorway that she had carried forty years, *your sister's got the bad lungs, and you've got the strong ones, and it means you can stay.* As if it were a mercy. As if being the durable one, the one the dust could not kill fast enough to matter, were a thing a father gave a daughter instead of a thing he spent.

And then he entered it. That was the page — what she had come up the stair to see before she went into the window and did the same thing one generation down. He entered the transfer the way he entered every transfer, in the square upright hand, without comment, the register taking no notice of kinship or of motive or of what was owed, and Della, fourteen, standing where Junie stood now, had watched his hand make the letters and had not known she was watching her own whole life go down in ink.

*Prine allotment, block sale, offset Ridley Fork. Balanced. Filed.*

That was the sentence. Rain taken off a Prine to save a Prine, the fee ruled off in the margin, the sky sold to buy a breath — and under it, in the same steady hand, the next day's transfer, and the day after that, because a ledger does not stop for what a page costs. She had thought, at fourteen, that the terrible thing was the color Cass coughed. She understood now that the terrible thing was the calm of the hand. That a man could do that to his children and then dip the pen and rule the margin straight.

---

"Della." Junie was watching her over the current ledger. "You've been on that page a while."

She had. The office had gone the flat gold of late afternoon, the light coming level off the elevator and lying on the counter, and the barometer on the wall held at FAIR the way it had held at FAIR for six years, because the wall unit had died long before Della had, and only the sky it pretended to read had kept changing.

"I'm at the origin," Della said. She turned the book so Junie could see it, the square hand, the ruled margin, the sentence. "There's your chain of title, back to the start. Kettle Basin Land & Rain, seventy-odd years ago. Same outfit that filed the nine-C we're standing under. It changed its name four times so it wouldn't have to change what it does."

Junie read the sentence. Her lips moved on it the way they moved on everything, a girl who believed the record mattered, reading the record. "*Offset Ridley Fork,*" she said. "That's — the proceeds went east. To Ridley."

"The money bought Ridley. Bought a farm in it." Della watched her get there. "For a girl who couldn't breathe here."

Junie was quiet. She was twenty-two and unblooded and standing at the counter where the man in the square hand had stood, and Della watched her set the block sale next to the name Prine next to the sister thirty miles east she now knew about, and arrive, the way the reader of any true record arrives, at the shape of the thing.

"That's your family's," Junie said. Not a question. "He sold your family's rain to move your sister. And kept — " She stopped, because the end of the sentence was standing in front of her hanging up her coat.

"And kept me," Della said, because she would not make the girl carry the mercy of not saying it. "Yes. Now you know what the office is. It's the desk where that gets a filing number." She closed the old ledger, not hard, the way you close a thing that is finished. "Log that I pulled the origin volume for the chain of title. Mark the time."

Junie logged it, the round long gone out of her hand, and Della watched the letters go down and thought, *that's my doing too,* and did not have room to feel it, because the clock in her chest was running. She had come up the stair having half-decided the thing. The old book had decided the other half.

The 9-C would not seal itself. Reyes would file the reinstatement within the hour, in time for the window; the chair was Della's again, bought at that price downstairs. And the defensive bond Iris had built — the volume that would pull enough off Cass's inch-and-a-hundredth to lock the rest above the floor and out of Meridian's reach forever — needed a broker's seal to be law. And the Act said a broker could not be a party to her own transfer.

Kinship made her a party. §2(i). And the fee made her a party twice over — the compensation could not be waived on an interbasin, it attached, hers by statute whether she wanted it or not, so that in the eye of the Act she would be robbing her sister *and taking a cut,* the exact two things her father had done.

So there was, the law being what it was, one clean way, and it was Junie. Junie held a probationary card. Junie was not kin to Cass, not a party, not interested. If a supervising broker directed her, Junie could affix the seal to the 9-C, and it would be valid, and Della's hands would stay — the word she had built a sober life on the muscle of — Della's hands would stay clean. The transfer would rob Cass. Someone would have signed it. It would not, on paper, be Della.

It would be Junie.

She stood with the old ledger shut under her hand and understood the offer the office was making her — the same offer it had made her father, who had not taken it, who had signed it himself, his own name, never once hidden behind a clerk. She had spent her whole life thinking herself better than him because she only filed, only handed along, kept her hands accurate instead of dirty. And here at the end the office was offering her the one thing that would let her be, at last, cleaner than Doss: let the girl sign it. Keep the fifty-four years intact. Go to the bridge with her palms empty.

She thought about it the way she thought about a bottle — held the whole shape of the yes and knew exactly what it would do. It was clean and it was the worst thing she had ever wanted: to make the twenty-two-year-old put the thunk down on Cass's sky, to move the good pen from her own reach into a probationary hand and call it supervision. Doss to Della to Junie, three pairs of hands over one ledger, the sin walking down the page without comment, forever.

"Junie."

"Yeah." The girl looked up.

"When Reyes files my reinstatement, the seal comes back live under my card. Not yours. Yours stays probationary. You're not to touch the nine-C, or the bond. If anybody from the Authority — Reyes, Prewitt, the district itself — directs you to affix a seal to Application forty-one or anything cross-filed to it, you refuse, and you cite that I directed you to refuse, and you put my name on the refusal so it's mine. Do you understand me."

Junie had gone still. "You said the clean-hands way was for me to sign it. You said it two days ago at this counter. You said Iris said pick the hand."

"I picked it."

"You picked me — "

"I picked me," Della said.

The girl looked at her. Outside the level light was going off the elevator, the gold sliding off the wood toward the door, and Della watched Junie do the last arithmetic — the arithmetic that was the whole difference between the office and a person — and arrive.

"You can't," Junie said. "Section two-i. You're kin and there's the fee. If you sign your own family's transfer you've broken the Act, on the record, in the ledger everybody can pull. That's not suspended, Della, that's *revoked.* A finding against you. After the diverter's statement." She had the good pen still in her hand and she set it down like it had gotten heavy. "It reaches you. Everything reaches you. You'd be — "

"I'd be the one who signed it," Della said. "Yes."

"I could sign it and none of that touches you. I *want* to sign it. It's the right move, it's the only clean — "

"There is no clean," Della said, and heard the flatness leave her voice, and let the true one come, because the girl was twenty-two and standing where Della had stood at fourteen watching a hand make the letters. "That's the thing this office is built to make you not learn. There's no clean basin, Junie. There's the one you can't see the faces in. My father found a company to be the far end of it so he could tell himself the sky just went, and then he signed it himself when it was his own family, because at least he was honest enough not to hand the pen to a girl. I've done worse than him for longer. I've handed the pen along my whole life and called my hands accurate. I'm not handing it to you." She put her palm flat on the shut gray book, the way Marvin used to set his hands flat on either side of his hat. "You're going to inherit this chair — there's no other job in this county and you'll take it the way you take the last good chair. But not with your first cross-line seal being the one that robbed my sister. That one's mine. I've been earning it for fifty-four years and I'm not going to let you pay it off for me."

Junie's eyes had gone wet and she was too proud or too young to let it past that. "It's your sister," she said, low. "You'd end your whole life to be the one who takes rain off your own sister. Why would you — when I could — "

"Because it's mine," Della said. "That's the whole answer. It was his, and he did it, and I've spent forty years pretending the shape of it was his alone. It isn't. It's the office's, and I'm the office." She looked at the barometer, the dead needle, FAIR over a county where water had run once this summer and drowned a man. "He never once apologized, and I finally understand the not-apologizing. It's not that he wasn't sorry. It's that being sorry was one more thing you could keep in your other hand while you signed. He wouldn't take the comfort. Neither will I."

She carried the old ledger to the shelf and stood it up in the gap it had left, the origin book back among the others, the sentence shut inside it — *Prine allotment, block sale, offset Ridley Fork, balanced, filed* — forty years shut back into the dark where it had waited for her to come up a basement stair and read it and go do it again on purpose.

Then she went to the safe. She turned the dial and heard the tumblers drop, the sound her father had drilled into her when her hands were smaller than the brass, and took out the stamp — heavy as a small tool meant for a different, older kind of work, the county seal cut into its face and worn shallow at the rim from his forty years and her twenty-two. And she did not put it back. For the first time in twenty-two years she took it out and did not put it back, because it was going to the window, in her coat pocket next to the chip: the two round things she carried, the one worn smooth by every *no* and the one worn shallow by every *yes.* Tomorrow inside the window she was going to bring it down herself on a Form 9-C that pulled a piece of the sky off her sister, her own name under it, kin and fee and all of it, the crime hers, entered at last *with comment* — because Cass had said *put that the holder knew what her sister was and let her do it anyway,* and Della was going to put it, so the register would take notice this once of kinship and of motive and of what was owed.

She set the stamp on the counter where it caught the last of the gold going off the wood, and the old wrongness went up her wrist before her hand had even closed on it, higher than the wrist, closer to the bone than it had ever been, and she was glad of it, because as long as it still did that she was not him yet.

"Log that I removed the seal from the safe," she told Junie. "Time it. And Junie." She waited until the girl looked up, wet-eyed and exact-handed and twenty-two. "When you copy this day's page tonight, leave nothing blank. Not the seal-out, not the refusal I told you to make. If they pull this ledger to hang me, and they will, I want the whole shape of it in your hand, plain. Don't file it down. That's the one thing I'm asking you to do different than I taught you. Keep the round in it, just this once."

Junie picked the pen back up. She looked at the stamp on the counter, and at Della's coat where the chip was, and at the old gray book back on the shelf with its sentence shut inside — and then she bent, and wrote the seal-out into the current book, and the round came back into her letters, careful and full, the hand of a person who believed the record mattered, keeping it, this once, on purpose.


---

# Chapter 29 — Form 9-C: Interbasin Reassignment (Seal Affixed) — THE SOILED CHOICE

> *The seal, once affixed, completes the transfer. There is no provision for its removal.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, §2(a)

The window would close at four minutes past six, and at two minutes past five Reyes came through the frosted-glass door with the reinstatement in a folder and the cold coming off her coat, and said, before the bell had finished, "It's filed. You're the broker of record as of the timestamp. Effective now."

She set the folder on the counter and squared it with two fingers the way she squared everything, and did not sit, because she was not staying, because an auditor who watched a broker seal a transfer she had audited was a thing the Act had opinions about, and Reyes kept the letter even when the letter was the only wall left standing in the building.

"Fifty-eight minutes," Reyes said. "The application's queued. Nine-C-forty-one, amended — the defensive volume, the bond attached, holder-of-record consent on file." She looked at Della across the counter with the plain forensic honesty that did not soften for the occasion because it did not soften for anything. "The consent's from Cassandra Prine. The applicant's Cassandra Prine. And the broker of record is her sister. You understand that when I say it out loud it becomes a fact I've said out loud."

"I understand it."

"Two-i makes you a party. Kinship. And the fee attaches on an interbasin — it can't be waived, I checked it three times looking for the box, there's no box." A pause, the small tired voice coming up under the forensic one, the one homesick for a coast she couldn't afford. "There's a clean way. If you direct the clerk, she seals it, and the finding never reaches you."

"I know the clean way," Della said. "Junie knows it too. She's known it and hated that I wouldn't take it."

Junie was at the ledger with the good pen and had not written anything since Reyes came in. She was twenty-two and she had gone very still, the way she went still now instead of arguing, a thing Della had taught her without meaning to, the way you teach a child to hold their breath by holding yours.

Reyes looked at the girl, and at the stamp on the counter — out of the safe since yesterday, sitting on the wood next to the inked pad, the county seal cut into its brass face and worn shallow at the rim from forty years and twenty-two — and back at Della.

"You're going to sign your own family's transfer," Reyes said. "On the record. With the fee attached. That's not a suspension, Della. That's a finding. It's revocation, and after the diverter's statement it's likely a referral, and there's no chair at the end of it for you to have saved." She said it the way she read a gauge. "I could have slowed this. I want it in the room that I could have and I didn't, because the letter didn't let me, and because you'd have hated me for it more than you're going to hate me for this. The letter's all I've got. It's all either of us ever had." She squared the folder one more time though it did not need it. "Fifty-six minutes. I'll be across the street. When it's down, log it and the wall clock, and don't let her — " a tilt of the head at Junie " — touch the pad."
