She went out. The bell rang her gone into the cold blue evening, and the office held its paper-and-machine-oil quiet, and the barometer on the wall held dead on FAIR the way it had held dead on FAIR for six years, over a county where the sky was going the color of dusk and meaning it for once.

Della drew the amended application up on the terminal and read it through.

She read it the way she had read eleven hundred of them, the plus meeting the minus, conservation holding, the arithmetic clean and closed. Off Ridley Fork — off the Prine bottomland, off the inch and a hundredth that had fallen on that ground since before the county forked — a controlled volume, a survivable volume, a number Iris had run to the knife's edge and Della had checked twice against the floor: enough to knock the basin below the sale trigger's optics and lock the rest, the surviving inch, into a bond that Meridian could not buy at any price, forever. It offset to Hale on paper. It would not save Hale. She had given up the deluge to do this; the deluge was gone, elected away in her own filing, and Marvin was in the Lutheran churchyard under lilies gone gray, and the corn that would have drunk the deluge would green up gray in June and die on schedule. The form did not have a box for what she was giving up. The form only balanced.

She inked the pad.

She did not pick up the stamp yet. She stood with her hand near it and did not close her hand, the way she had stood over it in the safe-cold morning eight days ago and the way her father had told her not to — *you do not leave the seal where a want can reach it* — and she understood, the way she had understood in the basement, that the want he'd meant was the want to keep her hands out, and that she was about to reach past it.

Thirty miles east, on a schedule she could read off her own screen, the front was already bending.

---

She could not see it. But she had witnessed enough deliveries to build it in the dark behind the numbers, and the numbers were on the terminal, timestamped, a delivery window opening east while she stood over the pad — and she built it, because building it was the only witness she was allowed.

The sky over the Ridley Fork bottomland had been, all summer, a wet confident blue, the blue of a county that had kept its rain, the blue Della had driven into like a rumor of the past. Now, on schedule, a degree at a time, it was going pale at the western rim. The color that had lived over Hale for six years — the flat spent guilty blue of a place that had done something and could not take it back — was crossing the county line the way the numbers said it would, coming east over honest alfalfa and a real reservoir, arriving punctual as an eviction, and no one under the sky knew yet that the sky was the thing being served.

Cass would be at the line.

She hung washing on a line because she could, white and stiff and clean, and had done it for forty years without learning it was a thing a sky could be sold out from under. She would be bringing it in now — the pressure gone strange, the dog off its feed and looking west, the tank down four inches, the barometer she trusted falling like a thing lowered on a rope — taking the wash off before the wrong rain came, and it would come off her hands not white but already going the color of the county to the west, dust in it, the gray arriving in the weave before the water arrived on the field, because the two lines had become one line, and Cass would fold it gray against her chest and not yet know that her broker sister thirty miles west was standing over an inked pad with the family stamp in reach, doing to Ridley's sky what a man in a gray suit had done to Hale's forty years ago.

She picked up the stamp.

The old wrongness went up her wrist before her hand had closed on it — higher than the wrist, closer to the bone than it had ever gone, the animal part of her that had thought weather was God's and not a lot number, that had flinched at eleven hundred stamps and flinched now — and she was glad of it, the way Toby had told her to be glad of it through the mesh: *long as it still does that, you're not him yet.* She held the brass over the form and did not bring it down.

"Della." Junie's voice, low. "Fifty-one minutes. If you're going to — "

"I know the clock."

The girl did not ask it. That was the thing Della marked, standing over the pad — that Junie, who had asked *are you going to make me sign it* at this counter the first week and every hard week since, the innocent question that indicted, did not ask anything now. She had learned where the questions went. She stood with the good pen in her hand that was not steady and let the silence do the asking, and the silence was worse than the girl's voice had ever been, because a question you can still answer; a silence just files itself down and waits. Della saw her want in it, plain, the twenty-two-year-old want to step in and take the sin off her broker with her own clean probationary card, to prove that competence was mercy, that a good clerk could carry a bad day for someone she loved — the exact want the office was built to grow in a person and then use.

"Keep your hand off the pad," Della said, not unkindly. "That's an order from your supervising broker and I want it in the log that I gave it. Write the time I said it."

Junie wrote the time. The round came into her letters, careful and full, the hand of a person who believed the record mattered, keeping it on purpose, this once, the way she'd been told.

Della looked at the barometer.

It held dead on FAIR, the wall unit that had died long before she had, the most honest liar in the office — and then, as she watched, over a county thirty miles wide and a sky going pale on a filed schedule, the needle moved.

It did not move much. It was a dead instrument and it had no business moving and it moved anyway, a hair, a tremor off FAIR toward the low side, the way a thing twitches that has been still so long you'd stopped believing it could — and she understood that it was not her office's weather it was reading. It was the delivery. It was the front coming down on schedule somewhere it had been sent, the pressure falling off a cliff over green ground going gray, and the dead needle in Hale twitching in sympathy with a barometer thirty miles east that Cass trusted, both of them lowered on the same rope, and the horror was the motion and not the relief, had always been the motion, and Della watched the dead thing find a pulse at the exact wrong moment and thought: *that's mine now too. I made the needle move.*

She brought the stamp down.

---

The *thunk* of it was the most honest sound the office made, and it went up her wrist the way it always had, the small satisfying jar of competence with a weight to it — and under the satisfaction, this time, no floor, the wrongness going all the way to the bone and not stopping, because there was no far end of this one, no company in a gray suit to be the somewhere the sky went, no Toby in the ditch to run it, no clerk's clean card to hide behind. Her hand. Her seal. The brass her father had pressed into her palm at his deathbed, worn shallow by his forty years of yes and her twenty-two, coming down on a piece of the sky over her sister, and the county seal biting the paper, and the transfer, in that instant, complete. §2(a). The seal, once affixed, completes the transfer. There is no provision for its removal.

She did not put the stamp back in the safe.

She lifted it off the form and the seal stood on the paper, sharp and round and legal, and she looked at it, and then she did the thing the Act had a box for and no one ever used, the thing Cass had made her promise at the line with the gray wash coming off it — *put that the holder knew what her sister was and let her do it anyway* — and she took the good pen out of Junie's hand and, in the narrative field where a broker almost never wrote because the register took no notice of kinship or of motive or of what was owed, she wrote it.

She wrote that the broker of record was sister to the holder of record and the applicant. She wrote that the fee attached and could not be waived and was therefore taken. She wrote that the broker affixed the seal with knowledge of the transfer's effect on the origin basin and on the persons within it, and did so freely, and not under duress, duress being presumed absent, §2(g), the Act golfing to the last. She wrote the volume and the origin and the destination and the timestamp, and then she wrote the sentence that was not on any form, the comment the register did not want, in the square upright hand she had made her own by copying a dead man's:

*Rain taken off a Prine to save a Prine. The holder knew what her sister was. She let her do it anyway.*

And under it, because Cass had not asked for this part and Della owed it anyway, one line more, the only thing in the whole record that was hers alone and not the office's:

*So did I.*

Then she initialed it, and dated it, and set the pen down, and the office was very quiet, and outside the frosted glass the Hale sky was going the ordinary dark of an ordinary evening while thirty miles east a sky that had never apologized before began, on schedule, to be spent.

"Log it," Della said. Her voice came out flat and exact, the way it came out under everything, competence the last armor and the last tell. "The seal and the wall clock. Five-eleven and — " she looked " — thirteen. And the comment. Copy the comment into the current book tonight, every word of it, in your hand, and don't file it down. If they pull this ledger to hang me, and they will, I want the round in it."

Junie took the pen back. She was crying and too proud to let it past her eyes, and she looked at the seal on the form, and at the stamp in Della's hand that had not gone to the safe, and at the comment in the narrative field that broke forty years of a register taking no notice, and she wrote it into the current book, careful and full and round, the seal-down and the clock and the comment, the whole shape of it plain, keeping the record the way she had been taught to keep it and this once told not to.

Della carried the sealed form to the terminal and filed it into the queue, and the machine took it without comment, the way the machine took everything, and returned a confirmation number and a timestamp, and it was done. Nine-C-forty-one, amended, sealed, filed, forty-one minutes before the window closed. Ridley bled a survivable inch and locked the rest above the floor. Meridian's endgame died in the district registry. Voss would read it in the morning and be sorry, and move to the next basin, intact, because that was what the machine's face did. Hale kept dying. Marvin stayed dead. Cass's rain was safe, minus the piece Della had just taken with her own hand, forever, no un-signing, no reversion, the thesis rendered as physics one last time.

She put the stamp in her right coat pocket, next to the chip.

The two round things she carried now — the bronze one worn smooth by every *no* of eleven years, and the brass one worn shallow by forty years of yes and twenty-two more and, tonight, the last one — and she felt them knock together through the fabric when she reached for her coat, a small dull sound, and she left the stamp there instead of the safe, because she was not the broker anymore, not after the comment, not after the fee, and the seal was not going where a want could reach it because the want was answered, spent, filed. There was nothing left in her hand to reach for.

She had said *no* to the drink ten thousand nights and it had saved her life. She had said *no* to Meridian and the market had routed around her. She had said *no* to handing Junie the pen, and that *no* had been the first one in her life that put her hands *in* instead of keeping them out. And now she had brought the brass down on her own family's sky, her own name under it, kin and fee and comment and all, and it had saved her sister's basin and damned her broker's name and greened nothing and raised no one, and it was filed — a confirmation number and a timestamp, five-eleven and thirteen, the machine's flat receipt for the one thing she had done on purpose in fifty-four years, entered and closed and past reversion before the ink of the comment had dried.

She turned off the terminal.

Across the street, in the lit window of the Trestle, Reyes stood with a cup she wasn't drinking and watched the broker's office go dark, and did not come over, because it was done, and an auditor did not offer comforts, and the two women who had chosen the rulebook over the person, in different chairs, looked at each other across the dark blue street through two panes of dusty glass and did not wave.

Della locked the frosted-glass door that read COUNTY RAIN BROKER · HALE, for what she was fairly sure was the last time she'd do it as the broker, and stood a moment on the sidewalk with the stamp and the chip knocking in her pocket and the county's dark coming down.

Then, muffled through the glass, the phone on the counter began to buzz itself across the wood, lit and blind in the dark office — and even through the glass and even before she'd read the screen she knew whose number it was, the way you know a shape on the horizon before it has a name, because there was only one person left who would call her the night the sky changed over Ridley. It was the sister she had just robbed. Della stood on the dark walk with the key still in her hand and understood that whatever Cass had to say, the seal was down, there was no provision for its removal, and she was going to have to unlock the door she had just locked and go back into the office she no longer ran and pick up the phone and hold still and let her sister tell her, at last, exactly what she was.


---

# Chapter 30 — Form 7-R: Broker's Bond (Surrendered)

> *A surrendered license does not expunge the record; the record is the property of the Act and shall be maintained by the office in perpetuity.* — Atmospheric Allotment Act, Reg. 7-R.9

She unlocked the door she had just locked and went back in and picked up the phone, and Cass's voice came out of it flat as a filed thing, no hello, the way the dry made you talk.

"Your rain came," Cass said. "About twenty of six. Came down a straight line across the bottom, wet the far fence and walked to the near one, and I stood on the porch and watched it come like it had a schedule, because it had a schedule." A silence with the county in it. "That's yours. I can smell it's yours. It smells like the wrong thing."

Della stood in the dark office with the stamp still knocking the chip in her pocket and did not say *I'm sorry,* because sorry was a thing you offered a person you could make whole, and she had just spent the last box on the form making it a fact that she could not.

"It's bonded," Della said. "The rest of your inch is locked above the floor. Meridian can't touch it. Not at critical, not at any price, not ever. That's what the taken piece bought."

"I know what it bought. Iris told me the shape of it." The cough came up in Cass, deep, the receipt her body had carried forty years for the rain that was sold to save her, and she rode it down. "You want me to say it was worth it."

"No."

"Good. Because I'm not going to." And then, in the plain unforgiving grammar she had built out of a guilt she couldn't live inside: "You did to my sky what he did to this county. With your own hand. I felt the pressure drop and I knew it was a Prine doing it before I knew it was you." A breath. "Don't come out here to be forgiven. There's no rain in that either."

"I wasn't going to come," Della said, which was true, and was the cruelest true thing she had, and Cass took it the way a sister takes the one blow that lands, without a sound, and hung up, and the line went to the flat tone of a thing spent.

Della set the phone down. Outside the frosted glass the ordinary dark had come all the way down over Hale, and thirty miles east a sky that had never apologized before was learning the color, and the barometer on the wall held dead on FAIR over both of them, reading nothing, the most honest liar in the office to the last.

---

It was six weeks before the Authority got the surrender processed, because the Act was slow to let go of a person even when it was done with her, and in those six weeks the corn that would have drunk the deluge came up in June and greened gray and died on the schedule the market had elected for it, and Marvin's ground went to the bank, and the bank listed it, and no one bid.

Della came in mornings still, because the reinstatement had made her broker of record until the revocation hearing, and the office did not stop needing an arm just because the arm was condemned. She worked the corrective queue Reyes had built her — the cleanup of her own harm, item by item, a closed loop she sealed with her father's brass and did not flinch from anymore, because the flinch had gone somewhere the night she brought the stamp down on Cass's sky and had not come back, and she understood that too was a thing she had spent.

The diverter's statement did what a diverter's statement did. Toby was bound over. He would not go up for Marvin — Reyes could not make the volumes prove a man had steered the killing pull that two pulls had let fall unsteered — but he would go up for the running, for a truck and a channel and a hundred filed 31s with his signature under them, and it was enough to end him, and Della had signed the paper that started it. He got word to her once, through the lockup, on a form he had no business having. *Same rain, Della. You just kept the stamp.* She copied it into the current ledger under the day's transfers, in her own square hand, because he was right and the record was the only place left she could tell the truth to, and she did not file it down.

Voss she saw one more time. He came through on a Thursday and stood at the counter where he'd stood the day he offered Hale its life, and he was sorry in the exact even weight he was sorry about everything, and did not gloat, because gloating would have meant he'd lost something to be glad of winning, and he had lost nothing.

"You cost us the basin," he said. "The bond holds. Legal work, Della. I've had three lawyers try to crack it and it doesn't crack." He set his card on the counter, the wet confident blue of it, the blue of a company that had never had to live under a spent one. "There's a county two ranges south that's a season off critical and hasn't got a broker with a sister in the offset." A pause that might have been a conscience-shaped thing moving in the place a conscience would go, and might have been nothing, and the book of him did not open to show which. "I hope you land somewhere green."

"I'm from here," Della said. "There isn't a green to land in. You made sure of it before I could vote."

He took that the way he took everything, as information, and went out into the apologetic blue toward the county two ranges south, intact, sorry, efficient, the machine's face going on to the next name it would remember. She left his card where he'd set it and it grayed over by afternoon like everything the county touched.

---

The revocation hearing was a formality and she waived it. There was no defense to make — she had signed her own family's transfer with the fee attached, on the record, in her own hand, and she had written it into the narrative field so no one would ever have to prove she'd known. She had built her own case against herself in the register, careful and full, the way a competent woman does a thing she means. Prewitt read the finding into the phone from the district seat and it took four minutes. Dereliction sustained. Bond revoked. License surrendered in lieu, effective on receipt of the seal.

That was the part they wanted. The seal.

She brought it in on the last morning in her right coat pocket next to the chip, and the two round things knocked together through the fabric when she walked, the bronze one worn smooth by twelve years of *no* now — the anniversary had come and gone in the six weeks and she had not marked it, had only noticed one night that the number under her thumb had turned over, that the muscle had held one more year while everything else in her life came down — and the brass one worn shallow by forty years of yes and her own twenty-two and, that once, the last.

She set the stamp on the counter next to the inked pad, the county seal cut into its brass face, worn shallow at the rim, and she did not put it in the safe, and Junie stood at the ledger with the good pen and looked at it and did not reach for it.

"Reg. 7-R-nine," Della said. "The surrendered seal reverts to the office. Not the licensee. It was never mine to keep. It was his, and then it was the office's and I just held it, and now it's the office's again and the office is yours." She slid it across the wood the way her father had pressed it into her hand at his deathbed, except she did not press it, she set it down and let go, because the whole thing she had learned this year was the difference between a hand that stays clean by not-taking and a hand that takes on purpose and pays. "You passed the exam. Prewitt bonds you Monday. You'll be the broker of record for Hale County by the end of the month, and there won't be another one, because there's no other chair in a county this dry, and you knew that when you took the clerk job, and you're taking this one with your eyes open, which is worse than not knowing and truer."

Junie looked at the brass on the counter between them.

"You could tell me not to," she said.

"I could." Della had thought about it in the six weeks, in the hot dark that came into the house at night and sat on her chest — telling the girl to run, to take the last good chair and set it on fire. But the office did not need Junie to run it; it would designate someone from the district, and the machine would go on with a stranger's hand, and Hale would have a broker who had never turned Marvin's hat over in her mind, never waived a fee and logged it waived, never told a man the true price when the market only wanted him told the low one. "I could tell you not to. And they'd bond someone who never knew him. The Act doesn't stop for one clerk's conscience. It didn't stop for mine. All refusing does is decide whose hand." She looked at the girl she had taught to hold her breath, taught to file a conscience down, taught, in the end, one true thing by refusing to hand her the pen. "So take it with your eyes open and keep the round in your letters and don't ever let them tell you where to leave a line blank. That's everything I've got to hand you and it isn't enough and it's yours."

Junie picked up the stamp.

The weight of it went into her young hand, the hand that was the fourth pair the book had shown over this ledger, Doss's and Della's and now hers, and she held it the way you hold a thing you have decided to be responsible for, and she did not say anything, because they had said it all and set it down, and then she inked the pad and pulled the day's first filing up on the terminal — a small legal one, a man moving his own rain off his own fallow onto his own corn, the plus meeting the minus, conservation holding, the arithmetic clean and closed, a rare mercy, a transfer where the man robbing you was you — and she brought the stamp down.

The *thunk* of it was the most honest sound the office made, and Della heard it go into the girl's wrist the way it had gone into hers eleven hundred times, the small satisfying jar of competence with a weight to it, and she watched Junie's face to see if the flinch was in it, the animal part that thought weather was God's and not a lot number, and she could not tell, because a flinch is a private country, and that was the whole horror and the whole mercy of handing it on: you could not see from the outside whether the next clerk was still awake. Junie logged it and marked the timestamp in the round careful hand of a person who believed the record mattered, keeping it on purpose, and the office had a broker again, and the sky was still for sale.

Della took her coat off the hook. The chip was in the right pocket and the stamp was not, for the first time in twenty-two years, and the pocket was lighter and did not feel emptier, because the want that had reached for the seal was answered and spent and filed, and there was nothing left in her hand to reach for.

"Della," Junie said, when she was at the door. Not a question. Just the name, the way you say a name to keep a person one second longer than the leaving allows.

"Keep the barometer," Della said. "It's dead. It'll always read FAIR. But tap it anyway, the way you check a dead thing for a pulse — and the day the needle moves, you'll know a delivery's coming down somewhere it was sent, and you'll know to hate the motion and not the rain. That's the last thing. Tap the dead one." She opened the frosted-glass door that read COUNTY RAIN BROKER · HALE and it would say that still, the sign, only a different woman under it now. "Lock up at six."

The bell rang her out.

---

She drove home the long way, past the Kettle, the way she always had and did not examine.

But at the boat ramp she did not go home. She turned east instead, onto the county road, and drove the thirty miles she had driven only a handful of times in eleven years, toward the seam where the dust ended and a rumor of the past began, and she came to the bridge at the county line at dusk with the light going down flat and gold across two skies.

She stopped on the bridge and got out. Below her the Kettle ran — on the Ridley side it ran, a real current the color of tea over gravel, moving, alive, because of what she had done; and on the Hale side the channel was gray pan and cracked mud and a scatter of bleached driftwood, dead, because of what she had not done, the deluge given up, the valley left to die on schedule so the sister's basin could be locked above the floor. The same water. The same channel. One of them robbed twice now, forty years apart, by the same family's hand.

She stood at the rail with the stamp gone out of her pocket and only the chip in it, twelve years of *no* worn smooth, and she looked across the water at the Ridley bank where the light still lay green.

The farm was there, the bottomland, the line strung between two posts. There was washing on it. Even from the bridge she could see it, and even from the bridge she could see that it had not dried white. It hung the color of the county to the west, gray in the weave, dust the wrong rain had brought down into it and the wrong sun had baked in, the two lines that had been two skies apart become one line now, one color, the innocence gone out of it the way the innocence goes out of a thing you have learned, at last, was always for sale. It was on the line still. It was not white. But it was saved, the ground under it locked, the sister under the sky kept — kept the way Doss had kept Cass, dirtily, at a price paid by someone else's rain, entered without comment except the comment Della had written and would not file down.

Cass was on the porch.

Della saw her across the failing river, across thirty miles compressed by the road and the dusk into thirty yards of dying current — a broad brown shape in the green light, the same Prine hands hanging at her sides, and Cass saw her, saw the truck on the bridge and the woman at the rail, and did not come down to the water, and did not go inside.

She did not wave. She did not turn away. She stood on her porch under her graying wash on her saved and dry-going ground and looked back at her sister across the water her sister had robbed to keep it running, and she did not spit, and she did not raise a hand, and she did not soften, and that was all of it, that was the whole mercy the future had to give: the sister took the dry sky she'd been dealt and looked at the one who dealt it and did not curse her out loud across the running water.

Della stood in it as long as it lasted, which was not long, because the light was going. Then Cass turned and took her gray washing off the line, unfinished, the way you bring it in before the wrong rain comes, and went inside, and the porch light did not come on, because it was not that dark yet, and the current ran, and the dead channel on the Hale side held its dust.

Della got back in the truck.

She did not feel absolved and she did not feel damned; she felt like a woman who had signed a true thing and would pay for it accurately for the rest of her life, which was neither of those and was the thing the book of the county had been walking her toward all along. She put her hand in her right coat pocket and there was only the chip there, one round thing now, worn smooth by twelve years of the one refusal she had kept, and she turned it once with her thumb the way she'd turned it ten thousand nights, and she noticed, sitting on the bridge over the two skies with the seal gone out of her hand forever, that the barometer in her chest — the one that had been dropping all year ahead of a weather she could feel coming in her fillings and her wrists, the private needle that had never once held still since Voss's card came under the door — had gone quiet.

Not to peace. There was no peace in it and there was not going to be. Just to the flat held stillness of a needle that has finally read the true pressure, and has nothing left to fall toward, because the front has already come down, on schedule, exactly where it was sent, and belongs now to someone she can see.

She started the truck and drove back across the county line into the dust, toward the dead lilac and the slack line and the hot dark waiting in her mother's house, and behind her, in an office thirty miles west that she no longer ran, the ledger lay open under another woman's careful hand, the record continuing, the machine intact, the next clerk already bonded, the sky still for sale — and the last line entered in it that day, in a round full hand keeping the record on purpose, read only what the register would ever allow: a volume, an origin, a timestamp, and no comment, the county's arithmetic clean and closed and balancing, as it always had, to the third decimal.
